Tiger Woods, a 14-time major champion who has had two surgeries in the past 15 months, is nine strokes behind leader JB Holmes. The 40-year-old Woods had four birdies in his first eight holes but bogeyed the ninth after a poor drive and chip. He also bogeyed the 11th, and double bogeys on the 16th and 18th left him one stroke over par. Despite the struggles, Woods expressed optimism, saying he felt good and was looking forward to the tournament. He attributed his mistakes to poor drives, which led to shots in bushes and water. His first double bogey came on the 16th after pulling his approach shot, and his second came on the 18th when his drive landed in a lake. Earlier in the round, Woods had briefly tied for the lead at four under after a string of three birdies. Holmes, another American, leads the 18-man event with an eight-under-par 64. Hideki Matsuyama of Japan is a stroke behind, and Dustin Johnson is at six under. Henrik Stenson, Matt Kuchar, and Louis Oosthuizen are tied for fourth at five under. Justin Rose, the Olympic champion, is last at two over par.
Three Americans were flown out of Sanaa, Yemen, on Wednesday with the help of Oman, at the request of the US government. A Yemeni security official said the Americans were detained by Houthi security forces on suspicion of spying. The US State Department had previously announced the death of an American contractor for the United Nations who was detained at Sanaa's airport last month. Earlier this year, two Americans and a British citizen were released from Houthi captivity and flown to safety in Oman. The Houthis, who seized control of Sanaa this year, are engaged in a conflict with a Saudi Arabia-led coalition backed by the US. Oman, not a member of the coalition, has been involved in peace negotiations. The fighting has resulted in at least 5,700 deaths, half of them civilians, since March, according to the UN.
Before officially starting his duty, Prince Harry laid a wreath at the Tomb of the Unknown Australian Soldier in Canberra. He also delivered a letter from the Queen, expressing her belief that her grandson would greatly benefit from time spent with Australian troops. Prince Harry, known as Captain Wales in his military role, will leave the British army in June after serving for ten years. During his placement in Australia, he will patrol with Aboriginal soldiers and train with the country's special forces. After laying his wreath and placing a poppy during a tour of the Australian War Memorial, Prince Harry officially reported for duty to Air Chief Marshal Mark Binskin, Chief of the Defence Force. He presented the Queen's letter, which stated her delight in the long-standing association between the Australian and British armies, further strengthened by her grandson's military secondment. The Queen emphasized the shared skills, resources, and resolve of their armed forces in upholding and defending their common values. She highlighted the significance of commemorating the sacrifices made at Gallipoli in 2015, a century after the event, as an opportunity to reflect on the enduring strength and persistence of these values and military ties. The Queen expressed confidence that Captain Wales would greatly benefit from time spent with Australian soldiers and thanked them for welcoming him. The Australian military has described the placement as "challenging and hectic," while Prince Harry has expressed his immense anticipation. The placement will be briefly interrupted by a trip to Turkey later this month for commemorations marking the allied campaign at Gallipoli in 1915, in which thousands of soldiers from Australia and New Zealand perished. It is believed that Prince Harry will engage in voluntary work for charities later in the year as he contemplates his future options.
The 23-year-old Celtic midfielder expressed his hope that Dundee United, currently 13 points behind in the Premiership, can avoid relegation. United chairman Stephen Thompson has challenged his team to maintain their top-flight status and "redeem" their professional reputations. Armstrong, who was one of three players United sold to Celtic in 2015, along with Nadir Ciftci and Gary Mackay-Steven, believes their future in the league is uncertain. United's performance has declined since their departures. Following a 3-0 loss to Motherwell on Tuesday, Thompson apologized to the club's supporters for their "abysmal" performance and league position. Armstrong expressed his disappointment at the result but urged optimism, hoping they can finish the season strongly and return to the Premiership quickly if relegated. St Johnstone manager Tommy Wright believes Dundee United should not be written off until mathematically eliminated from staying up. Wright acknowledged their difficult position but emphasized that anything is possible until it's mathematically impossible. He also commented on the changing landscape of Scottish football, citing the recent departures of Rangers, Hearts, and Hibernian from the top flight. While Rangers and Hibs are aiming for promotion next season, Wright admitted it would be disappointing to lose a Tayside rival from the league, highlighting the importance of local derbies. Dundee striker Greg Stewart, while personally enjoying the derby matches, acknowledged that Dundee fans might not share his sentiment, hoping for United's relegation for bragging rights. He emphasized the electrifying atmosphere of these games, motivating players to perform at their best. Stewart looks forward to their final derby match of the season, hoping for a victory.
Koukash's club was penalized with a six-point deduction and a £5,000 fine by the RFL for salary cap violations in 2014 and 2015. Prior to this ruling, Koukash threatened to engage in a "full-scale war" with the RFL if they were found guilty. He stated that he was in discussions with other club chairmen to organize a meeting aimed at initiating a vote of no confidence in the RFL's leadership. Koukash asserted that the RFL was not effectively serving the sport and that despite the clubs' substantial financial investments, rugby league had regressed commercially during his four years in the sport. To call an emergency meeting and propose a no-confidence motion, Koukash, a millionaire racehorse owner, would require the support of six out of the twelve Super League clubs. In response, the RFL issued a statement emphasizing their commitment to working for the clubs and all stakeholders in rugby league. They highlighted their established democratic processes, allowing clubs to voice their concerns, and their willingness to listen to those concerns. The RFL also pointed to a recent independent report indicating that the sport's overall financial health was stronger than it was two years prior. Koukash is scheduled to hold a press conference on Wednesday, May 18th, where he is anticipated to address the recent salary cap charges further.
While I've only spoken to those directly affected, such as the leadership of a major appliance manufacturer and stock market investors, my understanding of the economic changes here will likely evolve as I meet others. However, there is relief that the US Federal Reserve has delayed raising interest rates, which would have made borrowing more expensive and less accessible globally due to the massive amount of dollar-denominated debt. Although Janet Yellen and the Federal Open Market Committee did not postpone the rate increase solely due to concerns for Chinese stock market speculators and businesses, the Fed chair stated that they were worried about fragile global market and economic conditions, particularly China's slowdown, which is significantly impacting growth in emerging economies, especially commodity producers and manufacturers reliant on Chinese demand. Essentially, a decline in share prices resulting from a weak global economic outlook, coupled with a rise in the dollar (up 15% over the past year) as investors shift capital from struggling emerging markets to a stronger US, has already tightened monetary conditions in America. In other words, the reduction in China's growth rate, from 10% to 7% and possibly down to 3% or 4% in the medium term, has made American investors feel less wealthy and hindered American exporters' sales. Ms. Yellen argues that this indirect and modest impact of China's slowdown on US prosperity is equivalent to the effect of the small interest rate increase they would have otherwise implemented. If inflation is a concern in the US, as some argue, China's slowdown has lessened that threat by tempering America's recovery. This logic seems sound, except for two things. Investors might worry that if even the Fed is concerned about China, the growth slowdown there could be more severe than anticipated, potentially causing further market instability. Additionally, a significant bubble in global debt markets, fueled by historically low interest rates since 2008, will remain vulnerable. The longer rates remain low, the greater the risk that the bubble will burst abruptly and painfully rather than gradually deflating.

Since his time at Millwall, Bircham has coached in the USA for Chicago Fire and Arizona United. From 2009 to 2014, the 38-year-old served on the coaching staff at QPR, starting as a youth team coach and progressing to a development squad coach. Having also played for QPR for five years, making 167 appearances, Bircham called his return to the club "a dream come true." Bircham, a former Canada international, stated on the club website that QPR was the only club he would have returned to England for. He emphasized the allure of working with Holloway again at a club they both understand well. Meanwhile, David Oldfield and Dirk Heesen, members of the backroom staff under Holloway's predecessor Jimmy Floyd Hasselbaink, departed the west London club on Monday.

Paatelainen expressed his gratitude to the players, staff, and supporters, acknowledging the challenging season. He stated that he enjoyed his time at the club. Paatelainen, whose contract extended to the summer of 2018, replaced Jackie McNamara in mid-October when United was last in the table. Despite his efforts, the team only achieved five wins in 25 Premiership matches before their relegation was confirmed following a 2-1 loss to Dundee. Thompson apologized to fans, promising significant changes to ensure a swift return to the top flight. United's next match is against Inverness Caledonian Thistle on Friday. Raith Rovers faces Hibernian in the first leg of their quarter-final on Wednesday. Daniel Morgan was found dead in a London pub car park in 1987 after being attacked with an axe. Despite five police investigations, no one has been convicted of his murder. His brother Alastair Morgan has teamed up with investigative journalist Peter Jukes to tell the story of his brother's death in a weekly podcast. Alastair Morgan stated that the full story has never been presented in a clear and logical way. He wants the public to have the opportunity to hear what happened, calling it a "really outrageous case." When Daniel Morgan's body was discovered in March 1987, he had been drinking at the Golden Lion pub in Sydenham shortly before his death. Police investigations have consistently failed to secure a conviction, and the case has been marked by allegations of corruption and cover-ups. An independent panel has been established to examine the circumstances surrounding Mr. Morgan's death and the police's persistent failure to bring a perpetrator to justice. Peter Jukes, the investigative journalist hosting the podcast, expressed astonishment at the details of the case after his research. He hopes that witnesses will come forward, prompting memories and leading to new connections. As part of the podcast, Alastair Morgan revisited the scene of his brother's murder with the production team. Jukes believes that podcasts are an effective medium for telling complex and in-depth stories, allowing for a single voice to guide the listener. The producers aim to replicate the success of the Serial podcast, which investigated a single true story over a series of episodes. The Daniel Morgan Murder podcast will be released to registered subscribers before Christmas and will become more widely available in the new year.
Elmbridge Museum has launched a "museum without walls" initiative to offer its services in a more accessible, relevant, and cost-effective manner. The museum's gallery in Weybridge closed on Saturday, and its new website went live on Sunday. While Surrey County Council owns the museum building and will determine its future, Elmbridge Council highlights the website's interactive online experience, representing the entire museum and its diverse offerings. The website features sections like "object in focus" for detailed information on selected items and "what we're up to" showcasing projects by the museum team, now based at the Civic Centre in Esher. Although physical objects will still be displayed at temporary exhibitions throughout the borough and a schools program will run in September, the museum's collection is being moved to a dedicated storage facility where artifacts can be viewed by appointment. Currently, two exhibitions are on display at Thames Ditton Library and Walton Library.
Scarborough Hospital, facing financial and care quality issues, has proposed a partnership with York Hospital. This move is driven by the government's mandate for all hospitals to become foundation trusts by 2013. Scarborough, burdened by £4 million in debt and recent criticism from regulators regarding care standards, believes it cannot achieve foundation trust status independently. York Hospital, which gained foundation status in 2007, is considered Scarborough's preferred partner. A spokesperson for Scarborough and North East Yorkshire NHS Trust stated that achieving foundation trust status requires meeting specific clinical and financial benchmarks, which Scarborough is unlikely to meet by the 2013 deadline. The trust's board, acknowledging the financial and policy challenges, explored options to ensure sustainable services and decided to approach York Hospital for a potential partnership. Patrick Crowley, chief executive of York Teaching Hospital NHS Foundation Trust, confirmed that both trusts have been collaborating with NHS Yorkshire and the Humber to explore options for Scarborough's foundation trust status. While York Hospital has not yet formally responded to Scarborough's proposal, Mr. Crowley expressed optimism about continuing existing alliances to deliver healthcare services in the region.
A letter criticizes the Dubs resettlement scheme for lacking planning and information sharing. Last year, only about 200 children entered the UK under the scheme, and none have arrived in 2017, according to the Home Office. The UK government pledged to uphold its commitments to refugees. The "Dubs amendment," named after refugee campaigner Lord Dubs, requires the UK government to assist a specific number of unaccompanied child refugees who arrived in Europe without family connections. The government agreed to take up to 480 children under the scheme, but it will close, with ministers arguing that an open-ended commitment would encourage human trafficking. The letter, written by Scottish Secretary for Equalities Angela Constance and Welsh counterpart Carl Sargeant, criticizes the scheme's execution. It states that they have faced challenges due to a lack of information from those running the operation. They note that only a couple of hundred children have been transferred out of the 480 placements identified. The letter acknowledges the difficulties caused by the emergency clearance of the Calais refugee camps and a legal challenge against the scheme. However, it asserts that the lack of planning and information sharing from coordinators has hindered their ability to plan effectively. Immigration Minister Brandon Lewis stated that the UK government is committed to fulfilling its Dubs scheme obligations "as soon as possible" and that other mechanisms exist to assist vulnerable children. He emphasized that this is one aspect of a broader response, highlighting their commitment to resettling 23,000 people directly from conflict zones and granting protection to over 8,000 children last year. Lewis expressed gratitude for Scotland and Wales' support and stated their intention to extend the National Transfer Scheme to the rest of the UK, enabling local authorities to fully participate. He believes the most vulnerable children are in the Syrian conflict zone and that direct resettlement from the region is the best way to help them, as it prevents traffickers and smugglers from exploiting vulnerable people and children.
The Scottish Environment Protection Agency (Sepa) stated that Dawnfresh, one of the UK's largest trout producers, had excessively used the organophosphorus pesticide azamethiphos at a site in Loch Etive. Azamethiphos is used to control parasites like sea lice. Dawnfresh acknowledged a "very small number of errors" and is collaborating with Sepa to minimize the site's environmental impact. Friends of Loch Etive, a conservation charity, alerted Sepa to the rule violation. Dawnfresh produced approximately 3,000 tonnes of rainbow trout at Loch Etive last year. Sepa confirmed that consecutive bath treatments with azamethiphos were conducted at site Etive 6 less than 24 hours apart, violating the site's Controlled Activities Regulations (CAR) licence. Further investigations are underway. Fish farms use various methods, including chemicals, to control sea lice, which can harm fish health and growth. Dawnfresh Farming director Stewart Hawthorn stated that the company achieved a "historically low number of sea lice" in Loch Etive last year due to a more proactive approach. He admitted six instances where the company did not meet its usual standards during treatment implementation at one site. Hawthorn emphasized that Sepa confirmed the site would have received an "excellent" rating for environmental compliance without these errors. He assured continued collaboration with Sepa and the company's team to minimize environmental impact. Hawthorn highlighted Dawnfresh's overall commitment to environmental compliance, noting that two other sites on Loch Etive received "excellent" and "good" ratings. He added that two additional sites will not be operated due to concerns about meeting the highest environmental protection standards. Looking ahead, Dawnfresh remains dedicated to effectively controlling sea lice and is implementing new treatment methods to reduce chemical reliance.

The staff at Claythorpe Watermill, near Alford, Lincolnshire, stated that Stan the cockerel had a reputation for chasing toddlers and pecking ankles. As the busy summer season approached, they believed it would be best if Stan found a new home. Owner Rebecca Agate expressed her desire for visitors to have a pleasant and trouble-free experience at the watermill. She explained that with many young children visiting the park, Stan's chasing and pecking behavior could be intimidating. To prevent negative memories, she hoped someone would provide Stan with a suitable home. Agate described Stan, believed to be at least partly Dutch bantam, as having a lot of personality and enjoying human company, but also acknowledged his grumpy side. Numerous other instances of birds attacking people, animals, or objects have been reported. Last year in Cambridgeshire, a pheasant was described as a "terrorist" for attacking vehicles and chasing cats and dogs at a farm in Hail Weston. One delivery driver was trapped for 20 minutes when the bird blocked his path, flew at his vehicle, and chased his van. Another pheasant, named Phil, began attacking members of a Shropshire family, prompting one of them to use a badminton racket for self-defense. Claythorpe Watermill and Wildfowl Gardens houses various animals, including waterfowl, wallabies, chipmunks, peacocks, and a family of otters.
An estimated 20,000 Syrian refugees will be resettled in the UK over the next five years. Concerns have been raised about housing, education, healthcare, and funding for additional services. The Local Government Association has requested more resources to assist its members in housing refugees rather than utilizing their limited housing stock. BBC correspondent Daniel Sandford reported that the UK aims to accept approximately 400 refugees monthly, selected by the UN Refugee Agency (UNHCR), with the plan to begin resettling people in October. Sandford stated that many local authorities are reluctant to use their council housing, leading to efforts to identify landlords willing to house Syrian refugees, funded by the state. Prime Minister David Cameron has indicated that refugees will come from camps bordering Syria, not from those already in Europe. Unlike other asylum seekers, they will be permitted to work immediately and are eligible for benefits. David Simmons from the Local Government Association expressed the councils' desire for assurance regarding long-term plans to support refugees. He emphasized the need for sustained resources to ensure families and children receive proper support, enabling them to integrate into UK life and access education, work opportunities, and housing. Separately, Central European ministers have once again rejected a mandatory quota system for distributing migrant arrivals. Tens of thousands of migrants, fleeing conflict in Syria and Libya, have recently attempted to reach Europe, with many traveling through Hungary to Germany, Austria, and Sweden, which have more generous asylum policies. European Council President Donald Tusk has announced his intention to convene an emergency summit later this month if a solution to the crisis is not found promptly.

Last month, allegations emerged that Exxon's scientists had raised concerns about global warming decades ago, and that the company subsequently worked to conceal this information. In response to a request from the New York attorney general for emails and financial records, Exxon Mobil called the allegations "inaccurate distortions." Kenneth Cohen, Exxon Mobil's vice-president for public affairs, stated on Thursday, "We unequivocally reject the allegations that Exxon Mobil has suppressed climate change research." The investigation focuses on whether the company misled investors this year about climate change risks identified by its own scientists. Exxon was accused by Inside Climate News and the Los Angeles Times of funding anti-climate change groups to misinform the public about the dangers of global warming. Both US presidential candidates Hillary Clinton and Bernie Sanders have called for inquiries into Exxon's actions. Attorney generals from other US states may also join New York in investigating the company.
On Monday at approximately 5:30 PM, three men broke into a house on Rouken Glen Road in Giffnock, near Glasgow. They stole cash and jewelry before fleeing when the homeowner's family arrived. Two of the men escaped in a dark grey Volvo driven by a fourth individual. The third man was seen walking towards Eastwood Toll. Although unharmed, the woman who was threatened was described by police as "extremely shaken." Police are actively searching for the suspects, all of whom were wearing dark clothing. Detective Constable Craig Faskin stated that this was a terrifying experience for the woman in her own home and emphasized the need to apprehend those responsible. Police are currently collecting information to determine the exact value of the stolen items and the precise details surrounding the robbery. As Rouken Glen Road was likely heavily trafficked during the incident, Detective Constable Faskin urged any witnesses who haven't already contacted the police, or anyone with relevant information, to reach out to Giffnock Police Office.
China's Purchasing Managers Index (PMI) dropped to 50.8, lower than analysts' predictions of a slight increase. While any score above 50 indicates growth, this figure represents a decline from the previous month's 51.1. This news follows recent data revealing China's overall economic growth slowed to its weakest pace in over five years, reaching 7.3% between July and September compared to the previous year, down from 7.5% in the preceding quarter. Analysts at China International Capital, commenting on the PMI data released on Saturday, stated that downward pressure on the economy persists and monetary policy will remain accommodative. In response to these challenges, Beijing has implemented measures to stimulate consumer spending, including easing restrictions on home purchases and injecting billions of dollars into major banks. The central bank has also reduced certain inter-bank interest rates. The government aims for 7.5% economic growth this year, though many analysts doubt its feasibility. There is speculation that further actions may be taken to bolster growth. Such substantial growth is crucial for creating sufficient employment opportunities for China's large and increasingly educated population. However, these growth ambitions exceed the projected rates in more developed economies, with the US economy currently expanding at an annual rate of 3.5%.
David Tinsley, a UK economist at UBS, believes Brexit will result in significantly reduced growth, a substantial decline in the pound, and further interest rate reductions by the Bank of England. He anticipates two rate cuts over the next six months, lowering the current record low of 0.5% to zero. Interest rates have remained at 0.5% since 2009 as Britain has grappled with economic recovery following the 2008 financial crisis. UBS also predicts that the vote will delay interest rate hikes in the US. Economists previously anticipated that Janet Yellen, governor of the US Federal Reserve, would increase rates in September, but UBS now believes this is unlikely. Tinsley expects UK growth rates to drop to zero in the second half of the year, down from the previous growth rate of about 2%, with potential for even greater long-term damage. The outcome hinges on future trade negotiations and the UK's access to the European single market. According to Tinsley, "Brexit could lower long-run UK GDP by about 3.0% under a scenario with very restricted access."
Harry Gregg, now 84, survived the 1958 Munich air crash that killed 23 people, including eight of his Manchester United teammates. The Northern Irishman shared that he asked to be alone when he heard the news. He found it incredibly difficult to process the tragedy. Gregg recently spoke about the crash on his charitable foundation's Facebook page after learning about the Colombian plane crash that killed 71 people, including 19 Chapecoense players. He mentioned losing friends and teammates in Munich, but also others on board, including a prominent journalist. A grieving Brazilian journalist reached out to Gregg after the Colombian tragedy, explaining that he had lost three sports journalist friends in the crash. Gregg emphasized the importance of remembering everyone who died in both accidents, not just the players. He hoped his words brought some comfort to the grieving journalist. Gregg heroically stayed with the burning plane in Munich and helped rescue several people, including a mother and baby. Six people, including three players, survived the Colombian crash, though goalkeeper Jackson Follmann had to have his right leg amputated. Gregg credits returning to training after the Munich crash with helping him cope with the trauma. A few months after the Munich air disaster, Gregg was named the best goalkeeper in the world for his performances for Northern Ireland at the 1958 World Cup in Sweden.
Rafael Marques de Morais, a long-time critic of Angola's President Jose Eduardo dos Santos, faces accusations of defamation from seven generals. The generals are alleged to be involved in murder, torture, and land grabs in Angola's diamond fields. De Morais, who previously spent time in prison for his writings, authored a book titled "Blood Diamonds: Torture and Corruption in Angola" which sparked the latest legal action against him. His lawyer maintains there is no connection between the Angolan armed forces and the alleged crimes. The trial, which began in Luanda, is being held behind closed doors. Protests outside the courthouse resulted in clashes between police and demonstrators. De Morais stated that the number of charges against him has increased from nine to at least 24. He received a freedom of expression award in the UK last week and has called for the charges against him to be dropped. De Morais believes that Angola's leaders, despite enjoying luxurious lifestyles, suppress dissent and label critics as "stooges of imperialists." If found guilty, he could face up to nine years in prison and a fine of $1.2 million.
Pavel Durov, the former head of VKontakte, stated that he learned of his dismissal from news reports. He claimed the shareholders lacked the courage to inform him directly. Durov asserted that VKontakte is now under the control of Igor Sechin and Alisher Usmanov. He expressed satisfaction with their seven and a half years of operation, highlighting their accomplishments. Sechin, the CEO of Rosneft and Putin's former deputy chief of staff, and Usmanov, Russia's wealthiest individual according to Forbes, who made his fortune in iron ore and steel and previously held a stake in Facebook, control VKontakte through their internet company, Mail.ru. Durov initially announced his resignation on April 1st but later retracted it as an April Fool's joke. However, VKontakte stated they acted on his March 21st resignation letter as it was not officially withdrawn within the allotted month. Durov confirmed his departure from Russia, stating his lack of intention to return due to the country's current incompatibility with internet businesses. He expressed his belief that a return to the company is impossible following his refusal to cooperate with authorities. VKontakte, with over 100 million users, had faced government requests for information, including details of Ukrainians involved in anti-government protests and the closure of a group supporting Alexei Navalny. Durov refused these requests and sold his shares to maintain his independence in decision-making, emphasizing his clean conscience and commitment to his ideals. Reports suggest a replacement for Durov will be chosen at the upcoming VKontakte board meeting.
The accusations against former President Mohamed Morsi stem from violence outside the Cairo presidential palace in December 2012, where at least seven people were killed in clashes. Morsi is facing trial for "incitement to murder and violence" related to these events. Fourteen other Muslim Brotherhood members will also stand trial on the same charges. Since his removal from power in July 2013, Morsi has been held in a secret location and faces multiple accusations. The military-backed interim government has cracked down on Brotherhood supporters who demand Morsi's reinstatement. Last month, hundreds of protesters died when security forces dispersed pro-Morsi camps in Cairo. State media reported that an investigation revealed Morsi instructed the Republican Guard and police minister to disperse the protests, but they refused. Morsi's aides then allegedly called on supporters to handle the demonstrators, resulting in clashes that left at least seven dead and hundreds injured. Morsi's supporters claim they were defending the palace from attacks by opposition activists. Among the 14 other suspects are prominent Brotherhood figures Mohammed al-Beltagi and Essam El-Erian. Morsi has also been accused of "premeditated murder" of prisoners, officers, and soldiers during a prison break in 2011, as well as plotting attacks on jails during the uprising that ousted President Hosni Mubarak and conspiring with Hamas.
Ebrahim Raisi, a judge and cleric, is considered the frontrunner for hardliners in the upcoming Iranian presidential election. Another candidate is former president Mahmoud Ahmadinejad, who registered despite receiving advice from the supreme leader against running. The Guardian Council will review candidates and announce the final list on April 27th. The election for the four-year term will be held on May 19th. Hassan Rouhani, the incumbent president, won by a large margin in 2013 on a platform of reducing Iran's diplomatic isolation and promoting a more open society. Two years later, his government and six other countries reached a significant agreement where Iran agreed to limit its sensitive nuclear activities in exchange for the removal of economic sanctions. During his registration, Rouhani stated his commitment to Iran, Islam, freedom, and stability. Raisi, a descendant of the Prophet Mohammed, declared his candidacy as representing all of Iran, not just a specific group or faction. Ahmadinejad, who served two terms from 2005 to 2013, registered as a candidate on Tuesday, surprising election officials who were aware of the supreme leader's warning against his candidacy. Ahmadinejad claimed the advice was merely a suggestion. He stated his registration was intended to support his former vice-president, Hamid Baghaei, who also registered. Over 120 individuals, including six women, submitted their candidacy on the first day of registration. The Guardian Council, which includes clerics, will evaluate the qualifications of all candidates before announcing the final list on April 27th. The 2009 disputed re-election of Ahmadinejad led to the largest protests in Iran since the 1979 Islamic Revolution. Millions demanded a new election, but the supreme leader maintained the result's validity and ordered a crackdown on dissent that resulted in the deaths of dozens of opposition supporters and the detention of thousands.
John Cleese modeled the TV sitcom *Fawlty Towers* after the Gleneagles Hotel in Torquay, where he stayed with the Monty Python team in 1973. Former owner Donald Sinclair unknowingly inspired Cleese's character, Basil Fawlty. Churchill Retirement Living will transform the site into 36 retirement apartments after receiving approval from Torbay Council. The 41-bedroom hotel closed earlier this year. Initially, the council rejected the company's application for the site, deeming it "too large and unsympathetic to the area." A revised plan for a smaller development was subsequently accepted. The new project will involve demolishing the three-star hotel and constructing 21 one-bedroom and 11 two-bedroom apartments, along with a guest suite and two communal lounges. Planning officials approved the change of use from tourist accommodation to residential, citing the former hotel's "commercial unviability." *Fawlty Towers*, ranked number one in the British Film Institute's 100 Greatest Television Programmes in 2000, aired for only 12 episodes.

Each year, fires in kitchen appliances like cookers, refrigerators, and tumble dryers result in 70 fatalities and hundreds of thousands of injuries. The Electrical Safety Council (ESC) believes manufacturers need to improve their customer tracing systems to effectively communicate safety issues. They argue that current guidelines are insufficient. Last December, Catherine Paterson from Middlesbrough experienced a fire that destroyed her garage. The fire originated in her four-month-old tumble dryer, which she later discovered had a known issue and was recalled by the manufacturer. After her neighbor alerted them to the fire, Catherine's garage was destroyed within 15 minutes, causing £10,000 in damages. She criticized the manufacturer for not contacting her about the faulty product, noting that they were aware of the issue since November but only reached out in February, two months after the fire. While Trading Standards maintains that the tumble dryer manufacturer made every effort to notify consumers about the fault through media and online notices, Steven Curtler, product safety manager at the ESC, believes Catherine's case demonstrates the inadequacy of current manufacturer guidelines. He advocates for industry procedure changes to prioritize consumer safety. Curtler emphasizes the need for improved product traceability, suggesting manufacturers collect more consumer information at the point of purchase. He argues that this would facilitate the timely dissemination of critical safety information. The ESC reports an average of 70 deaths and 350,000 serious injuries annually due to household appliances like refrigerators, gas cookers, and washing machines. A tragic example occurred in 2011 when a fire involving a fridge freezer in north London claimed the lives of a mother and her five children. The London Fire Brigade identifies fridge freezers as the most dangerous household appliance in fire situations, citing the highly flammable foam insulation at the back of the appliance. They urge individuals to contact the manufacturer or an electrician immediately if they notice any unusual noises coming from their fridge or freezer.

The Queen presented the traditional Welsh leek, wrapped in red thread and dipped in gold, to cadets, troops, and veterans. She expressed her pleasure in celebrating St David's Day with the regiment and noted that soldiers now engage with her more, suggesting she has become less intimidating with age. Wearing a heather wool tweed coat by Karl Ludwig, the Queen took the royal salute at Lucknow Barracks in Tidworth, Wiltshire, before the national anthem. She inspected the parade from a Range Rover and later met the regimental goats, Shenkin and Llywelyn. The Queen praised the British Army's reliance on regimental traditions and the pride and comradeship of its soldiers. She commended the regiment's motto, "Death rather than Dishonour," and expressed confidence in their future. The Royal Welsh, formed on St David's Day in 2006, is a leading armored infantry battalion. The Queen has been associated with the regiment and its predecessors since her coronation.
A Mumbai court sentenced Khan to five years in prison for killing Noor Ullah Khan by running him over while he slept on the pavement. Khan's lawyers filed an appeal, leading to the Bombay High Court granting him two days of interim bail. Noor Ullah Khan, 38, was one of five people struck by Khan's Toyota Land Cruiser when it crashed into the American Express bakery in Mumbai's Bandra area on September 28, 2002. Three others suffered serious injuries, and another sustained minor injuries. While Khan claimed his driver was behind the wheel, the judge ruled that Khan was driving under the influence of alcohol. Following his interim bail, Khan, who faced a potential 10-year sentence, returned home where he was visited by several Bollywood celebrities. Reports indicate Khan does not need to appear in court on Friday when his bail plea is heard. The case has captivated Bollywood and India for years. Judge DW Deshpande, who found Khan guilty of culpable homicide, stated in his 240-page ruling that Khan "didn't visit the hospital to meet the injured or provide aid" and "could have gone to the police immediately if he had done nothing wrong."

Queen Street Mill Museum in Burnley is one of five museums owned by Lancashire County Council that will close, although they will remain open for school visits. Queen Street Mill and Helmshore Mills Museum, which will also close, are the last remaining examples of Lancashire's cotton spinning and weaving. The council aims to save £65m by 2018. Councillor Marcus Johnstone, cabinet member for environment, planning and cultural services, stated, "Closing museums is the last thing we want to do, but the scale of the county council's financial challenge means we have to make these difficult decisions to protect services for the most vulnerable people in our communities." The other museums closing include the Museum of Lancashire in Preston, Fleetwood's Maritime Museum, and Judges' Lodgings in Lancaster. Lancashire County Council initially planned to close the five museums in the spring, but this was delayed to allow discussions with groups interested in operating them. A council spokesperson said these discussions are ongoing. Over 10,000 people signed a petition to save the museums at Queen Street and Helmshore Mills, arguing that they are "an absolute cornerstone of Lancashire's rich industrial heritage" during a time when the county played a significant role in Britain's Industrial Revolution.
The Foster for Fife Choir is performing a song composed by Ian Hammond Brown specifically for them. The song, titled "Help a Child Belong," encourages people to become foster parents for children in need. The choir has been performing in various locations throughout Fife. Scotland anticipates a need for approximately 800 foster families in the coming year. Rae Ormiston, from Fife Council's children and families department, stated that the choir's performance is a creative way to raise awareness about fostering. She also mentioned a decline in the number of individuals coming forward to foster, emphasizing the increasing need for publicity. Nikki Perry, a member of the Foster for Fife Choir and a foster parent for over 17 years, hopes the choir will highlight the rewarding nature of fostering. She shared that witnessing children thrive and build their own families is incredibly fulfilling. Perry expressed her desire to foster more children if she had the space. Nine-year-old Brooklyn, a choir member, shared how finding a foster family has positively impacted his life, providing him with a home and loving care. The Foster for Fife Choir is believed to be the first of its kind in Scotland.
Knight Frank reported a global increase of 6,340 ultra-high-net-worth individuals in 2016, reaching 193,490. This growth reversed a similar decline observed the previous year and was attributed to strong stock market performance. Individuals with over $30 million in net assets were included in the count. Despite political uncertainties and economic fluctuations caused by Brexit and the election of Donald Trump, many developed economies performed well, with US and UK stock markets reaching record highs. Andrew Amoils, head of research at New World Wealth, noted that while uncertainty exists, strong economic fundamentals and progress in regulation and policy indicate potential for growth. Knight Frank anticipates a 43% increase in ultra-high-net-worth individuals globally over the next decade, but expects regional variations. US growth is projected to slightly outpace the UK over the next ten years, while Europe is expected to see only 12% growth. Asia, however, is predicted to experience the most significant increase at 91%, potentially narrowing the gap with the US by 2026. Currently, the US has 27,020 more ultra-wealthy individuals than Asia. The research also suggests that Asian cities like Singapore, Shanghai, and Beijing will surpass wealth concentrations in cities like San Francisco as their wealthy populations grow. Several African countries, including Ethiopia, Rwanda, and Tanzania, are also projected to witness substantial increases in their wealthy populations.

A study by the University of Edinburgh's Physical Activity for Health Research Centre found that Scots aged 45 to 54 spend an average of 7.8 hours sitting each weekday. This is higher than the 7.4 hours of sedentary time for those over 75. Sedentary time includes any waking activity done while sitting, such as working, eating, watching TV, or using a computer. The study authors emphasized the potential health risks of excessive sitting at work. Experts warn that more than seven hours of sedentary time daily increases the risk of premature death, cardiovascular disease, type 2 diabetes, and certain cancers, even if individuals are physically active at other times. Only the youngest group surveyed, aged 16 to 24, are less sedentary than those over 75 on weekdays. The findings, published in the Journal of Sports Sciences, are based on data from over 14,000 people in Scotland from the 2012-14 Scottish Health Survey. On weekends, the pattern reverses, with those aged 25 to 54 being the least sedentary, sitting for 5.2 to 5.7 hours daily, while those over 75 sit for 7.3 to 7.4 hours. For the youngest group, most sitting time is spent in front of a TV or screen. Men spend less time on screens as they age, while women peak in middle age. Lead researcher Tessa Strain stated that a significant portion of the population is dangerously sedentary, and that addressing high levels of sedentary time in early and middle adulthood is crucial, as habits established then may persist. She suggests that changing workplace habits could be a good starting point due to the amount of time spent sitting at work.

Although Ms. Tsai previously stated her intention to maintain the "status quo," some analysts believe her rhetoric has become more assertive since her victory, emphasizing that any suppression will destabilize cross-strait relations. Chinese state media condemned the election results, urging Taiwan to abandon its "illusion" of independence. A Global Times report attributed Ms. Tsai's victory not to her pro-independence stance but to dissatisfaction with the performance of outgoing President Ma Ying-jeou and his Kuomintang party. Ms. Tsai stated her commitment to advancing cross-strait policy within the existing framework of the Republic of China's constitution, based on democratic principles and public opinion. She emphasized her intention to seek mutually acceptable interactions between Taiwan and mainland China, avoiding provocations or surprises. She stressed the responsibility of both sides to engage in dignified and reciprocal interactions, ensuring stability and preventing incidents. Ms. Tsai highlighted the Taiwanese people's will as expressed through the election, emphasizing Taiwan's democratic system, national identity, and international space, and reiterating that any suppression would harm cross-strait relations.
Ocado, a company with an automated warehouse in Andover, Hampshire, utilizes robots to select crates containing customer order items. While human workers currently assist with selection, Ocado plans to automate this process entirely. They are also developing a humanoid maintenance robot called SecondHands to work alongside human colleagues in the warehouse. Ocado's fruit and vegetable picking robot is part of a five-year EU-funded research collaboration involving five European universities and Disney, known as Soma (Soft Manipulation). This demonstration device is an early prototype. Ocado aims to mimic the human hand by using air pressure to control the robotic fingers. Their goal is to combine computer vision, allowing the robot to recognize products, with the ability to grasp them accurately. Initially, only the gripper is being demonstrated, but the robot will eventually learn to distinguish fruit ripeness through machine learning. It will also be able to handle other delicate items like wine bottles and detergent. Ocado emphasizes the difficulty of fruit and vegetable picking, as customers expect high-quality produce. Professor Chris Melhuish, director of the Bristol Robotics Laboratory, acknowledges the challenge of creating robotic devices capable of performing multiple tasks. He highlights the complexity of replicating the human hand's flexibility and adaptability, noting that many robotics projects focus on specific applications rather than general-purpose manipulation.
Truro chairman Peter Masters announced that the club will move to their new Silver Bow ground on the outskirts of the city by July 26, 2017, after receiving a Section 106 planning order. Construction is scheduled to begin in October, with the possibility of starting earlier. This agreement mirrors the one sought by the developers of the rival Stadium for Cornwall, who aim to build a 10,000-seat ground for the Cornish Pirates. Both projects received planning permission in July 2015 but needed further approval from the Department of Local Government and the Section 106 order. Truro, a National League South side, had initially planned to share Torquay United's Plainmoor ground next season but decided to remain at Treyew Road for one more year. After the new stadium is built, a retail park will be developed on the Treyew Road site. The new ground will meet the minimum 'A' grade standards, accommodating 4,000 fans with the potential to expand to 5,000, making it suitable for the highest level of non-league football. It will also feature a synthetic 3G surface and conference facilities. Masters and business partner Philip Perryman took over the club in December 2012, rescuing it from the brink of liquidation after administration earlier that year. Following relegation from the Conference South, the club was promoted back to the second tier of non-league football last year by winning the Southern Premier League play-offs. Masters expressed pride in the club's turnaround, highlighting their debt-free status, promotion, and the ownership of a new stadium.

Prior to the May general election, Layla Blackwood, the Conservative MP for Oxford West and Abingdon, disclosed her diagnosis of Ehlers-Danlos syndrome. This condition affects the joints and muscles, often resulting in persistent pain. Blackwood is advocating to diminish the stigma surrounding chronic illnesses. Diagnosed two years prior, she experienced joint pain, migraines, and asthma before receiving her diagnosis. She chose to share her condition publicly before the election, believing it was important for constituents to know. Blackwood also aimed to demonstrate that individuals with invisible illnesses can successfully perform demanding, high-profile roles. She emphasized that Ehlers-Danlos syndrome is not terminal and, despite its chronic nature, individuals can lead fulfilling lives. Blackwood expressed concern about the stigma surrounding disability and chronic illness in the UK, urging a shift in perspective. In an interview with BBC Oxford, Blackwood shared that dietary modifications and Pilates have been beneficial in managing her condition.

In October 2016, Communities Secretary Sajid Javid approved Cuadrilla's plans for shale gas extraction at the Preston New Road site in Little Plumpton, overturning Lancashire County Council's (LCC) 2015 refusal. LCC had denied permission due to concerns about noise and traffic. The Preston New Road Action Group (PNRAG) criticized Javid's decision, calling it "fundamentally flawed" and an "abuse of power." They plan to challenge the decision in court on March 15th in Manchester. Cuadrilla's project, which involves horizontal fracking, is expected to increase gas yield. Construction began in January, prompting ongoing protests from anti-fracking activists. Claire Stephenson of PNRAG stated that Javid's decision disregarded the community's wishes and local authorities' rejections. The Department for Communities and Local Government declined to comment on the upcoming case.
The Prison Officers Association reported an ongoing disturbance at HMP Swaleside, a category B training prison on the Isle of Sheppey. The Prison Service deployed specialist "Tornado" teams to manage the situation. POA chairman Mike Rolfe stated that the extent of the disturbance was uncertain, but it did not appear to be as severe as the riot at HMP Birmingham last week. Riot police were used to control last Friday's 12-hour disturbance, which involved hundreds of inmates. A Prison Service spokesman confirmed an incident involving 60 prisoners on A Wing at HMP Swaleside, assuring that the rest of the prison was secure with all prisoners confined to their cells. The situation was contained and being resolved, he added. Mr. Rolfe described Swaleside as a challenging workplace due to staff shortages and high levels of violence. An unverified source claiming to be an inmate reported the disturbance began when staff searched cells and confiscated belongings. The source attributed increased tension between inmates and officers to the arrival of inexperienced staff and reduced family visits. BBC home affairs correspondent Danny Shaw reported that while the disturbance was contained, prisoners refused to return to their cells. "Tornado" squads were also employed to quell last week's riot in Birmingham, where prisoners started fires, stole keys from a guard, and one inmate sustained serious injuries. Guardian prison correspondent Eric Allison stated he was informed of problems at the prison during a recent research visit. Graham expressed surprise, stating he was up against established pop stars. The Critics' Choice award, a strong indicator of success, has previously been awarded to artists like Adele, Sam Smith, and Emeli Sande, with most winners achieving platinum status for their debut albums. Jack Garratt, last year's winner, is the exception, with his album "Phase" currently certified silver. Rag N Bone Man, from Uckfield near Brighton, began his career as a jungle MC before his parents encouraged him to pursue singing. Inspired by blues artists like John Lee Hooker, his music blends soulful melodies with hip-hop beats and gospel influences. He was discovered after his girlfriend sent recordings of him singing, including some made on the toilet, to promoters. He chose his stage name after watching "Steptoe and Son," envisioning it as a classic blues moniker akin to Muddy Waters or Howlin' Wolf. "Human" was a hit in Europe before reaching the UK, where it entered the top 40 after being covered by an "X Factor" contestant. His debut album is scheduled for release in February, and he is also nominated for the BBC's Sound of 2017. A headline tour is planned for April, culminating in two shows at London's Shepherd's Bush Empire. The Critics' Choice award is chosen by a panel of music experts, and over 100 artists were considered for this year's shortlist. Rag N Bone Man will perform at the Brit Awards launch show on ITV on January 14th. The main ceremony will be held on February 24th at the O2 Arena in London, hosted by Michael Buble, although his participation is uncertain due to his son's illness.
Belfast City Council plans to spend £35 million on renovations for the Robinson Centre in Castlereagh and the Brook Activity Centre in Twinbrook. The Robinson Centre will be replaced with a modern facility featuring an eight-lane pool and leisure waters, costing £20 million. It closed in 2015 due to asbestos concerns. The £15 million Brook Activity Centre redevelopment will prioritize outdoor leisure with new Gaelic football, football, and five-a-side pitches. These projects are part of a £105 million citywide initiative to improve leisure centres. Council chairman Peter Johnston sees the renovations as an investment in the city's health and well-being, anticipating construction to begin this summer with completion in early 2019. Also approved at the planning meeting was a revised plan for Windsor House, Ireland's tallest office building, purchased by Hastings Hotels in 2015. It will be transformed into the Grand Central Hotel, featuring 304 bedrooms, serviced apartments, and offices.
Senator David Leyonhjelm, a libertarian, believes Section 18C of Australia's Racial Discrimination Act, which prohibits insulting someone based on race, hinders free speech and should be repealed. To illustrate his point, he filed a complaint under the very law he opposes. Mark Kenny, a newspaper columnist, made remarks about Leyonhjelm in an article, labeling him a "boorish, supercilious know-all with the empathy of a besser block" and referring to him as an "angry white male." While Leyonhjelm stated he wasn't offended, he saw an opportunity to demonstrate why Section 18C should be repealed. He argued that Kenny's article violated the law because it was likely to offend, insult, humiliate, or intimidate individuals based on their race. Leyonhjelm predicted that his complaint would succeed if the Human Rights Commission adhered to the law. He added that if Section 18C were repealed, Kenny would be free to express his opinions about him without legal repercussions. Section 18C prohibits public actions that "offend, insult, humiliate or intimidate" individuals or groups based on their race, color, or national or ethnic origin. Conservatives have criticized the law since it was used against Andrew Bolt, a prominent right-wing commentator. Former Prime Minister Tony Abbott pledged to repeal Section 18C in 2013 but ultimately abandoned the effort due to strong opposition. This week, Abbott reignited the debate by stating that he should have pursued a less radical reform that removed the words "offend" and "insult" from the law.
Matthew Chapman, a 48-year-old businessman from Louth, Lincolnshire, was the sole occupant of a privately owned helicopter when it crashed on June 6th. The inquest learned that Mr. Chapman had dropped off two passengers at the Creg Ny Baa, a popular viewing spot for the TT races, shortly before the accident in Baldwin Valley. He had departed from Milton Keynes around 6:00 BST with two passengers on board his Bell Jetranger aircraft. Mr. Chapman's plan was to refuel at Ronaldsway Airport before returning to watch the races with friends. However, his helicopter crashed at approximately 9:00 BST in a field on a hillside near the 33rd milestone on the Mountain Road. Mr. Chapman's body was discovered trapped in the wreckage and pronounced dead at the scene. A paramedic's report indicated multiple traumas consistent with a helicopter crash. Coroner John Needham stated that the cause of death remains undetermined and that the UK's Air Accidents Investigations Branch (AAIB) is conducting inquiries into the crash. The inquest has been adjourned to a future date.
Graham Ovenden, from Bodmin Moor, Cornwall, abused children who were the subjects of his paintings in the 1970s and 1980s. District judge Elizabeth Roscoe later ordered the destruction of paintings and photos she deemed indecent. Ovenden was imprisoned in 2013 after the Court of Appeal deemed his suspended sentence "unduly lenient." Judge Roscoe acknowledged the decision would "invite the wrath of the art world." In the House of Lords, independent peer Lord Clancarty stated that the "unprecedented judgement has disturbing implications" and carries "huge implications" for the "freedom of expression of many contemporary artists." Following Ovenden's conviction, the Tate removed over 30 prints from its online collection. Labour's Shadow Culture spokesman Lord Stevenson questioned whether the government condones criticism of other countries for destroying art while refusing to take action domestically. Internet Safety Minister Lady Shields asserted the government cannot interfere with the court's decision but noted the artist could appeal. She stated, "There were works of art in this collection that were relating to the individual specifically that were child sexual abuse images, and you would agree that those should definitely be destroyed." Ovenden was found guilty at Truro Crown Court of six counts of indecency with a child and one of indecent assault. The jury acquitted him of three counts of indecency and two of indecent assault.
Simon Goldsmith, an Elvis impersonator and delivery driver from Kesgrave, Suffolk, wrote a rap for the pre-event briefing at the Kesgrave Parkrun. Inspired by a fellow organizer's 100th run, the 42-year-old rapped to the runners before they started. He said his phone has been "crazy" since with people amused by his performance. The 90th run at the Kesgrave venue took place on Saturday. It started in September 2014 and is one of hundreds of free, weekly runs worldwide. Organizer Jen Evans, who was called a "legend" in the rap, said the briefing was "surreal." She explained that Mr. Goldsmith showed up with a speaker and a PA system and told her he was going to try something different. He then began rapping, and everyone fell silent, awed and finding it very funny. Mr. Goldsmith said it took him 15 minutes to write the rap and the response has been "pretty amazing." He joked that it's about time the world noticed a white, middle-class rapper from Suffolk rapping in a Suffolk accent.
Judges, lawyers, academics, and student leaders are among the suspects in the case. The attorney general claims they created a "secret society" connected to the Muslim Brotherhood that planned to overthrow the government. Human rights organizations criticize the trial as deeply flawed and a "mockery of justice." The UAE, comprised of seven sheikdoms ruled by families, prohibits political parties and demonstrations. Several of its Gulf neighbors, including Bahrain, Yemen, Oman, and Saudi Arabia, have experienced pro-democracy protests inspired by the 2011 revolutions in Tunisia and Egypt. The activists were arrested last year during a campaign targeting civil society activists suspected of political dissent. Many are associated with the Islamist group al-Islah, which authorities claim has ties to the Muslim Brotherhood. Al-Islah maintains its support for peaceful reform and denies any connection to the brotherhood. They are accused of utilizing media and social networking platforms to incite opposition to the UAE's government and ruling families. "They established and ran an organization aimed at opposing the fundamental principles of the UAE's system of governance and seizing power," stated the attorney general. Human rights groups denounce the trial as a sham, alleging that defendants were denied legal representation until two weeks before the trial and were not provided with documents outlining the charges and evidence against them. They also report being subjected to harsh treatment in custody, including bright lights, hoods, and verbal abuse from prison guards. "It appears the UAE authorities will subject numerous citizens to a shamelessly unfair judicial process that mocks justice," stated Human Rights Watch in a press release. They urge US Secretary of State John Kerry to address concerns about a fair trial during his visit to the UAE this week. The 94 defendants are facing trial in the UAE's highest court, meaning the decision will be final with no possibility of appeal.
The standards committee in Wales is investigating whether stricter lobbying regulations are needed. While Scotland and Westminster have established lobbying registers, Wales has not. A campaigner believes that Wales would best protect its reputation by opening the lobbying sector to scrutiny now. Lobbyists are individuals or companies paid to influence government decisions. Plaid Cymru Assembly Member Neil McEvoy called for regulation of lobbying in Wales earlier this year, including a requirement for lobbyists to register. He argued that regulation would ensure transparency within the assembly by revealing who commercial lobbyists represent. However, the current standards commissioner, who will be replaced by Sir Roderick Evans in December, stated to the committee that there have been no complaints regarding lobbying at the National Assembly. He made this comment in a briefing prepared for his appearance before Assembly Members on Tuesday. The standards commissioner also informed the committee that establishing a statutory register of lobbyists would likely have significant resource implications. Tamasin Cave, leader of the Alliance for Lobbying Transparency, emphasized that a key concern surrounding lobbying is its covert nature, stating that it is unclear who is communicating with whom in government and what agreements are being made. She believes this applies to politicians in both Cardiff and Westminster. Cave suggested a straightforward solution is a lobbying register requiring paid lobbyists to disclose their identity, who they represent, whom they are lobbying, and their objectives. She argued that Wales should open lobbying to scrutiny now to maintain its reputation, which has not been marred by scandals involving lobbyists like those frequently seen in Westminster. Huw Roberts, chairman of the prominent Welsh lobbying company Deryn, stated that his company publishes a list of clients and employees and adheres to a "strong code of conduct" as members of the Association of Professional Political Consultants (APPC), the industry's self-regulatory body. Mark Glover, chairman of the APPC, stressed the importance of any new Welsh register providing consistent transparency for both consultant and in-house lobbyists, avoiding excessive bureaucracy or cost. In 2013, the assembly standards committee concluded that Assembly Members should face tighter scrutiny but not a full official register. First Minister Carwyn Jones has stated that lobbyists do not have access to Welsh ministers. The Assembly committee agreed to conduct a written consultation on the issue at its meeting on Tuesday.
Encircled by Rome, Vatican City has a population primarily composed of priests and nuns from diverse countries. It is a small entity compared to the former Papal States of central Italy, which were absorbed by the Italian unification movement in the mid-19th century. Following this, popes became confined to the Vatican until 1929, when Italy's Fascist government signed the Lateran Treaty, establishing the current Vatican City state. Within its limited area, the Vatican City features prominent buildings such as St. Peter's Basilica, a significant pilgrimage site. The Vatican Museums and Art Galleries showcase the extensive art collections amassed by the popes. In response to a 2010 investigation into the Vatican Bank for money-laundering violations, the Vatican has taken steps to enhance financial transparency. With a population of approximately 800 and an area of 0.44 square kilometers, Vatican City's official languages are Latin and Italian. Christianity is the dominant religion, with a life expectancy of 74 years for men and 81 years for women. The euro is the currency, and Pope Francis is the head of state. In 2013, cardinals elected Pope Francis, the first Latin American pope, choosing Cardinal Archbishop Jorge Mario Bergoglio of Buenos Aires to succeed Pope Benedict XVI, who resigned due to health concerns after an eight-year reign. At 76 years old, Pope Francis was only two years younger than Benedict at the time of his election, surprising expectations for a younger leader to address the Catholic Church's challenges. His health, marked by a single lung, raised concerns about his ability to handle the demanding role. He garnered broad support within the College of Cardinals due to his conservative stance on homosexuality and liberal views on social issues like poverty and inequality. Vatican Radio, known as "The Pope's Voice," broadcasts globally through powerful transmitters. However, the station has faced accusations of endangering lives in a Rome suburb by exceeding Italy's electromagnetic radiation standards, claims the Vatican denies. The papacy maintains a Twitter presence at @pontifex.
Brexit could alter the rules regarding signing players from other EU countries. This could impact clubs like Canning's, which have seven or eight players from the EU. Canning questioned how the new rules would be implemented, whether immediately or upon contract renewal. A BBC report stated that none of the 53 non-British EU players currently in the Scottish Premiership would qualify for a work permit based solely on their international experience. Professor Raymond Boyle, a sports industry expert at the University of Glasgow, suggested that the rules might be relaxed to facilitate the arrival of non-EU players, similar to the arrangements for Norway and Switzerland. Canning acknowledged that the potential changes could influence their recruitment strategy, as they typically avoid players requiring work permits. He explained that attracting international players who meet work permit requirements would be challenging, making it harder to sign foreign talent. Canning believes that while developing homegrown players is important, a balance must be maintained.

A steel company has halted production at its Redcar blast furnace due to decreased demand and rising expenses. The company, SSI, acquired the former Tata Steel facility in 2010 and employs approximately 2,000 workers. A government representative stated that European Union regulations prevent direct government funding for British steel production. The company's chief operating officer urged the government to take concrete actions to save the plant, noting that other European countries are supporting their steel industries. He requested government approval for underwriting key projects to secure commercial loans, along with reductions in business rates and eased CO2 emission restrictions. However, the government representative explained that strict state aid rules, particularly for the steel industry, limit their options. While they can invest in research and development, providing loans or underwriting is prohibited. They acknowledged the company's difficulties but emphasized the global steel overproduction issue. Despite the challenges, the government expressed commitment to supporting the British steel industry. Unions at the plant called the production halt "devastating" and requested immediate discussions with management.
Dan Tan, also known as Tan Seet Eng, has been detained indefinitely since October 2013. Despite this, a judge ruled that his detention was unlawful because he did not pose a threat to public safety. Tan, who maintains his innocence, is wanted by several countries, including Italy, although Singapore does not have extradition treaties with them. Interpol has implicated him in fixing hundreds of sporting events, primarily football matches, and suspects his involvement in fixing matches in Italy's Serie A and Serie B in 2011. Interpol has previously labeled the 51-year-old as the "mastermind and leader of the world's most notorious match-fixing syndicate". Singapore has a law permitting indefinite detention of suspected criminals without trial, with annual reviews. Judge Sundaresh Menon stated that while Tan's alleged actions were "reprehensible and should not be condoned", there was no evidence to suggest they harmed public safety or peace. He explained that the fixed matches, whether successful or not, occurred outside of Singapore, and there was no indication Tan collaborated with international crime syndicates or that such activities were based in Singapore.
The EU decided to withhold funding for the elections due to restrictions on the media, excessive force against demonstrators, and an intimidating climate for opposition parties and civil society. This decision follows the Catholic Church's announcement that it will no longer assist in organizing the elections amid the political unrest. Earlier this month, Burundi's president survived a coup attempt. Rights groups report that at least 20 people have died in protests since the president announced his intention to seek a third term in April. The UN estimates that 70,000 people have fled to neighboring countries due to fears of political violence. The EU's foreign policy chief stated that their team on the ground observed serious problems with the election process, including restrictions on independent media, excessive force against protesters, intimidation of opposition groups and civil society, and a lack of confidence in the election authorities. The Catholic Church, which holds significant influence in Burundi, has also expressed concern, asking priests who head provincial electoral commissions to step down. The Bishop of Burundi stated that the church cannot support an election with so many shortcomings and urged people not to vote under duress or coercion. Parliamentary elections are scheduled for June 5th, and the presidential election for June 26th. The president's opponents argue that his bid for a third term violates the constitution, which mandates a two-term limit. However, Burundi's Constitutional Court ruled that the president's first term does not count because he was elected by parliament, not by popular vote.
The Joint Ministerial Committee (EN) met for two hours on Thursday morning at the Cabinet Office to discuss Brexit papers from the Scottish government and Northern Ireland executive. The Scottish paper outlines demands for Scotland to remain in the single market and for increased devolution. Scottish Minister for UK Negotiations on Scotland's Place in Europe, Mike Russell, expressed hope that the options presented in the paper, particularly those regarding Scotland's single market membership and increased devolution, would be considered. He stated these options remain realistic and have widespread support. The Northern Ireland paper focuses on the border situation between Northern Ireland and the EU post-Brexit. The Welsh paper was absent, leading to inquiries about its delay. Welsh Finance Secretary Mark Drakeford explained that the paper will be published next week and will be a key item on the next JMC agenda in early February. He stated the Welsh government wants to consider the positions of Scotland and Northern Ireland, as well as the Prime Minister's recent views, before finalizing their paper. The Welsh paper will address matters of paramount importance to Wales and outline the Welsh government's priorities for the negotiations. Despite each devolved government having its own Brexit agenda, frustration with the JMC (EN) process was evident. Sinn Fein, facing upcoming elections, expressed consideration of withdrawing from the committee unless the British government alters its approach. Mike Russell criticized Theresa May's recent speech as a violation of the process, asserting that devolved governments should be involved in decision-making rather than simply being informed. Mark Drakeford clarified that the Welsh government, while acknowledging anxieties regarding the JMC, will not follow Sinn Fein's lead in withdrawing.

Detective Inspector Kevin Hayward from Suffolk Police stated that the investigation remains active. Nicki Durbin believes someone local possesses crucial information and urges them to come forward. The Norfolk and Suffolk major investigation team believes Luke was unlawfully killed. Scott Van Zyl, a South African hunter, was killed last week while on a hunting safari near the Limpopo River on the Zimbabwe-South Africa border. He disappeared while searching for crocodiles with a local tracker and his dogs. After his dogs returned to camp without him, a search and rescue operation was launched. Van Zyl's footprints and discarded rucksack were found on the river bank. He was married with two children and led hunting trips for foreign clients. Staff from the Heritage Protection Group, an organization headed by Sakkie Louwrens, assisted Zimbabwean authorities in the search. Three Nile crocodiles in the area were shot, and one contained Van Zyl's remains, confirmed by DNA testing. This incident is one of at least four fatal crocodile attacks in Zimbabwe this year. One Green Planet, a conservation group, criticized the circumstances of Van Zyl's death, stating that he should not have been hunting in the wild.

In March 2015, the tug Asterix was maneuvering the chemical tanker Donizetti away from a berth at Fawley marine terminal when strong winds caused the tug to capsize. The coxswain was trapped in the wheelhouse and rescued by a colleague just before the vessel sank. The Marine Accident Investigation Branch (MAIB) determined that the crew's training was inadequate. The coxswain lost control of the tug shortly after 8:00 PM BST on March 30, 2015, due to a lack of training in using a 'gog' rope, designed to prevent the tug from being pulled over. The investigation also revealed communication issues between the Donizetti and Asterix and insufficient experience among the launch crews in using the tug's towing hook emergency release mechanism. Safety and training recommendations were issued to the tug's operator, Ã˜stensjÃ¸ Rederi AS, ABP Southampton, and the National Workboat Association. A crew member received a bravery award for rescuing the coxswain, who along with the rescuer, required hospital treatment for shock and hypothermia.
Antonio Conte, the former Juventus and Italy coach, faces potential charges of "sporting fraud" related to alleged match-fixing during the 2010-11 season. A prosecutor has requested charges against Conte and 103 others involved in the case, which centers around suspected attempts to manipulate matches in Serie B, Lega Pro, and some Coppa Italia games. Conte, who has consistently denied any wrongdoing, previously served a ban for failing to report alleged match-fixing involving his former club Siena. The Italian Football Federation (FIGC) conducted its own investigation, leading to a 10-month ban for Conte (later reduced to four months on appeal) and bans of up to five years for over 50 players. Several clubs, including Atalanta and Siena, also faced point deductions. The next step is a closed-door hearing where a judge will decide whether to press charges as requested by the prosecutor. Conte achieved three consecutive Serie A titles with Juventus before becoming Italy coach last year.
The Council of Mortgage Lenders (CML) reported a significant decrease in loan demand during April. Mortgage lending reached £18.4 billion, an 11% decline from March. However, monthly mortgage lending figures can be volatile, often dropping in spring. Adjusting for seasonality, the CML stated that borrowing last month exceeded the year's average and was 4% higher than April 2016. The CML's senior economist, Mohammad Jamei, described the housing market as "moving sideways." Despite this, other indicators suggest a housing market slowdown. HM Revenue and Customs reported a 22.5% drop in property transactions in April compared to March. One potential reason is that landlords may have accelerated purchases to avoid recent tax changes. Effective April 1st, the tax relief available on mortgage payments for landlords was reduced. The CML also noted that first-time buyers surpassing home movers for the first time since 1996 in mortgage applications. Research shows that while home movers and landlords are taking fewer mortgages, first-time buyers are increasing. "First-time buyers and remortgage customers seem to be supporting the market," said Mohammad Jamei. "Home movers are facing challenges. Their activity has been weak, and the low number of movers means fewer properties for sale. This supply and demand imbalance will continue to support house prices, even as the pace of price increases slows."
