The owners of a 1,500lb (680kg) animal named Moses in Clackamas County, Oregon, contacted rescuers because they couldn't free him from a sinkhole on their property. The owners, worried Moses might break his legs, explained they had tried to pull him out but were unsuccessful. Firefighters responded late Tuesday and dug for hours to reach Moses, who cried and wailed throughout the ordeal. They finally managed to slip straps under his belly and pull him out. After lying on his side for an hour to rest, Moses got up, walked to his knees, and slowly made his way to his barn. A veterinarian later examined Moses and confirmed he was unharmed.
Four-year-old Macsen Oatway from Neath suffers from LAD1, a rare immune deficiency affecting only around 400 people globally. He spent weeks in a specialized isolation unit in Newcastle, only accessible to his parents, doctors, and nurses. Macsen received life-changing treatment consisting of blood transfusions, chemotherapy, and a bone marrow transplant, eliminating the need for lifelong antibiotics. His parents, Richard and Zoe, describe the treatment as a "transformation" for Macsen and urge others to register as bone marrow donors, ensuring future children with LAD1 can live full lives. Richard emphasizes the importance of registering, stating, "You may never get called, but if you do, take it with both hands. It's a fantastic opportunity to save a life." He expresses profound gratitude to the anonymous donor who saved Macsen's life. Doctors have declared Macsen well enough to attend school, which he reportedly enjoys.

Christopher Langley, a resident of Pontypridd, collaborated with pub owner James Lovegrove to create a collection of paintings. The Campaign for Real Ale (Camra) reports that two pubs in Wales close each week. Langley has completed 13 paintings and aims to finish another 20 in the coming months, driven by his concern for the potential loss of historical significance. Featured pubs include The Vulcan, recently saved by St Fagan's Museum, along with the York Hotel, the Pantmawr Inn, and the Glendower. Langley stated that the project has broadened his understanding of pub culture, as he had not grown up around them. He explained that his conversations with Lovegrove about Cardiff's pubs have shed light on this aspect of history. Langley emphasizes the importance of preserving and sharing this history. He uses drawings or photographs if the buildings no longer stand. Langley also shared that his perspective on pub architecture has evolved through the painting process, allowing him to appreciate the craftsmanship and effort invested in their creation.

Elephant and Castle roundabout in south London had the highest number of cycling accidents between 2009 and 2013, with 80 reported incidents. Data from the Department for Transport (DfT), analyzed by Aviva insurers, included accidents within the M25 that were reported to or attended by police. Transport for London (TfL) acknowledged that any accident was concerning and stated that they were investing nearly £1 billion to improve cycling infrastructure, including upgrading Cycle Superhighways, introducing new segregated cycle routes, and redesigning junctions. In March 2014, the London mayor's office announced a £4 billion plan to revamp London's roads, which included a new layout for Elephant and Castle roundabout with more cycle lanes. Trafalgar Square in Central London had the second highest number of accidents involving cyclists during the same period, with 46 recorded. Caroline Pidgeon, leader of the Liberal Democrat London Assembly Group, criticized TfL for under-spending on junction improvements and cycling infrastructure. In 2015, there were four cyclist fatalities in London, all involving lorries. The most recent occurred in Victoria when a mother of two died. Earlier in February, an increase in road fatalities prompted Scotland Yard to reintroduce Operation Safeway, which deploys 600 officers at 166 junctions during peak hours to target traffic offenses.
Scotland now uses 111 for medical advice, replacing the previous 11-digit number. Previously, callers paid local rates from landlines, while mobile users often incurred significantly higher charges. This change aligns Scotland with England, which adopted the 111 number in February. The NHS 24 service remains unchanged, with nurses and healthcare professionals providing advice on non-urgent health issues around the clock. Scottish Health Secretary Alex Neil first announced the introduction of a freephone number in January 2014, stating it would eliminate a barrier to healthcare. This move was praised by poverty campaigners, such as Caroline Mockford, who faced a nearly Â£8 charge from her mobile provider for a previous call to the helpline. Mockford subsequently presented a petition to the Scottish Parliament, urging the elimination of phone charges.
The Dominican Republic is a Caribbean nation heavily reliant on tourism and free-trade zones, which now serve as its primary sources of income and employment, replacing the country's previous dependence on sugar, coffee, and other exports. The population is predominantly of mixed European and African descent. Western influence is evident in the colonial architecture of Santo Domingo, the capital city, as well as in the country's art and literature. African heritage is reflected in Dominican music. With a population of 10.2 million and an area of 48,072 square kilometers, the Dominican Republic's official language is Spanish, and the dominant religion is Christianity. The average life expectancy is 71 years for men and 77 years for women. The national currency is the Dominican peso. Danilo Medina Sanchez was re-elected as president in May 2016. He first assumed office on August 16, 2012, succeeding three-time president Leonel Fernandez Reyna. Medina, an economist and long-standing member of the Dominican Liberation Party (PLD), was previously barred from seeking consecutive terms due to constitutional restrictions. However, a constitutional amendment in 2015 allowed him to run for re-election. His presidency has been marked by controversial decisions, including the relaxation of abortion laws and the implementation of a naturalization law that resulted in thousands of Haitian immigrants' descendants becoming stateless. Media ownership in the Dominican Republic is concentrated among a few economically or politically influential entities. The country boasts numerous terrestrial television channels and many multichannel cable TV operators, along with over 300 radio stations, most of which are commercial. The government operates its own television and radio networks. Press freedom is constitutionally guaranteed, and media outlets reflect diverse political viewpoints. Key historical events in the Dominican Republic include: 1492 - Christopher Columbus's arrival on the island, which he named Hispaniola, or "Little Spain"; 1496 - the establishment of the first Spanish colony in the Western Hemisphere at Santo Domingo, which subsequently served as the capital of all Spanish colonies in America; 1697 - the Treaty of Ryswick, which ceded the western part of Hispaniola (Haiti) to France and the eastern part (Santo Domingo - present-day Dominican Republic) to Spain; 1795 - Spain's cession of its portion of Hispaniola to France; 1808 - Spain'
Last month, the PSNI initiated an investigation after discovering that a law firm had received confidential documents from the ombudsman's office unlawfully. A 69-year-old former ombudsman investigator, arrested in England, was later released on bail. To address concerns about a potential conflict of interest, as the ombudsman investigates allegations against PSNI officers and former RUC members, the PSNI has decided to appoint an independent police service to lead the investigation. This new team is expected to be in place within weeks. The documents, believed to be part of an ongoing investigation into police misconduct, allegedly name at least eight former RUC officers and are considered potentially dangerous by the PSNI and MI5. The PSNI declined to comment on whether the investigation focuses solely on one case or has expanded to encompass other cases, citing the ongoing criminal investigation. In response, the PSNI has temporarily suspended the release of sensitive material related to numerous ombudsman investigations while reviewing security protocols. The Ombudsman, Dr. Michael Maguire, has referred the alleged theft and disclosure to the information commissioner. The Centre for the Protection of National Infrastructure has also been tasked with reviewing information handling processes within the ombudsman's office. The ombudsman's office stated that the theft likely occurred around ten years ago and they are implementing a review by the Centre for the Protection of National Infrastructure to ensure current information handling systems are secure.

Remy and Paul Hoggard are using 80 tonnes of sand to create a sculpture of King John at Runnymede in 1215 for the Festival800 anniversary event. The 4m tall and 9m wide artwork is part of a series of events celebrating democracy and freedom. Mr. Hoggard, who with his Dutch wife has created sand sculptures worldwide, considers this one of his most ambitious projects due to its size. Originally from Beverley, East Yorkshire, Mr. Hoggard now resides on a farm in Bulgaria. Magna Carta, a peace treaty between King John and rebellious barons, established principles of freedom under the law in 1215. Only three of its 63 clauses remain in law today: one protecting the English Church's rights, another concerning the City of London's privileges, and a third establishing the right to trial by jury. One of the four surviving copies of Magna Carta is on display at Lincoln Castle. Festival 800, running from August 28th to September 6th across Lincoln, features performances by artists like Billy Bragg and the Levellers, along with comedy, poetry, street theatre, lectures, and debates.
The Welsh government plans to introduce a "duty of quality" for health boards and replace community health councils with more powerful patient advocacy groups. Health Secretary Vaughan Gething stated that these changes would ensure that health and social care services are prepared for the future. A three-month consultation period will follow the publication of the plans, outlined in a White Paper jointly released by Mr. Gething and social services minister Rebecca Evans. These plans respond to criticism from the OECD, an influential international organization, which found that health boards and social care services in Wales were not effectively collaborating. The OECD also recommended improvements to the inspection and regulation system within the NHS and social care in Wales. Mr. Gething expressed confidence that these proposals would make a significant difference by prioritizing patient needs in the delivery of health and care services. He believes the changes will also enable health and social care organizations to work together across different sectors to provide the highest quality care for all individuals in Wales.

Kadyrov shared a video of Senchenko's apology on Instagram, where Senchenko expresses remorse for his words, stating he was overcome by emotion. The controversy began when Kadyrov, a close ally of Russian President Vladimir Putin, labeled opposition members "enemies of the people and traitors," demanding their prosecution. Senchenko responded with a Facebook post condemning Kadyrov as a "disgrace to Russia" and urging him to leave. He also implied Kadyrov's corruption and lack of education. Kadyrov, in response to Senchenko's apology, wrote "I accept" on Instagram, adding five emojis. Despite this, Kadyrov's original inflammatory statement regarding the opposition remains unchanged on his official website, according to BBC's Sarah Rainsford reporting from Moscow. Kadyrov assumed control of Chechnya in 2007 with Kremlin backing, continuing a campaign against Islamist rebels. In exchange for loyalty to Russia, Kadyrov has been permitted to maintain his own security force and govern the southern Russian republic independently. Human rights organizations accuse Kadyrov's security forces of abuses, including torture and extrajudicial killings.

A few minutes after launching a Japanese satellite into orbit, the Falcon-9 booster landed on a drone ship off the Florida coast. This marks SpaceX's second successful at-sea touchdown, following a similar achievement last month. This landing was particularly impressive because the rocket was traveling at a higher speed due to the mission's requirements. SpaceX officials have stated that recovering boosters used for geostationary satellite launches is more challenging due to the increased velocity needed to reach those orbits. Elon Musk, SpaceX CEO, humorously tweeted about the landing, suggesting a need for a larger rocket storage hangar. The primary objective of the Falcon launch was to place the JCSAT-14 satellite into orbit. This satellite will provide telecommunications services for the Asia-Pacific region for Sky Perfect JSAT Corporation. The launch took place at 01:21 local time (05:20 GMT) from Cape Canaveral Air Force Station in Florida. The satellite separated from the Falcon's upper stage, which is not recovered, 32 minutes after liftoff. The mission was postponed by a day due to unfavorable weather conditions over the Cape. SpaceX has now successfully returned three boosters to Earth. Their first successful landing occurred in December, when a stage landed on solid ground near the Florida launch site. However, mastering ocean landings on specialized barges is a key priority for SpaceX, as many of their missions will require this type of return. Sending satellites to geostationary transfer orbit demands significant performance from the Falcon-9 rocket, and this energy must be dissipated before landing. It's not just the increased speed of the booster that needs to be managed, but also the additional stress and heat on its components. SpaceX's goal is clear: by recovering, refurbishing, and re-flying rockets, they aim to offer their customers more affordable launch services.

Ali Qazimaj, 42, was detained in Luxembourg in connection with the deaths of Sylvia and Peter Stuart. Mr. Stuart, 75, was found dead near his home in Weybread, Suffolk, on June 3rd after being stabbed. Mrs. Stuart, 69, has not been found. Police believe her disappearance is a murder. The European Commission investigated whether major technology companies were fulfilling their commitment to remove hate speech within 24 hours of reporting. Their findings revealed that only 40% of hate speech reports were removed within a day. This pledge was made in May when the companies agreed to a "code of conduct" facilitated by the Commission. Vera Jourova, the Justice Commissioner, emphasized the importance of social media companies taking responsibility for online issues like radicalization, hate speech, and fake news. The research analyzed 600 hate speech incidents reported to tech companies during a six-week period in October and November. Campaign groups and NGOs monitoring extremist movements across Europe used social media platforms' reporting systems to submit these incidents and track the companies' response times. Of the 600 reports, 270 were submitted to Facebook, 163 to Twitter, 123 to YouTube, and none to Microsoft. The remaining seven reports were made to social media groups that hadn't signed the May pledge. The Commission stated that the 600 reports represented a sample of racist, sexist, and misogynistic content and didn't encompass all hate speech on the platforms during the study period. Approximately 20% of the flagged messages were anti-Muslim, and 23% were anti-Semitic. Tech companies removed the flagged content in 169 cases. YouTube removed 48.5%, Facebook 28.3%, and Twitter 19.1%. The research found that reports were reviewed within 24 hours in 40% of cases and within 48 hours in 43% of cases. Ms. Jourova stressed the need to protect Europeans from online hate and violence, acknowledging that while tech companies are progressing, they must do more to effectively address the issue.
Attendees at Winchester's Boomtown Fair experienced issues with long queues, leading to complaints of sunstroke, dehydration, fainting, and a lack of toilets and water. Organizers attributed the delays to increased security searches and adverse weather conditions that hampered the opening of the site gates. However, many attendees expressed their discontent on social media, characterizing the situation as dangerous. Some individuals, like Charlie Hadley, highlighted the plight of vulnerable attendees, while Richard Evans voiced concerns about safety due to fainting incidents. Tommy Palmer echoed these concerns, emphasizing the dangers posed by the combination of excessive heat and limited water access. Despite these challenges, the festival, featuring headliners such as The Specials, M.I.A., and Cypress Hill, boasts a reputation as a highly immersive and theatrical experience. Organizers acknowledged the delays and emphasized their commitment to attendee well-being, stating that they were providing additional provisions and water to those waiting in line. They also reassured attendees that security, event management, medical, and welfare teams were on site, actively working to expedite the queue process.

Carly Fiorina is the first woman in her party to run for president, while Ben Carson is the first African American. Both are political newcomers, unlike the senators and governors they are now competing against. Frontrunners in the race include Democrat Hillary Clinton and Republican Jeb Bush, who has not yet officially declared his candidacy. Fiorina immediately targeted Clinton in her announcement on ABC's Good Morning America, stating that she lacks trustworthiness due to the recent controversy surrounding her use of a private email server. Fiorina lost her only previous political campaign, a 2010 Senate race in California, by 10%. Her most notable achievement was leading the technology company Hewlett-Packard, a position she held for six years before being ousted amid shareholder unrest. Some might suggest her book, scheduled for release the day after her announcement, indicates a greater interest in publicity than votes. However, supporters point to her experience as a Fortune 500 CEO and her unique position as the only woman seeking the Republican nomination with such business background, allowing her to criticize Clinton without facing accusations of sexism. Fiorina has received positive responses at Republican gatherings leading up to her announcement. Now, the question is whether this enthusiasm will translate into tangible support. Carson, a retired surgeon, announced his candidacy in Detroit Music Hall, using a choral rendition of Eminem's "Lose Yourself". He envisions the US as a "place of dreams" where individuals can thrive free from government restrictions. He believes it's time for people to "rise up and take the government back," stating that the political class, from both parties, will not approve of his message. While Carson has shown inexperience in media interviews, his controversial stances on issues like evolution, healthcare reform, and gay rights have not significantly harmed his standing among conservative voters. If these voters remain loyal, he could potentially disrupt other socially conservative candidates like Senator Ted Cruz and former Governor Mike Huckabee. Three other Republicans have already entered the race: Senators Ted Cruz, Rand Paul, and Marco Rubio. On the Democratic side, Clinton faces a challenge from left-wing Senator Bernie Sanders.

Honey Rose, 35, was found guilty of gross negligence manslaughter after Vincent Barker died in July 2012 due to fluid buildup in his brain. She received a two-year suspended sentence in August for missing the condition during a routine eye exam. Vincent's family expressed devastation over the appeal court's ruling. Judges acknowledged a "serious breach of duty" but stated it was a matter for regulators, not the courts. During her trial last year, Ms. Rose, a mother of three from Newham, east London, claimed she conducted all necessary tests during Vincent's eye examination at the Ipswich Boots branch on February 15, 2012. However, the jury heard she failed to notice the boy's swollen optic discs, a symptom of hydrocephalus. Vincent, known as Vinnie, died in July 2012, about five months after the eye exam. Sir Brian Leveson, presiding with two other judges at the Court of Appeal, stated the decision did not condone the negligence found by the jury. He emphasized that the serious breach of duty was a matter for her regulator, but in this case, it did not constitute the crime of gross negligence manslaughter. A statement from the Barker family after the ruling expressed their devastation and belief that the manslaughter conviction should have been upheld. They maintained that if Ms. Rose had not breached her duty of care, Vincent would still be alive. They lamented that Vinnie was "let down" by his optometrist and "failed by the legal system."
East Lindsey District Council is considering transferring ownership of the So Festival, Embassy Theatre in Skegness, and Meridian Leisure Centre in Louth to a charitable trust. Officials believe this move would enable the facilities to secure new funding sources and save the council at least £1 million over five years without significant job losses. The council has faced substantial funding cuts from central government, totalling £3.1 million over the past two years. The Meridian Leisure Centre, opened in 2010, currently requires an annual subsidy of £250,000. Its events program, including the So Festival, costs approximately £350,000 annually. Councillor Adam Grist, responsible for culture, leisure, and tourism, emphasized the need for action. He stated that maintaining the current situation was not feasible and that proactive measures were necessary to protect the services provided. In addition to grant access, charitable status would provide business rate and VAT relief. Mr. Grist explained that the council would pay the trust a management fee, which would gradually decrease over time. The council would retain ownership of the buildings, have representatives on the trust's board, and ensure employee pay and conditions are guaranteed. These proposals will be presented to the full council next month.

A £282 million relief road planned for junction 6 near the National Exhibition Centre could lead to the closure of Pairc na hEireann, the home of Warwickshire Gaelic Athletic Association. The site was developed with funds raised by the Irish community, who purchased the land in the 1950s. Treasurer Martin Grogan expressed concern that Highways England was "taking memories away". Mr Grogan, who has been involved with the club for 30 years, stated that "This is home to Gaelic sport in Britain. Full stop. There's no other complex as big as this outside Ireland. It's in a prime location and we're not going to move from here." The complex hosts hundreds of people playing hurling, camogie, and Gaelic football nearly every weekend. Highways England is considering three route options for the road. A dual carriageway link west of Bickenhill would directly impact the association's land. Highways England stated that the junction is nearing capacity and the upgrade is necessary to alleviate congestion and prepare for the arrival of a new HS2 station, which is expected to increase traffic. A spokesperson said they are reviewing responses from a public consultation and a decision has not yet been made. They added that they are in communication with the club and will keep them informed. The spokesperson emphasized that they are committed to minimizing the impact of the project on the environment, local communities, businesses, road users, and residents.
Images captured by Wildcat Haven show a protected wildcat on a large branch near Huntly in Aberdeenshire. The images were taken during a camera trap surveillance operation. Wildcat Haven stated this was the first wildcat to achieve a perfect score on a scale measuring its genetic purity compared to domestic cats. However, Scottish Wildcat Action challenged this claim, asserting that ten wildcats had been previously spotted in the area, one of which was verified as the purest. Dr. Paul O'Donoghue, Wildcat Haven's chief scientific advisor, described the footage as "feeling a bit like looking at a unicorn," emphasizing the rarity and mythical status often attributed to the animal. A Scottish Wildcat Action spokesperson countered that the wildcat in the video closely resembles one they had already identified.
The Cormorant, previously known as AirMule, is a drone developed over 15 years for rescue and military purposes. It costs $14 million and can carry 500kg at a speed of 185km/h. Urban Aeronautics claims its internal rotors make it safer than a helicopter, and it can take off and land vertically, fly between buildings, and operate under power lines. The company's CEO, Rafi Yoeli, believes this flight marks progress towards commercializing the technology. Commercial drones are gaining popularity, with Amazon's Prime Air delivery service and Ehang's passenger drone, 184, receiving approval for test flights in Nevada. Dr. Mirko Kovac, director of the Aerial Robotics Lab at Imperial College London, sees potential for personal drones in various fields, including transportation. He acknowledges that technological advancements are needed for safety and ease of use, but believes successful implementation could revolutionize urban travel. Drone expert Ravi Vaidyanathan from Imperial's mechanical engineering department considers the recent flight a significant step in low-altitude navigation. He supports the initial focus on humanitarian rescue and military applications, emphasizing the importance of establishing a safety record in a niche market. While civilian drone use is likely to follow, addressing challenges like infrastructure and congestion, the timeline remains uncertain. Vaidyanathan highlights regulatory hurdles concerning takeoff and landing locations, as well as flight altitude.
Dow Corning workers in Barry have been informed about potential job losses due to company restructuring. A 45-day consultation period will soon begin, affecting the 630 employees at the plant. Despite the job cuts, Dow Chemical Company emphasized that the decision is unrelated to Brexit. The company announced a global workforce reduction of 2,500 jobs, approximately 4% of its total workforce. Dow Chemical leadership met with Barry employees to explain the rationale behind this consolidation and its potential impact on their roles. Silicone production at the Barry site began in 1952 under the name Midland Silicones. Dow Corning's website describes it as a world-leading facility, producing silicone fluids, rubber, and specialty polymers used in various applications, including cosmetics, car airbags, and construction sealants.
The strike by the RMT union against Southern Rail, which began in April, is over the removal of guards from trains and their replacement with on-board supervisors. The union has also taken action against Arriva Rail North and Merseyrail over the same issue. Protests in London featured banners demanding safety and the retention of guards. Talks between the RMT and Govia Thameslink Railway, Southern's parent company, were adjourned on Tuesday but are expected to resume next week. Southern is also in conflict with the train drivers' union Aslef. A 24-hour strike on Arriva Rail North is scheduled for Friday, coinciding with the Tour de Yorkshire cycle race. Negotiations between Merseyrail and the RMT also failed, raising concerns about further strikes. RMT general secretary Mick Cash blames the government for the disputes, claiming it restricts companies' staffing decisions on the railways.
George Osborne stated that 98% of homeowners in England and Wales will experience lower taxes after the changes compared to the current system. Only those purchasing homes exceeding £937,000 in value will face increased tax. He described the existing stamp duty as a "badly-designed tax on aspiration" and announced changes to its structure. Currently, stamp duty, applied throughout the UK, charges progressively higher rates on the entire purchase price, leading to criticism as a "slab tax." This structure results in abrupt increases in stamp duty when a property price surpasses a specific threshold. For example, a £250,000 purchase incurs a 1% stamp duty of £2,500. However, a £1 increase in price would result in a £5,000 additional charge due to the application of a 3% rate on the entire purchase price. Starting on December 3rd, the new stamp duty rates will apply only to the portion of the purchase price falling within a specific duty band, resembling an income tax structure. Consequently, a £200,000 purchase would see no tax on the first £125,000, with a 2% charge on the remaining £75,000, totaling £1,500. Previously, a 1% rate on the total purchase price would have resulted in a £2,000 bill. Although percentage rates appear higher in some instances, the overall charge will generally be lower. The new rates will save the average homebuyer in England and Wales approximately £4,500 in stamp duty. The new system will also eliminate the sudden jumps in existing stamp duty thresholds. From April 1st, 2015, stamp duty in Scotland will be replaced by Land and Buildings Transaction Tax (LBTT). Until then, the new stamp duty rates will apply in Scotland as well. Similar to LBTT in Scotland, the new system in England and Wales will only charge higher rates on the portion of the property price falling within a specific band. Under Scotland's LBTT, buyers purchasing homes priced below £324,280 will pay less than under the previous stamp duty system, while those spending more will face higher taxes. The government has developed a stamp duty calculator to assist buyers, and several examples are provided. LBTT rates in Scotland will be lower than the equivalent stamp duty rates in
Police arrested two men in Sydney suburbs on Wednesday morning. Authorities allege the men conspired to attack several locations, including a naval base in Sydney Harbour. NSW Police Deputy Commissioner Catherine Burn stated that there was no indication of an imminent attack. The arrests stemmed from documents found during raids in December 2014 as part of Operation Appleby. These arrests are connected to five others made in December of this year, according to Ms. Burn. She explained that a group had formed with the intention to carry out a terrorist attack and began making preparations. The Wolloomooloo Navy base, along with the Australian Federal Police and NSW Police offices, are believed to have been potential targets. Australian media identified the men as Abdullah Salihy, 24, and Mohammed Almouie, 20. Both men's families' homes were previously searched during Operation Appleby, according to Australian media reports. Operation Appleby is an ongoing investigation targeting a group of young men suspected of planning a terrorist attack in Australia. Thirteen individuals have been arrested in connection with the operation to date.
An IPC taskforce has recommended that Russia's suspension from Paralympic events, which began in August, remain in effect. This follows Russia's ban from the 2016 Rio Paralympics due to systematic doping revelations. The taskforce expressed concern about Russia's lack of progress ahead of the 2018 Winter Olympics. IPC president Sir Philip Craven stated that Russian athletes may still be banned from next year's Games. Russia's suspension stemmed from a report by World Anti-Doping Agency investigator Richard McLaren detailing widespread drug use and alleged government cover-ups. However, according to the IPC taskforce report, Russian officials have not acknowledged or refuted McLaren's findings. The taskforce, working with the Russian Paralympic Committee, believes meaningful cultural change cannot occur until the issues leading to the suspension are fully addressed. Taskforce chairman Andy Parkinson, the chief executive of British Rowing, noted some positive developments, including efforts to establish a short-term testing program. Earlier this month, the IAAF extended Russia's suspension from international athletics competition.
Abubakar Shekau rejected the Islamic State's (IS) declaration that Abu Musab al-Barnawi was the new leader, accusing al-Barnawi of attempting a coup. Boko Haram, which aims to overthrow Nigeria's government and establish an Islamic state in the north, has lost most of its territory in the past 18 months due to offensives by Nigerian and regional forces. Shekau last communicated in an August audio message, claiming he was alive and hadn't been replaced, a claim echoed in an April IS video. In a recent 10-minute audio message, Shekau distanced Boko Haram from IS while still referring to its leader, Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi, as "caliph." He alleged that individuals within Boko Haram prevented him from communicating with al-Baghdadi, manipulating his letters to the IS leadership for personal gain. He claimed to have sent eight letters to IS leaders, which were ignored, only to later learn of his purported replacement. He labeled al-Barnawi and his followers as polytheists. This split, the most significant in Boko Haram's history, reveals deep divisions that could lead to clashes between loyalists of the two leaders. It also signifies the group's weakness, potentially foreshadowing its collapse. Military officials view the split as a sign of Boko Haram's demise, while some security analysts warn that internal strife could make the group more dangerous and unpredictable. Nigeria and its regional allies aim to exploit this factionalism. Shekau had previously been accused of hoarding supplies and expelling IS military advisors. This split is expected to significantly impact Boko Haram's operations and could mark a turning point in the fight against the insurgents. Shekau assumed leadership after the group's founder, Muhammad Yusuf, died in Nigerian custody in 2009. Under his leadership, Boko Haram intensified its radicalism, violence, and pledged allegiance to IS in 2015. Shekau frequently taunted Nigerian authorities in videos, glorifying the group's violence, including the abduction of the Chibok schoolgirls in 2014. Nigeria's army has repeatedly claimed to have killed him, and he hasn't appeared in a video since Boko Haram aligned with IS.

Wenger expressed frustration over the lack of preparation time for players returning from the tournament. Liverpool, on the other hand, secured a victory despite conceding two goals in the latter stages. Theo Walcott initially put Arsenal ahead after missing a penalty, but Liverpool responded with goals from Philippe Coutinho, Adam Lallana, and new signing Sadio Mane. Jurgen Klopp, Liverpool's manager, acknowledged his team's lapse in concentration during the final half-hour, taking responsibility for his exuberant celebration of Mane's goal, which he deemed a mistake. Arsenal's next fixture is against defending champions Leicester City next Saturday.

The St James Theatre, located in Palace Street, Victoria, was constructed on the land previously occupied by the Westminster Theatre, which burned down in 2002. The owners anticipate that the £7 million complex will draw in new audiences and encourage the growth of commercial theatre beyond the West End. Sandi Toksvig's play, "Bully Boy," will open the theatre's inaugural season. Originally a chapel in 1766, the building was modified to serve as a cinema in 1924 and later transformed into a theatre in 1931. For an extended period, it was owned by the Moral Re-Armament movement, which utilized it to present performances with moral or spiritual themes. The new complex features a 312-seat main auditorium and a studio space with a capacity of up to 150 people, along with apartments. The theatre will be equipped with multimedia broadcast facilities to transmit performances on its dedicated BSkyB satellite channel.
Annuity rates have decreased by 3.5% since June 24th, according to Tom McPhail, head of retirement policy at Hargreaves Lansdown. Prior to the vote, a 65-year-old with £100,000 in savings could purchase an annual income of £5,069. This has now fallen to £4,890, a record low. Experts anticipated this decline before the referendum, despite a majority of voters over 60 supporting leaving the European Union. McPhail stated that annuity rates are at historic lows, noting that just six months ago a 60-year-old could secure a better deal than what is currently offered to a 65-year-old. While rates are at record lows, there is no guarantee they will rise. Annuity rates have been declining for several years due to increasing life expectancy. The recent drop has been further accelerated by falling bond yields since the vote. Despite this, McPhail advises individuals considering purchasing an annuity not to delay due to current low rates. He emphasizes that waiting for a potential increase in rates is not advisable.

The authenticity of a projectile launch believed to be a submarine-launched ballistic missile (SLBM) by North Korea around 6:30 pm (0930 GMT) on Wednesday near the port of Sinpo is unclear. South Korea's defense ministry confirmed the launch and stated they are closely monitoring the situation while maintaining a full defense posture. North Korea has not yet acknowledged the test in its official media. Previous claims by North Korea of conducting similar tests have been met with skepticism. While the secretive state asserts it has tested SLBMs before, the US disputes these claims, alleging that photographs from a purported December launch were manipulated. Others believe North Korea has fired missiles from submerged platforms but not submarines. South Korea's Joint Chiefs of Staff reported the missile traveled approximately 30km (19 miles), significantly shorter than the typical range of at least 300km (186 miles) for an SLBM. This launch follows North Korea's four previous nuclear tests, the most recent being in January 2017. The UN Security Council responded to this test with its strongest sanctions to date, targeting North Korea's nuclear weapons program. Last month, North Korea announced the development of nuclear warheads small enough to fit on ballistic missiles, although experts have expressed doubt about these claims. Analysts suggest that this potential test could be a display of strength by North Korea ahead of the Workers' Party Congress, the first since 1980.
Protests erupted in Siyazan, Azerbaijan, resulting in at least one hospitalization and several injuries. Police responded with tear gas and rubber bullets to disperse the demonstrators, who were protesting worsening economic conditions caused by the decline in oil prices. Azerbaijan's economy relies heavily on oil, which accounted for nearly half its GDP in 2014. Oil prices have plummeted by 70% over the past 15 months, reaching $31 per barrel on international markets. This has led to a significant devaluation of Azerbaijan's currency, the manat. The interior ministry attributed the protests to opposition groups and religious extremists. In response to the crisis, the government announced a reduction in the price of flour, effective Friday. Additionally, VAT will be waived on wheat imports and the sale of bread and flour. To support the weakening manat, the central bank has prohibited the sale of foreign exchange in bureaux de change operated by commercial banks. Azerbaijan's economy, heavily dependent on oil revenue, has been severely impacted by the falling oil prices. Small businesses have struggled in recent years due to alleged bribery demands from officials. Corruption has been a major obstacle for businesses in Azerbaijan, according to the International Monetary Fund. The manat, which was almost equal to the euro at the beginning of last year, was devalued against the dollar in February 2015. In December 2015, the Central Bank unpegged the manat from the dollar, resulting in an immediate price surge that disproportionately affected low-income families and those with large bank loans. Azerbaijan relies heavily on imports for even basic consumer goods, and while some agricultural products are produced domestically, raw materials are sourced from abroad. Despite repeated warnings from international and local analysts about the dangers of oil dependency, the Azerbaijani government has taken limited steps to diversify the economy. In a country with strict media control, dissenting voices are met with force. While discontent was relatively low during the oil boom, the volatile currency now means that price increases impact nearly everyone. As the number of people affected grows, it may become increasingly difficult for the government to appease the broader public.
Just before midnight, a message from space will be shown on screens at Edinburgh's Hogmanay. The event is collaborating with the Edinburgh International Science Festival, the UK Space Agency, and the European Space Agency. Over 80 countries are represented at the street party. Pete Irvine, director of Edinburgh's Hogmanay, stated, "Edinburgh's Hogmanay is a truly global event with attendees from over 80 countries around the world. This year, we're taking it a step further with a visit from space." He continued, "Astronaut Tim Peake, currently aboard the International Space Station, will deliver a special message to Edinburgh's Hogmanay, helping us welcome in 2016. Revellers throughout the city center should watch the event and stage screens just before midnight." Dr. Simon Gage, director of the Edinburgh International Science Festival, said, "While revellers enjoy the Edinburgh's Hogmanay street party, the International Space Station, traveling at five miles per second, will orbit the Earth three times. We might even be lucky enough to see it pass overhead. With UK astronaut Tim Peake onboard, 2016 will be a remarkable year for UK space science, providing much for us all to follow and be inspired by. We're thrilled to have extended the invitation to Tim and, with the UK Space Agency and ESA, bring a touch of science to this fantastic celebration." Edinburgh's Hogmanay is produced by Unique Events on behalf of Edinburgh City Council.
Ciaran McClean, a member of the Green Party, claims the agreement between the Conservative government and the DUP violates the Good Friday Agreement and the Bribery Act. The DUP will support the Conservative government in crucial votes in exchange for funding for Northern Ireland. A former government lawyer considers the bribery accusation unfounded. The government asserts that the confidence and supply agreement is legal. McClean has launched a crowdfunding campaign to finance a judicial review. His solicitor, David Greene, states that an application for a judicial review will be submitted on Monday or Tuesday. On his crowdfunding page, McClean, who ran unsuccessfully for the Green Party in West Tyrone in June's election, expresses concern that the government's deal with the DUP threatens hard-won peace. He believes the Conservatives are being propped up by the DUP to maintain power after the election, calling it straight bribery – money for votes. McClean argues that the agreement contradicts the Good Friday Agreement, which mandates the government to exercise its power impartially on behalf of all people. He is pursuing the legal challenge as an individual, not on behalf of the Green Party. McClean's solicitor is a senior partner at Edwin Coe solicitors, who previously represented a claimant in the successful Brexit challenge at the Supreme Court. Greene states that there has been public outcry over the Conservative-DUP deal, emphasizing that it's about the rule of law and a citizen's right to challenge what seems wrong. Alberto Costa, a former government lawyer and current MP for South Leicestershire, maintains that the investment in Northern Ireland is not a personal inducement and that Prime Minister Theresa May has a constitutional duty to form a government. He asserts that the deal is transparent and lawful, dismissing the bribery claim as vexatious and baseless. Under the confidence and supply arrangement, the DUP guarantees its 10 MPs will vote with the government on the Queen's Speech, the Budget, Brexit-related legislation, and national security matters. In return, Northern Ireland will receive an additional £1 billion over the next two years. While rival parties in Northern Ireland largely welcome the funding, concerns exist that the deal could undermine the peace process and devolution negotiations, as the UK government relies on the DUP's support. The deal also faced widespread criticism from UK opposition parties, with Labour labeling it "shabby and reckless," and Welsh First Minister Carwyn Jones calling it a "straight bung" that undermines fair funding.

A study found that taller individuals have a slightly elevated risk of developing certain cancers, including breast and skin cancer. The research, which tracked a large group of Swedish adults for over 50 years, indicated that for every additional 10 centimeters (4 inches) in height, the risk of cancer increased by 18% in women and 11% in men. While these findings align with previous studies that have suggested a link between height and cancer risk, experts emphasize that the study did not consider numerous other risk factors. They advise against undue worry for taller individuals. Scientists propose that height might serve as a marker for other factors related to childhood growth that influence cancer development. These factors could include increased growth factors, a higher number of cells (leading to a greater chance of cellular mutations), and potentially increased food intake. However, it's important to note that height itself is not considered a direct cause of cancer. Experts stress that regardless of height, adopting healthy lifestyle choices, such as not smoking, limiting alcohol consumption, maintaining a healthy weight, and practicing safe sun exposure, can significantly reduce cancer risk.

Despite objections from SDLP and UUP MPs, a bill concerning welfare reform was quickly passed by the House of Commons on Monday. It is expected to be finalized in the House of Lords on Tuesday. MLAs voted last week to transfer control of the welfare system to Westminster following a new agreement between the DUP and Sinn Féin, Northern Ireland's two largest parties, along with the British and Irish governments. This agreement includes a Â£585 million, four-year package to mitigate the impact of welfare reform and tax credit cuts. Northern Ireland Secretary Theresa Villiers stated that the lack of progress on welfare reforms had been harming the political process in Northern Ireland. She asserted that the new benefits system in Northern Ireland, with top-ups funded by Northern Ireland's executive, will be the most generous in the UK. Labour's shadow Northern Ireland secretary, Vernon Coaker, announced that his party would support the bill despite their opposition to much of the government's welfare reforms. Attempts by the SDLP to amend the bill were unsuccessful. Mark Durkan, an SDLP MP, criticized the government for using a "kangaroo parliamentary procedure" to limit debate and expedite the bill's passage. He argued that more time was needed for deliberation, expressing concern that the SDLP and other parties were being silenced. However, DUP MP Sammy Wilson emphasized the urgency of the matter, stating that it had already taken too long to reach an agreement. Danny Kinahan of the UUP called the legislation "most extraordinary," claiming it was even less favorable than the original proposals discussed at Stormont House.

Ryszard Masierak, 31, faces eight counts of causing death by dangerous driving and four counts of causing serious injury by dangerous driving. He is also charged with causing death by careless driving while over the legal alcohol limit. David Wagstaff, 53, the driver of the second lorry, is also facing charges of eight counts of causing death by dangerous driving and four counts of causing serious injury by dangerous driving. Wagstaff is scheduled to appear in court on September 11th. The crash occurred on the southbound carriageway of the M1 near junction 14 at 03:15 BST. A five-year-old girl, a man, and a woman sustained life-threatening injuries and remain hospitalized. Another individual was admitted to the hospital with less severe injuries. Cyriac Joseph, the minibus driver and owner of Nottingham-based ABC Travels, was killed in the crash. He was transporting a group from India to London for a European tour. Joseph has been remembered as an "extraordinary father" and a "great leader" within the south Indian community in Nottingham. Three other fatalities were identified as employees of IT company Wipro. The company confirmed that Karthikeyan Ramasubramaniyam Pugalur, Rishi Rajeev Kumar, and Vivek Bhaskaran died in the crash, while another employee is critically injured.

A contest, run in partnership with the Scottish Crofting Federation and the Crofting Commission, aimed to explore the significance of crofting life in 2014. Heather Gray from Shetland won first prize with her photograph "Hentin Totties," depicting a multigenerational family working the land. Gray stated that her main inspiration was family, highlighting the joy of seeing extended family members, from grandparents to toddlers, collaborate on the annual crop. Ria Macdonald received second prize for "Checking the Sheep with Grandad," a picture taken on the Isle of Lewis showing Cormac Sandison and his dog Mac inspecting pregnant ewes on his grandfather's croft. Fiona MacKay's photograph of haymaking on Lewis, titled "Summer Bales," secured third place. John King, a judge from RoS, remarked on the high quality of the numerous photographs submitted by people of all ages from across Scotland's crofting regions. He emphasized that the images conveyed the passion crofters have for their work and the enduring importance of family in this traditional way of life. "Hentin Totties" was chosen for its portrayal of everyone actively participating and enjoying the experience.
A data breach affecting 51 UPS franchises across 24 states exposed client information including names, addresses, email addresses, and payment card details. The company stated that the malware responsible had been removed and services were secure. Separately, a large US hospital chain, Community Health Systems, reported a breach impacting 4.5 million patients. Last week, the grocery chain SuperValu also disclosed a breach. UPS, founded in 1907 and now a multibillion-dollar corporation, has over 4,450 franchised locations in the US, each independently owned and responsible for its own network security. The breaches, discovered through a US government notification, occurred between January and August. While UPS reported no instances of stolen data being used fraudulently, they advised customers to monitor their accounts for suspicious activity. Tim Davis, president of The UPS Store, urged vigilance in reviewing account statements and credit reports. He assured customers that the incident had been contained and an internal review was underway.

Students in Northern Ireland currently receive maintenance loan and grant payments three times a year, at the beginning of each academic term. Student leaders believe that monthly payments would improve students' financial stability. The Department of Education (DEL) estimates that implementing this change would cost between £250,000 and £350,000. Most students in Northern Ireland rely on loans to cover living expenses, but many also receive additional support grants. Unlike loans, grants are means-tested and do not require repayment. During the 2013/14 academic year, over 42,000 students borrowed a total of £131 million in maintenance loans. Nearly 25,000 students, approximately 60%, also received support grants, costing DEL over £68 million. The grant amount depends on household income; students from households with incomes of £19,203 or less are eligible for the maximum grant of £3,475 annually. Students from households with incomes between £19,203 and £41,605 receive a partial grant. In 2013/14, 38% of Northern Irish students received the full grant, while 22% received a partial grant. Both maintenance loans and support grants are typically paid in September, January, and April, similar to the current system in Northern Ireland. However, Scotland operates a monthly payment system, with a larger initial payment in September followed by smaller monthly payments from October to June. Fergal McFerran, President of NUS-USI, believes that monthly payments would benefit students, allowing them to cover expenses like accommodation deposits, household items, and study materials at the start of the academic year. There is no indication that the level of maintenance loans or support grants will increase. DEL Minister Stephen Farry stated that while there is limited flexibility in the current financial climate regarding overall student support levels, there is room to consider the timing and distribution of that support. The consultation on this matter closes on Sunday, September 27th. Tuition fee loans, currently set at £3,805 per year for a Northern Irish student studying at Queen's or Ulster University, are paid directly to the university and would not be affected by any changes.

A spokesperson for Mr Joyce stated that the Prime Minister has instructed frontbenchers to avoid appearing on Q&A. The Q&A program has sparked debate in Australia regarding national security and freedom of speech. The network is currently reviewing the decision. Mr Ciobo, a panel guest, responded that he was pleased to be part of a government that would take such action. High-profile politicians frequently participate. Mr Joyce initially intended to appear on Q&A but was subsequently informed not to. Speaking at a National Press Club address, Mr Joyce stated that while there was no explicit threat from Mr Abbott's office, he would have appreciated more notice. Following Mallah's appearance, Mr Abbott expressed his displeasure, stating that "heads should roll" and questioning the ABC's loyalty. Mallah, a self-described media critic and Muslim activist, has been interviewed by various Australian and international media outlets, including the BBC. However, his recent social media posts, which have included derogatory and sexist remarks towards female journalists, have drawn criticism. A government review conducted by the Department of Communications found that while the producers were aware of Mallah's criminal convictions, they were unaware of his recent offensive social media posts when he was selected to participate.

Although Joyce won the last round according to two judges, it wasn't enough to overcome the deficit. North Wales and Mid and West Wales fire services did not provide figures but stated they have a zero-tolerance policy towards attacks. A Mid and West Wales Fire and Rescue spokesman stated their service has a zero-tolerance approach to attacks on firefighters, promising that those responsible will be reported to and dealt with by the police.

With the German labor market now open to workers from Poland and seven other EU nations that joined in 2004, concerns are rising. Germany delayed opening its economy to these countries until the last minute, and ministers are assuring German workers that their jobs will be protected from cheaper labor. Labor Minister Ursula von der Leyen predicts 100,000 people will migrate annually and has announced increased checks in industries like construction and catering, where unions are worried about wage cuts from immigrants. She states that inspectors will ensure minimum wages are met and that new arrivals are registered. Finance Minister Wolfgang Schaeuble adds that his ministry will deploy 150 extra officials to monitor industries for illegal workers. In addition to Poland, workers from the Czech Republic, Hungary, Slovenia, Slovakia, Estonia, Lithuania, and Latvia are eligible for German jobs. Ministers chose to communicate their promises through popular newspapers, potentially reflecting public anxiety among Germans. While employers claim the economy needs skilled workers from abroad, unions fear that companies also desire unskilled workers at lower wages.

The UK government has leased 265,976 sq ft of space in Cardiff from 2020, in what developers claim is the largest office deal in Wales. Construction on the former bus station site began in 2014, with BBC Wales scheduled to relocate there in 2019. HM Revenue and Customs (HMRC) will move from their Llanishen office, and the hub will become one of 13 regional tax offices in the UK. Other government departments, including the Office for the Secretary of State for Wales, will also move to the site, which is located next to Cardiff Central railway station. Existing businesses at the site include Hugh James solicitors, Hodge Bank, and Motonovo Finance. Cardiff council leader Huw Thomas considers the announcement a significant step in the city's development, alongside plans for a new bus interchange and redevelopment of the nearby Brains Brewery site. He believes Cardiff is competing with major UK cities to attract jobs and investment. The UK government stated the deal is part of a plan to reduce its buildings from 800 to 200 by 2022. Secretary of State for Wales Alun Cairns emphasized the move demonstrates the government's commitment to being visible, accessible, and improving people's lives. HMRC chief executive Jon Thompson confirmed they will collaborate with Cardiff council to redevelop their current Ty Glas base, which houses 2,800 staff, following the relocation.

Researchers at Imperial College London studied national health survey data from 1994 to 2011, finding that more people were receiving treatment for high blood pressure and experiencing better outcomes. Their findings, published in the Lancet, indicated that the number of individuals with high blood pressure receiving treatment nearly doubled, from 32% in 1994 to 58% in 2011. Simultaneously, the proportion of people achieving normal blood pressure levels tripled, from 11% to 37%. Professor Neil Poulter, a researcher involved in the study, estimated that treating individuals in 2011's manner throughout the 1994 period could have prevented up to 100,000 major cardiovascular events, such as heart attacks, strokes, and deaths. He expressed optimism about the progress, stating that a significant number of lives have been saved. The study projects that 80% of patients will manage their blood pressure by 2022 if the current trend continues. Improved medications, increased public awareness, and financial incentives for doctors are believed to contribute to these advancements. Despite these improvements, heart disease remains the leading cause of death in the UK, accounting for 82,000 deaths annually.

In Cuba, private ownership of broadcast media is not permitted, and all major media outlets are government-owned. Reporters Without Borders (RSF) states that Cuba is the only country in the Americas without a non-state, independent press. Official media primarily function to disseminate propaganda for the regime. The US actively attempts to reach Cuban audiences through Radio-TV Marti, a Washington-backed station that claims to provide balanced and uncensored news. While internet usage reached 3.1 million users by 2014, according to Internetlivestats.com, access remains heavily restricted by the Cuban government, making it difficult for most citizens to connect from their homes. Freedom House, a US-based NGO, reports that bloggers who support dissidents have faced harassment and detention. RSF designates Cuba as an "Enemy of the Internet," noting that the government can no longer attribute internet connectivity issues to the US embargo since a submarine cable connecting Cuba to Venezuela became operational.

The Conservative party criticized Labour for failing to fulfill a 2011 promise of annual health checks by GPs for individuals over 50. Darren Millar, a Conservative Assembly Member, called the Add To Your Life self-assessment program a "complete folly" and suggested it be scrapped. The Welsh Government stated that lessons learned from the program would be incorporated into a review of health advice. Launched in April 2014, the survey, accessible via computer, phone, and tablet, aimed to provide individuals with an overall view of their health and well-being and suggest healthier lifestyle choices. According to Conservative analysis of written questions, only 12,939 people completed the survey in the following 23 months out of Wales' population of 1,177,000 individuals over 50. Millar, who previously served as shadow health minister, argued that the low participation rate reflects public dissatisfaction with Labour's broken promise of annual GP health checks for all Welsh residents over 50. He claimed that many older people lack the digital skills needed to use the service, and Angela Burns, the current Conservative health spokesperson, highlighted poor phone and internet connectivity in rural areas. Burns emphasized that individuals over 50 are susceptible to various health issues, including cancer, high blood pressure, diabetes, and arthritis. She asserted that the program has been a failure and that future funding should be redirected towards recruiting and retaining GPs. A Welsh Government spokesperson defended the program as an "innovative approach," noting that while startup costs were relatively high, operating costs were only £70,000 in 2015-16. The spokesperson stated that other individuals are benefiting from the program through charity and voluntary organizations that utilize it as a basis for group discussions on health and lifestyle. The spokesperson added that evidence suggests providing tailored health information to the public is more effective than other methods. Public Health Wales is reviewing its approach to providing public health information with the goal of better supporting individuals in taking control of their own health and well-being. The spokesperson concluded that lessons learned from Add to Your Life are a crucial component of this review.

A report analyzing women in England and Wales found that approximately 7% of women born in 1995 had a child before age 20. The Family Planning Association attributed this decline to teenagers' ability to make positive choices, while others suggested it resulted from contraceptive pressure. Statistics from the report demonstrate a gradual decrease in teenage motherhood rates, now comparable to levels seen in the early 1920s generation. This 7% figure contrasts with a peak of 20% for women born in 1952. This news follows a decline in teenage pregnancy rates, which reached their lowest point since record-keeping began earlier this year. The latest data, however, differs from teenage pregnancy rates, which include abortions. The report also examined family size across generations, revealing that women born in 1970 averaged 1.91 children by age 45, slightly lower than their mothers (born in 1943), who averaged 2.24 children by that age. The ONS noted this doesn't necessarily indicate fewer children but suggests a trend of later childbirth. Paul Casey, from the Family Planning Association, viewed the figures as evidence of teenagers' access to contraception, support services, and confidence in their use. He highlighted the success of the 10-year Teenage Pregnancy Strategy and urged continued effort. Casey also acknowledged the need for consistent, high-quality sex and relationships education and expressed concern about the impact of recent public health spending cuts on sexual and reproductive health services. Dr. Ellie Lee, Director of the Centre for Parenting Culture Studies at the University of Kent, offered a more cautious perspective. She pointed out that teenage births were already at historically low levels before the Teenage Pregnancy Strategy and that the downward trend had been ongoing. Lee questioned the strategy's emphasis on long-term contraceptives, arguing that teenagers should have the freedom to make informed choices. She also addressed the negative societal attitudes towards teenage mothers, emphasizing that there's no reason to believe they are any less capable than mothers of other ages.
The Metropolitan Police have up to 18 ongoing investigations into allegations spanning decades. However, Deputy Assistant Commissioner Steve Rodhouse stated that a lack of victims coming forward is hindering the investigations. This appeal comes as the Met faces accusations of covering up sex offenses involving high-profile individuals. The Independent Police Complaints Commission (IPCC) is currently investigating 14 separate referrals dating back four decades. Deputy Assistant Commissioner Rodhouse, head of Crime and Operations at the Met, told BBC Radio 4 that while they believe progress is being made in the wider inquiries, the lack of victims coming forward is a challenge. He urged anyone who has been a victim of past sexual abuse to come forward. Despite the IPCC's investigation into alleged cover-ups, Mr. Rodhouse expressed confidence in the Met's record of cooperating with internal investigations, noting that the Met itself referred the matter to the IPCC. He emphasized that the investigation will follow the evidence wherever it leads, without bias. The IPCC has characterized the cover-up allegations as "high-level corruption of the most serious nature," including claims of suppressing evidence, obstructing investigations, and concealing offenses due to the involvement of politicians and police officers. One recent Met Police investigation, Operation Midland, launched in November, is examining allegations of abuse of boys by a group of influential individuals from politics, the military, and law enforcement agencies in southern England and London during the 1970s and 1980s. The investigation also includes claims of three boys being murdered, potentially transforming the abuse inquiry into a murder investigation. Operation Midland has focused on the Dolphin Square estate in Pimlico, near the Houses of Parliament. Mr. Rodhouse revealed that the Met has been working with a young man for some time whom they consider a credible witness. While he declined to comment on whether any additional witnesses have come forward, he stressed that these cases can only progress if people come forward with confidence. He added that all evidence and accounts are thoroughly tested, and ultimately, the courts determine the credibility of witnesses.
A report determined that a combination of failings resulted in the deaths of 11 babies and one mother at Furness General Hospital between 2004 and 2013. Health Secretary Jeremy Hunt has requested an examination of the Nursing and Midwifery Council's (NMC) role, to be conducted by the Professional Standards Authority (PSA). The deaths were deemed preventable and occurred at a maternity unit found to be dysfunctional with substandard care provided by staff lacking in skills and knowledge. Poor working relationships existed between doctors and midwives, who referred to themselves as "the musketeers" as they advocated for normal childbirth at all costs. Hunt has previously stated that the NMC should no longer be responsible for the statutory supervision of midwives in the UK, proposing a model similar to that of other health professionals. The PSA review is expected to commence as soon as possible. Hunt emphasized the importance of the NMC in ensuring high standards of care and stated the review will provide independent assurance that lessons learned from the Morecambe Bay events have been implemented. The NMC's chief executive, Jackie Smith, welcomed the PSA's contribution in identifying areas for improvement and stated that while they cannot change the past, they are committed to learning from it to ensure different actions in future similar situations. The NMC faced criticism from the PSA in 2014 regarding its handling of disciplinary cases related to the Mid-Staffordshire NHS Trust scandal.
A group of teenagers playing a game at Trelai Park in Ely were attacked by a gang wielding knives and bats. Police were called to the scene, where they found a boy with a broken jaw who was taken to the University of Wales Hospital in Cardiff. The attack also resulted in damage to the windows of a school minibus. The gang, reportedly unconnected to either Fitzalan High School or Glyn Derw and Michaelston Federation, which the pupils attended, approached the group during their game. PE teachers from both schools intervened to protect the children. Cardiff council confirmed the incident and stated that "a number of individuals not involved in the match are believed to have assaulted some of the players." Both schools are providing support to the affected students, and a police investigation is ongoing. Three boys, aged 16 and 15, were arrested but later released on bail.
From the White House, President Obama announced his decision to reject the pipeline, stating it wouldn't benefit US national interests. The pipeline's construction had been fiercely debated for seven years, with environmentalists expressing concerns about its potential for irreversible damage. However, Obama asserted that the pipeline had become disproportionately prominent in the climate change discussion. The proposed pipeline, spanning 1,179 miles (1,897 km), was intended to transport 800,000 barrels of oil daily from Alberta, Canada, to Steele City, Nebraska. Obama maintained that the pipeline wouldn't provide a significant long-term economic boost. Republican presidential candidates criticized the decision, with Jeb Bush characterizing it as an attack on the US economy. Despite the Republican-led Congress voting in February to initiate construction, Obama vetoed the bill pending the completion of environmental reports. The decision, reached after more than seven years of deliberation, coincides with several potential factors, including the election of a Canadian government more skeptical of large energy projects, a decline in oil prices, and a drop in the US unemployment rate to 5%, the lowest point during Obama's presidency. This move is expected to be applauded by environmentalists, condemned by Republicans vying for the presidency, and largely ignored by the American public, which has shown limited engagement on the issue. Obama's decision favorably positions Democratic presidential frontrunner Hillary Clinton, who had recently expressed opposition to the pipeline. This action removes the issue from the forefront of the campaign at least for now. The decision comes as world leaders prepare to convene in Paris at the end of the month to finalize a significant global climate agreement, a key element of Obama's legacy. While the project is effectively terminated, the pipeline controversy is unlikely to subside entirely. Supporters are expected to challenge the decision in court, and if a Republican is elected president next year, construction could potentially be approved. The pipeline also generated diplomatic friction between former Canadian Prime Minister Stephen Harper and President Obama. Obama stated that he had spoken with newly elected Canadian Prime Minister Justin Trudeau, and they agreed that addressing climate change takes precedence over any disagreements regarding the pipeline.
Simon Roberts and Wayne McKenzie pleaded guilty to drug conspiracy and money laundering charges at Caernarfon Crown Court. A covert police operation in 2013, which involved bugging Roberts' business, uncovered the operation. Other gang members were also sentenced. Taxi driver David Taylor and Philip Burke acted as couriers. Taylor received a six-year and eight-month sentence, while Burke received five years and four months. Burke was found with £45,000. David Arfon Jones and Jason Maddocks-Jones each received six-year sentences. Laura Roberts, Simon Roberts' wife, was given an 18-month suspended sentence and 300 hours of unpaid work for providing banking services. She was the only defendant to plead not guilty. Lyndsey Bradley, McKenzie's partner, received a 14-month suspended sentence and 300 hours of unpaid work for a similar offense. Judge Niclas Parry stated that the case involved a criminal syndicate bringing cocaine into north Wales, particularly around Wrexham, in a sophisticated operation that generated significant profits. He emphasized the immeasurable impact on the community.
Keith Taylor, a Green MEP representing the South East, expressed concerns that a new runway could negatively impact air quality. Both Heathrow and Gatwick airports have stated their commitment to adhering to legal air quality standards. The Airports Commission, currently conducting a consultation on the issue, is expected to submit a recommendation to the government regarding airport expansion within the next few weeks. This recommendation follows a further air quality assessment ordered by the commission after the Supreme Court mandated the government to develop new plans for reducing air pollution. The commission is tasked with deciding between three shortlisted proposals: two for Heathrow and one for Gatwick. However, the Green Party, local councils near Heathrow, and the Gatwick Area Conservation Campaign have criticized the commission for underestimating the environmental consequences of airport expansion. Taylor stated that building any major new runway would significantly harm air quality, jeopardize climate change targets, and lead to unprecedented noise levels. Stewart Wingate, Gatwick's chief executive, acknowledged the paramount importance of air quality in expansion decisions. He argued that Heathrow's current breaches of legal air quality limits render a third runway at Heathrow illogical, while Gatwick's location ensures it can perpetually meet air quality standards. Matt Gorman, Heathrow's sustainability and environment director, countered Wingate's assertion, claiming that the commission's analysis confirms an expanded Heathrow will exceed local air quality limits.
Wiltshire Police Chief Constable Mike Veale stated that Operation Conifer is not a "fishing trip or witch-hunt", emphasizing that officers have received allegations against a significant number of individuals. Two people have been arrested and bailed in connection with the operation, which was launched in 2015 following claims against former Conservative Prime Minister Sir Edward Heath. Sir Edward died in 2005 at the age of 89. Mr. Veale, in an open letter, affirmed his commitment to conducting a thorough investigation despite pressure to conclude it prematurely. He acknowledged the intense scrutiny surrounding the case due to Sir Edward's prominence and influence. Mr. Veale confirmed that satanic ritual sex abuse is a part of the investigation, although he stressed it is a small aspect and unrelated to Sir Edward. He argued that investigating allegations against a former prime minister was essential, regardless of public reaction. Operation Conifer was prompted by concerns that Wiltshire Police may have mishandled a criminal prosecution against a suspected brothel keeper in 1994. These concerns stemmed from information provided by a retired officer. The Independent Police Complaints Commission (IPCC) found no evidence that the 1994 case was dropped due to threats against Sir Edward. However, Wiltshire Police stated that Operation Conifer is also investigating separate allegations made by individuals who came forward. The operation aims to protect children and vulnerable adults who may be at risk of abuse. The Sir Edward Heath Charitable Foundation maintains its confidence that Sir Edward will be cleared of any wrongdoing.

A voluntary agreement among clubs led to an earlier closing time, following controversy over the death of 20-year-old Joby Murphy, who drowned after consuming numerous cheap vodka shots. Lyndon Stephens, a music promoter, expressed confusion regarding the sudden change and its impact on people returning home. He clarified the distinction between alcohol and entertainment licenses, noting that while alcohol licenses traditionally ended at 1am, entertainment licenses previously allowed events to continue until 3am, but this has now reverted to 2am. Police enforced the new closing time by visiting clubs around 2am on Sunday. Stephens predicted negative consequences for tourism, as visitors from certain European countries typically start their nights out later. Colin Neill, representing the Pubs of Ulster, stated that their members agreed to the 2am closure voluntarily as a response to concerns about irresponsible promotions and alleged illegal activities. He emphasized the industry's commitment to self-regulation and suggested that the change reflects a societal understanding that by 2am, people are ready to leave. Neill advocated for modernizing Northern Ireland's licensing laws, expressing a desire for a system that allows some clubs to remain open later. Nightclub owners met with Environment Minister Alex Attwood last month to address what he deemed "irresponsible" drink promotions. Attwood aims to make it more challenging for pubs and clubs to obtain and retain entertainment licenses. This initiative follows the death of Joby Murphy, who drowned in the River Lagan in January after a night of consuming cheap vodka shots. His father publicly criticized the prevalence of discounted alcohol deals at the time.
Kings Heath Concorde's children's teams in Birmingham were sponsored by a nightclub and tattoo parlor. Birmingham County FA ruled this sponsorship violated their rules because the businesses offer age-restricted services. The club, unable to afford replacing the kits, argues they cannot find other sponsors. Manager Russell Jukes said he had contacted 50 local businesses for sponsorship, and the Arena nightclub and Warstones Body Art agreed to support the teams. After a complaint, the FA informed the club that the kits were banned and needed to be changed. Birmingham County FA stated in a letter to Mr. Jukes that advertising a tattoo parlor and nightclub on under-10 and under-8 team shirts would break their regulations. They deemed these products/services inappropriate for the young players, who are significantly younger than 18. Mr. Jukes said the club operates on a limited budget and hopes local businesses will sponsor them. Without sponsorship, he fears the two teams will have to disband, leaving around 30 children disappointed.

He also won five County Championship titles, three with Yorkshire and two with Nottinghamshire. Sidebottom, whose father Arnie played for Yorkshire and won one Test cap in 1985, has taken 1,028 wickets in all competitions, including 737 in first-class cricket.
