Mastercard is acquiring Vocalink, a UK company that manages the country's ATM network (Link) and automated clearing house (Bacs) for direct debits, for up to £869 million. This acquisition follows Softbank's £24.3 billion offer for British chip designer Arm Holdings, which raised concerns about British companies becoming takeover targets due to the pound's post-Brexit decline. Vocalink is currently owned by major banks like Barclays, Lloyds, and HSBC and is chaired by former Bank of England deputy governor Sir John Gieve. A Vocalink spokesperson stated that talks with Mastercard had been ongoing for eight months and denied that the deal was opportunistic due to the weakened pound. Chancellor Philip Hammond welcomed the acquisition, emphasizing Britain's attractiveness to international investors. Earlier this year, the Payment Systems Regulator recommended that banks relinquish control of the UK's payment systems to foster competition and innovation. Hammond noted that the Vocalink deal aligns with the regulator's recommendation, which aims to benefit challenger banks, fintech companies, consumers, and small businesses. Mastercard will acquire 92.4% of Vocalink, with the remaining 7.6% held by the banks for at least three years. The deal is valued at £700 million, with an additional payment of up to £169 million contingent on performance targets. While the specific targets were not disclosed, Vocalink confirmed consultations with the Treasury, Bank of England, and Payment Systems Regulator regarding the acquisition.

They have held preliminary discussions with various stakeholders, including landowners, Dumfries and Galloway Council, Scottish Natural Heritage, and the Scottish government. However, no final decision has been made, and several options are being explored. John Glen, Buccleuch's chief executive, explained their structured land management approach considers economic, environmental, and community benefits. They believe this land could offer greater economic and environmental value to local and regional communities. Currently, they are conducting feasibility studies on various proposals and plan to discuss the findings with stakeholders and communities. Glen stated that the options under consideration include building up to 140 turbines. At that scale, the project would be one of Scotland's largest wind farms, significantly contributing to the Scottish government's renewable energy goals. He also noted that the wind farm could create 300 to 400 construction jobs over four years and 20 to 30 permanent jobs upon completion. Consultation with Wanlockhead and other local communities is scheduled to begin soon. The proposed site is located near the Muirkirk and North Lowther Uplands Special Protection Area. Alan Baker, managing director of 2020 Renewables, emphasized that they are in the early stages of the process and continue to develop their plans. He believes the site has significant potential to deliver substantial economic and environmental benefits.
Some figures will be in Euros, some in Sterling, some adjusted for inflation, and others will be rough estimates. All these claims will be misleading. Even if you understand what "X squillion Euros" means when spread across the 503 million inhabitants of 4 million square kilometers, it would be difficult to grasp the full costs and benefits for each of the 28 countries from any given EU action. We will have to vote based on instinct. In southern England, we are closer to the continent than the rest of the UK. Traveling there is easier, yet this seems to make us more Eurosceptic. At the last election, we elected more UKIP MEPs than any other party, including their leader Nigel Farage, who received 32% of the vote. The Conservatives came second with 31%, and Labour third with 15%. The EU spends less on development in our region compared to other parts of the UK. Blue flags announcing Euro-grants are more common in Burnley than Basingstoke. As one of the most prosperous areas of the UK, are we more confident about the economic impact of leaving the EU? Are we more worried about immigration with Calais nearby? Or, considering London's proximity, should we be concerned that the City could lose ground if Paris or Berlin take over its financial position? The last remaining Liberal Democrat MEP, Catherine Bearder, elected by south-east England voters, publishes a list of the top 10 "myths" about the EU. Her number one myth is that Brussels bureaucrats impose laws on us without our say. She argues this is untrue because the commission can only propose legislation, not enforce it. However, others might point to David Cameron's struggles to secure compromises from other EU states. Sceptics might argue that his persistent efforts to gain concessions demonstrate the extent of power lost. Predicting the outcome based on gut feeling might prove as difficult for pollsters as predicting the election results. One analysis suggests it will be a "Bank versus Bus" scenario. The "Bank" theory posits that despite our complaints about the bank, the inconvenience of change will deter us from leaving the EU. Conservative MEP Dan Hannan proposes the "Bus" theory: if you find yourself on the wrong bus, heading in the wrong direction, get off at the first available stop. Bank or Bus. It's certainly a better way to approach this than counting Euro grants.
A 32-year-old man sustained a concussion and required 14 stitches to his head, according to his lawyer, Robert Goggin. Fire officials reported the recovery of an eighth body from the Amtrak wreckage on Thursday. Officials confirmed that all 243 passengers on the train have been accounted for. The train was traveling at 106 mph (170 km/h) before derailing on a curve where the speed limit is 50 mph. Federal investigators are currently unsure why the train was traveling at such a high speed. A cadaver dog discovered the eighth body, which was transported to the medical examiner's office. The identity will only be released if the family chooses to do so, stated Philadelphia mayor Michael Nutter during a news conference. Mr. Goggin, representing Mr. Bostian who has not yet provided a statement to law enforcement, said Mr. Bostian remembers entering the curve, attempting to reduce the train's speed, and then losing consciousness. He does not recall using the emergency brake. Mr. Bostian is cooperating with the police and has consented to a blood test and the surrender of his mobile phone. He maintains that he was not consuming alcohol or drugs. Mayor Nutter characterized the driver as "clearly reckless and irresponsible," emphasizing the need to understand the engineer's actions and the reason for the excessive speed. He defended his choice of words at the news conference, stating that no reasonable person would consider traveling at such a speed knowing the significant speed restriction on the curve. The National Transportation Safety Board (NTSB), however, is taking a more cautious approach, wanting to allow Mr. Bostian time to recover from the shock before interviewing him about the accident. Mr. Bostian had worked for Amtrak as a conductor for nine years before becoming a driver in 2010. He graduated from the University of Missouri-Columbia in 2006. Amtrak CEO Joe Boardman stated that the agency anticipates limited service between New York and Philadelphia by Monday, with the possibility of full service resuming on Tuesday. He pledged to have Positive Train Control, a system that could have potentially prevented the accident, installed throughout the Northeast Corridor by the end of the year. Amtrak has been working on implementing this system since 2008. Mr. Boardman asserted that Amtrak will likely be the only railroad in the Western Hemisphere with Positive Train Control.
Harbinder Khatkar, who had previously been acquitted of rape in 2011, attacked three women on the street and three more in their homes in Derby on February 1st. Six weeks after his first trial, he was convicted of the 2011 rape under double jeopardy laws. This was his third trial for this case, as previous juries were unable to reach a verdict. One victim from February 1st was repeatedly raped in her home over several hours and threatened with violence against her children. Judge John Burgess, who presided over the sentencing at Derby Crown Court, described Khatkar as a manipulative individual with no regard for others. He noted the significant damage inflicted on the victims, particularly the woman who had to testify three times, including at two previous trials. Judge Burgess expressed concern about Khatkar's behavior during the trial, demonstrating contempt for the court and those involved. He questioned what Khatkar's actions might be like towards a vulnerable woman alone in her home. Khatkar, from Sinfin in Derby, was found guilty of 18 offenses, including five rapes, and sentenced to a minimum of 14 years in prison before parole eligibility. Judge Burgess stated his belief that life in prison was the only appropriate sentence, citing the real danger Khatkar posed to women if released. He emphasized the exceptional nature of the case due to the home invasions and attacks on four women. Khatkar's crimes included rape, robbery, causing actual bodily harm, assault, sexual assault, and trespass with intent to commit a sexual offense. One victim shared that her life had been "completely devastated" by Khatkar's actions in her home. The Crown Prosecution Service successfully overturned the original verdict at the Court of Appeal, allowing for a retrial under the amended double jeopardy law, which came into effect in 2005. This marked the first retrial under this law in Derbyshire.

A collection of unseen archive footage paints a vivid picture of Ulster life, spanning from Winston Churchill's controversial visit to Belfast in 1912 to a Viking re-enactment in a County Down fishing town in 1963. The British Film Institute (BFI) has released hundreds of unique films online for free as part of the "From Britain On Film" project. The collection includes royal visits, Lord Mayor's shows, and Churchill's address to nationalists in Belfast, where he expressed support for Home Rule. Dozens of travelogues promote Northern Ireland to tourists in Great Britain and the United States, contributing to tourism growth before the Troubles. The 1930 Ulster Tourist Trophy race at the Ards circuit in County Down is also captured, attracting thousands of spectators and garnering international motorsport attention. Beyond these significant events, the archive offers glimpses into everyday life. Films showcase people working in spinning mills and constructing the M1 at Stockman's Lane in Belfast, highlighting the region's industrial prosperity. Another film documents Northern Ireland's first hosting of the World Ploughing Championships, where a local man, Lawrence McMillan, wins the title in 1959. A 1938 recording of the Royal Victoria Hospital in Belfast provides a rare look into its casualty department and operating theatres, ten years before the establishment of the National Health Service. While medical advancements have been made, the film concludes with an appeal to reduce waiting lists, a concern that persists today. The project aims to preserve delicate film that might otherwise be lost, according to Francis Jones of Northern Ireland Screen, who gathered much of the footage. He emphasizes the unique nostalgia and visceral impact of seeing these films firsthand, stating that they "really strike a chord with people." The full "From Britain On Film" archive is available on the BFI Player.

Twenty-three Shia Muslims were convicted of forming a terrorist organization and carrying out bombings in two Shia villages last December. The bombings resulted in the deaths of a Jordanian policeman and a Bahraini man. Prosecutors stated that the group specialized in creating explosives to target police officers. The defendants also had their citizenship revoked. This case is one of many trials in Bahrain related to unrest and attacks since 2011, when Shia-led protests demanding reforms were suppressed. At least 90 people, including 18 police officers, have been killed in the violence. In a separate case, 29 people were sentenced to five years to life in prison for a bombing that injured police officers last year. Human rights groups have criticized Bahrain's justice system for unfair trials and harsh sentences.

The Japan Coast Guard rescued six Chinese crew members and is searching for eight others. China expressed gratitude to Japan for the rescue. This incident follows a warning from Japan's foreign minister earlier this week that relations with China were worsening significantly. The uninhabited islands, known as Senkaku in Japan and Diaoyu in China, are at the center of a long-standing dispute between the two countries. The fishing boat sank shortly after 5:00 am local time, according to the Japan Times, after colliding with the bulk carrier Anangel Courage, about 65 kilometers off Uotsuri Island. The cargo ship sent a distress signal, prompting the Japan Coast Guard to dispatch a vessel and a plane to the area. China expressed appreciation for the rescue after being informed of the incident, according to a statement from the Japanese foreign ministry. Earlier this month, approximately 230 Chinese fishing boats and coast guard vessels entered waters near the islands. Japan's Foreign Minister Fumio Kishida demanded their departure, stating that the situation surrounding the Japan-China relationship was significantly deteriorating.
Mobile phone operators in the UK have had their licences amended to require them to provide voice coverage to at least 90% of the country's landmass by the end of 2017. While the companies already ensure 3G coverage for 90% of UK buildings, this doesn't address the issue of dropped calls in rural areas. In December 2016, Ofcom, the UK communications regulator, published a report detailing the 2G geographic coverage provided by three of the four major operators, highlighting their ability to support calls and texts. Ofcom was unable to provide similar figures for Three's 3G and 4G coverage. Culture Secretary Sajid Javid announced the licence changes, stating that poor mobile coverage has plagued too many parts of the UK for too long. He believes the changes will bring world-class mobile phone coverage to the country. The Department for Culture, Media and Sport (DCMS) anticipates a two-thirds reduction in "not-spots" as a result of this agreement. In November 2016, Mr Javid had threatened to force operators to automatically transfer customers to rival networks at no cost if they lost signal, citing concerns about poor coverage. The operators argued this would negatively impact battery life and network security. However, they agreed to the licence changes in December. The 2017 target aims to reduce the frequency of mobile signal black spots, particularly for travellers and visitors in rural areas. Which?, a consumer association, welcomed the government's action and urged swift implementation by the mobile phone companies, with financial penalties for failing to meet their commitments. Ofcom noted that EE, Three, and Vodafone are also on track to match O2's provision of 4G coverage to the interior of 98% of UK premises by the end of 2015. The European Union's competition regulators are yet to approve the plan. Three may be better positioned to achieve the 2017 target due to its parent company, Hutchison Whampoa, attempting to acquire O2 from Telefonica.

However, Rutherford reconsidered and will attend the event in Belfast. He has stated that he will not engage with Fury during the ceremony. Rutherford's girlfriend, Susie Verill, detailed the abuse they received in an article for Standard Issue Magazine. Rutherford expressed his commitment to standing up for what he believes is right, acknowledging the harsh Twitter abuse directed at him and his family since his stance against Fury. A petition calling for Fury's removal from the shortlist of 12 nominees has garnered over 130,000 signatures. Rutherford, who won gold at the World Championships in August, confirmed he would avoid speaking to Fury, emphasizing his previous statement to the BBC that he would not socialize with someone holding such views. He believes it is important to distance himself from individuals whose opinions demean those who may not have a strong voice. Fury, the 27-year-old heavyweight champion, won the WBA, IBF, and WBO titles in November, defeating Wladimir Klitschko, who had held the world championship for 11 years. This victory led to Fury and Andy Murray, Britain's Davis Cup winner, being added to an extended shortlist of 12 nominees shortly before the announcement on November 30th. Fury, who has since been stripped of the IBF belt, has attempted to clarify controversial comments about women, homosexuality, and abortion. He maintains that he does not hate anyone and that his only intention is to spread love. The BBC clarified that Fury's inclusion on the list does not signify endorsement of his personal views. BBC Director General Tony Hall stated that Fury was included based on his sporting achievements, leaving the final decision to the public.

Barren circles, surrounded by grass and measuring 2 to 15 meters in diameter, are found in various locations. Researchers from Strathclyde and Princeton universities propose that these circles are formed by the interaction of plants and termite activity. Previously, this phenomenon was thought to be limited to the Namib desert in southern Africa. However, similar circles were discovered in western Australia in 2014. Scientists have long debated the causes of these circles, with some suggesting sand termites consume plant roots and others proposing competition between plant species. The new theory combines both explanations, suggesting that the interaction between termites and plants creates the fairy circles. Termites remove vegetation around their mounds to conserve water. The surrounding vegetation then grows taller due to the increased moisture. Dr. Juan Bonachela from the University of Strathclyde's Department of Mathematics and Statistics explained that their findings unify the two existing theories and provide a possible explanation for regular vegetation patterns globally. He stated that termites remove vegetation on their mounds to increase moisture, essential for their survival in dry environments, creating the bare center of the circle. The surrounding vegetation thrives in this water-rich area, forming the circle. The repetitive pattern results from competing termite colonies. This behavior benefits the entire ecosystem, allowing it to withstand harsh conditions and recover from droughts more quickly than without termites. This research utilized field data from four continents and computer simulations.
The Sudanese government approved the independence of South Sudan, where the predominantly Christian and animist population had long fought against rule by the Arab Muslim north. However, disagreements over oil revenue sharing and border demarcation persist, causing tension between the two newly formed nations. Sudan has a history of conflict. Two civil wars between the north and south resulted in 1.5 million deaths, and ongoing conflict in Darfur has displaced two million people and killed over 200,000. Sudan's population is 45.7 million, with Arabic and English (official) as the main languages. Islam is the dominant religion, and life expectancy is 60 years for men and 64 years for women. The Sudanese pound is the national currency. President Omar Hassan al-Bashir has ruled Sudan for over 25 years. He seized power in a coup in 1989 and was elected president in 1996. He has been re-elected multiple times, most recently in 2015, securing another five-year term. Most opposition parties boycotted this election. President Bashir faces two international arrest warrants issued by the International Criminal Court in The Hague for charges of genocide, war crimes, and crimes against humanity. Sudanese broadcasting is heavily restricted, with state-controlled TV and radio reflecting government policy. While the private press offers opposition viewpoints, the government exerts influence over publications. By July 2014, Sudan had 9.3 million internet users, and blogging is subject to monitoring. Key dates in Sudanese history include: - Circa 2000-1500 BC: Emergence of the Nubian Kingdom of Kush in present-day northern Sudan and Lower Nubia, extending to Egypt's Aswan province. - 651: Islam spreads to Sudan from Egypt. - 1820-21: The Ottoman Empire conquers the northern part of Sudan. - 1899-1955: Sudan is governed jointly by Britain and Egypt. - 1956: Sudan gains independence. - 1983: President Numeiri implements Sharia Islamic law. - 2003: Conflict begins in the Darfur region. - 2009: The International Criminal Court issues an arrest warrant for President Omar Bashir for war crimes and crimes against humanity related to the ongoing Darfur conflict. - 201

Fourteen-year-old Seren Bernard was found deceased in Milford Haven, Pembrokeshire, in April 2012. John Keith Mumberson, Seren's drug worker, stated at the Milford Haven hearing on Tuesday that she seemed optimistic about the future during their final encounter. However, he mentioned that she could act impulsively and sometimes disregarded her own safety. Mumberson had assisted Seren in managing her drug and alcohol use, conducting hourly sessions with her at her school. On Monday, the inquest revealed a mental health assessment indicating Seren's suicidal thoughts was not communicated to her social worker. Despite this, Mumberson asserted that Seren never expressed suicidal feelings to him. He believed Seren's foster parents provided a supportive and positive environment, and he felt things were progressing well for her. "My primary recollection is that she was beginning to look forward," he said. "She was in a secure environment, and we could build upon that. That's what I remember from our last meeting." The inquest is ongoing.

No one had seen Mr. Corfield since he left the White Horse Inn in Llanfair-ym-Muallt in the early hours of Thursday, July 25. Throughout the week of the show, police, search and rescue teams, and volunteers searched for the young man from Trefaldwyn. Police said his family is receiving specialist support from officers following the discovery. James was a keen and talented cricketer, playing for Trefaldwyn Cricket Club. Stephen McCafferty, 62, from Thetford, Norfolk, admitted to raping a 22-year-old woman on Wells Road in Knowle in 1981. He surrendered to police and confessed on December 30, 2015, two weeks after officers collected a DNA sample from him. McCafferty will serve half his sentence in prison, as stated by Bristol Crown Court. In a recorded confession, McCafferty expressed remorse, saying, "I hate myself. I wish I hadn't done it. You have your family, you don't want to upset them, you're scared of that." The victim, speaking through the police, said she had lost hope of the perpetrator being caught but was grateful for the police's persistence. She stated that despite the years, the effects of the assault still linger, but the conviction brings some relief. Avon and Somerset Police revealed that McCafferty was unaware his DNA sample matched the DNA found on the victim's clothing the day before he traveled to Bristol to confess. On July 15, 1981, the victim was cycling along Wells Road near Somerset Road when she was attacked and raped. No charges were filed at the time. Years later, during a case review, DNA from the victim matched DNA recovered from a burglary in Long Ashton, Bristol, in May 1999, but there was no match on the national database. Six months ago, another review led detectives to McCafferty, who was connected to the burglary investigation. Shortly after Christmas, McCafferty confessed to both the rape and the burglary. Detective Constable Scott Westbrook-Smith highlighted the profound impact of the crime on the victim's life, stating that she still lives in fear and avoids going out alone after 35 years. He commended her bravery and support throughout the investigation. He also acknowledged the devastation to McCafferty's family, whose lives were disrupted by his unexpected confession.
In 2012, a record 668 rhinos were killed for their horns, a nearly 50% increase from 2011. Most of these deaths occurred in South Africa's Kruger National Park, the country's largest wildlife reserve. Experts attribute this surge in poaching to the growing demand for rhino horn in Asia, where it is believed to have medicinal properties and can treat diseases like cancer. This belief drives the illegal trade, with horns fetching around $65,000 per kilogram. South Africa, which is home to approximately three-quarters of the world's 28,000 rhinos, saw a drastic increase in poaching since 2007, when only 13 rhinos were killed. Criminal gangs, utilizing sophisticated technologies, are behind this rise in poaching. Despite the South African government's efforts to combat poaching with soldiers and surveillance aircraft, the number of rhino killings continues to rise, indicating a loss in the fight against this illegal activity. According to TRAFFIC, a wildlife monitoring network, South Africa's rhinos are facing a poaching crisis that threatens their population decline. Rhinos are being illegally killed, their horns removed, and the animals left to die, all for the perceived medicinal benefits of rhino horn. Since the beginning of the year, five more rhinos have been killed in South Africa. India, home to over 2,200 rhinos in the well-protected Kaziranga reserve, is also experiencing poaching threats. Despite the presence of approximately 900 armed rangers and guards, poachers killed 18 rhinos in India last year, an increase from ten in 2011.

A recent appointment has sparked concern among internet rights activists that net neutrality laws are at risk. Net neutrality advocates believe all internet traffic should be treated equally, preventing internet service providers from prioritizing certain content, such as charging more for faster access to data-heavy services like Netflix. Supporters, including companies like Google and Facebook, argue that net neutrality is crucial for online competition. They contend that without it, new video services would struggle to compete with established platforms like YouTube and Vimeo. Republican members of the FCC have previously voted against net neutrality, and under President Trump, the commission will have a Republican majority. During President Obama's administration, net neutrality laws were frequently debated, often generating significant public attention. In 2015, landmark laws were enacted to protect net neutrality, seen as a victory for advocates who feared corporate influence. However, Ajit Pai, the newly appointed FCC chairman, has expressed a desire to roll back these regulations, stating that they hinder investment, innovation, and job creation. Pai, who previously voted against strengthening net neutrality laws, will assume his role without a Senate confirmation hearing. Critics, such as Craig Aaron from the advocacy group Free Press, have condemned Pai's record on issues before the FCC, arguing that he favors large corporations and undermines public safeguards. Conversely, telecom giants like Verizon, Comcast, and AT&T have welcomed Pai's appointment. AT&T, in particular, could benefit significantly from Pai's support for its proposed merger with Time Warner, a move that contradicts President Trump's previous stance against the deal. AT&T stated that Pai will work with commissioners to implement a regulatory framework that supports the president's agenda.
Shared spaces aim to enhance pedestrian safety by eliminating traffic signs, crosswalks, and curbs. However, a survey commissioned by Lord Holmes of Richmond, involving 600 participants, revealed that 63% perceived their shared space experiences negatively. Lord Holmes expressed hope that the study would shift perspectives on these "dangerous and costly planning follies." The report also indicated that over a third of respondents actively avoided shared spaces and that accidents in these areas were significantly underreported. Lord Holmes, a blind former Paralympian, emphasized the urgent need for a moratorium on shared spaces, calling them "dangerous and costly planning follies" that transform town centers into "dangerous third-world traffic free-for-alls." While the Department for Transport (DfT) defines shared spaces as streets or areas designed to prioritize pedestrian movement and comfort by reducing vehicle dominance and encouraging shared usage, Lord Holmes contends that the survey results demonstrate the ineffectiveness of these schemes in achieving these goals. The report recommends a reevaluation of shared space implementations. Legally, shared spaces are also facing scrutiny. Solicitor Chris Fry, representing five visually impaired individuals, is pursuing legal action against local authorities responsible for shared spaces lacking pedestrian crossings. Fry argues that the reliance on eye contact between pedestrians and drivers for safety compromises the well-being of visually impaired individuals, violating their rights under the Equality Act. The DfT maintains that local authorities are responsible for assessing the suitability of shared spaces, considering the needs of all community members, including those with disabilities.
Samsung anticipates an operating profit of 8.4 trillion won ($7.9 billion; £4.8 billion) for the first quarter of 2023, representing a 4% decrease compared to the same period last year. This decline follows a 6% drop in operating profit during the previous quarter. Analysts attribute this trend to the challenges Samsung faces in boosting earnings amidst falling smartphone prices. Young Park, an analyst with Hyundai Securities, points to shrinking smartphone margins and a slowing growth rate in the sector as contributing factors. Samsung's Galaxy smartphone series has been a major driver of its growth in recent years, but the company is encountering increasing competition from firms like Apple, HTC, Lenovo, ZTE, and Huawei, all vying for market share. This heightened competition has led to price reductions to attract customers, squeezing profit margins. Earlier this year, Samsung warned of further intensifying competition. One analyst suggests that to sustain earnings growth as profit margins continue to decline, Samsung may need to implement cost-cutting measures. Greg Roh, an analyst with HMC Investment and Securities, emphasizes the importance of cost reduction and marketing expense reduction for Samsung's growth. He believes that improving internal efficiencies is crucial for Samsung to avoid further earnings decline.

Ms. Parmar's parents passed away when she was very young, and she can't recall ever doing anything other than scavenging for rubbish to sell. It's a difficult way to live. The BBC's Rupa Jha spoke with five individuals in Ahmedabad, one of India's largest cities, about their work experiences. Ms. Parmar describes the challenges of her work, stating that people treat her poorly and she often faces harassment and danger. She acknowledges her anger but believes there's no use in dwelling on it, as this is simply her reality. She lives with her unemployed son and daughter-in-law in a two-room dwelling. Rising food prices have made life more difficult, forcing her to rely more on inexpensive chillies as a source of vegetables. She earns approximately $20 per month by working two shifts a day, one in the morning and one at night. Ms. Parmar supplements her income by donating blood for clinical research. Despite India's overall development, she feels her life has deteriorated. She explains that the city's cleanup efforts have reduced the amount of rubbish available for her to collect.
Disney parks are banning selfie sticks, citing safety concerns for both guests and staff. The ban, effective Tuesday in US parks and Wednesday in Paris and Hong Kong, stems from worries that the extended arms of selfie sticks could cause injury during photo opportunities or on rides. Visitors will be asked to store their selfie sticks in lockers upon entering the parks. This follows similar bans at several football clubs, the National Gallery, Wimbledon, and Apple's WWDC conference. While St John Ambulance hasn't seen a spike in selfie stick-related injuries, they advise seeking medical attention if struck in the head, experiencing dizziness, nausea, confusion, or memory loss.

Following consultations with businesses, Gorwel is urging the creation of a dedicated Brexit minister. Theresa May, the new Prime Minister, has appointed David Davis as Secretary of State for Exiting the EU in her first cabinet. Welsh ministers stated that they had already outlined their "immediate priorities" after the referendum and would provide further updates in due course. Gorwel believes businesses view Brexit as a chance to "transform" the Welsh economy and reduce reliance on government assistance. Think tank chairman Meirion Morgan emphasized: "With Theresa May as prime minister, it's clear that Brexit will dominate the UK Government's agenda for the foreseeable future. We recognize the substantial impact an EU exit will have on Wales, and we know the Welsh government is also aware of this. Following an outbreak in Brazil, Colombia, Ecuador, El Salvador, and Jamaica recommended that women delay pregnancies until more was known about the Zika virus. Brazil reported nearly 4,000 babies born with suspected microcephaly, or abnormally small heads, since October. US health authorities advised pregnant women to avoid traveling to over 20 countries in the Americas and beyond where Zika cases have been reported. While the link between microcephaly and Zika hasn't been confirmed, some deceased babies tested positive for the virus in their brains, and no other explanation for the microcephaly surge has been identified. Zika is not contagious and typically causes flu-like symptoms. Colombia's Health Minister Alejandro Gaviria urged women to delay pregnancies for up to eight months, stating that it was a way to communicate the risk and potential serious consequences. Similar warnings were issued in Ecuador, El Salvador, and Jamaica. However, women's rights advocates criticized these recommendations, arguing that women in the region often have limited control over their pregnancies. They pointed out that unplanned pregnancies are common and sexual violence is prevalent in the region. Brazil's health ministry reported 49 babies with suspected microcephaly had died, and Zika virus infection was found in five of these cases. Brazil is experiencing the largest known Zika outbreak, primarily in the northeast, with cases also detected in the southeast, including Rio de Janeiro and Sao Paulo. Several other Latin American countries have also seen a sharp rise in Zika cases, with over 13,500 reported in Colombia. The US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention issued initial travel warnings for pregnant women last week, adding eight more locations to the list on Friday.
Crime in London decreased by about 6% between 2012-13, resulting in 45,000 fewer reports. While personal thefts increased by 17% year-over-year, with 70% involving stolen mobile phones, the Metropolitan Police (Met) attributed the overall decline to proactive measures. The launch of the Trident Gang Crime Command, which targets approximately 250 active gangs in London using a combination of enforcement and prevention strategies, has contributed to the reduction. Offenses involving weapons also decreased, with a 20% drop in both knife and gun crime. Despite the success of Trident, the unit recently lost its responsibility for investigating firearms deaths. The Met stated this was because it created an "unhelpful" situation where murders were categorized based on the weapon used, when the same offenders might be involved in both knife and gun-related deaths. Homicides remained relatively stable at 106, compared to 103 the previous year, representing the lowest number in over 40 years. Victim Support expressed hope that the 16% increase in reported rapes reflects growing confidence in the reporting process. They believe the Met's efforts to improve the culture surrounding rape investigations have encouraged more victims to come forward. Jeff Gartner, from the charity, emphasized the importance of providing victims with adequate support. Mayor of London Boris Johnson commended the Met's efforts, stating that London remains one of the safest major cities globally and that the ongoing decline in crime rates is a testament to their hard work. He emphasized the commitment to maintaining a visible police presence to continue combating crime.
James Kinnell, a renowned poet, passed away on Tuesday at his Vermont home after battling leukemia. His wife, Barbara, confirmed his death. Kinnell was celebrated for his prolific body of work, which spanned five decades and included over a dozen books. He achieved significant recognition in 1982 when his collection "Selected Poems" won both the Pulitzer Prize and the National Book Award for Poetry, sharing the latter with fellow poet Charles Wright. Among his other notable works are "The Book of Nightmares," inspired by the Vietnam War, "When One Has Lived a Long Time Alone," and "Mortal Acts, Mortal Words." "The Bear," a famous poem about a hunter who empathizes with his prey after consuming animal substances, is one of his most celebrated pieces. Other notable poems include "After Making Love We Hear Footsteps" and "When the Towers Fell," a reflection on the September 11 terrorist attacks. Born in Providence, Rhode Island, on February 1, 1927, Kinnell was the son of Scottish and Irish immigrants. He described himself as a "very silent child, almost mute," and found solace in poetry, believing it to be the key to a happy life. At Princeton University, where he was roommates with future US poet laureate W.S. Merwin, he was introduced to the works of W.B. Yeats. Merwin, who considered Kinnell a "very generous soul" and "like a brother," praised his poetry as "warm hearted" and reflective of his friend's independent yet empathetic nature. Kinnell's breakthrough came in 1960 with "The Avenue Bearing the Initial of Christ Into the New World," a 14-part work exploring Avenue C in Manhattan and its inhabitants. He served as Vermont's poet laureate from 1989 to 1993 and resided there permanently from 2005. In 2010, the Academy of American Poets honored him with the Wallace Stevens Award for his lifetime achievements. He was married twice: first to Spanish translator Ines Delgado de Torres, with whom he had two children, Fergus and Maud, and later to Barbara in 1997.
Dr. Nihal Weerasena was sanctioned by the Medical Practitioners Tribunal Service (MPTS) for misconduct related to his care of six patients, including five children and one adult, while employed by Leeds Teaching Hospitals NHS Trust. The MPTS found Dr. Weerasena guilty of showing "reckless disregard for patient safety" and "substandard practice." Specifically, the MPTS ruled that Dr. Weerasena's treatment of a seven-year-old patient during a complex surgery in 2012 fell below acceptable standards. However, he was cleared of allegations that he omitted a key event from his typed report of the surgery. The MPTS also found that Dr. Weerasena failed to accurately record surgery on a six-year-old patient in 2010. An allegation regarding his care of a sixth child was not proven. The MPTS determined that Dr. Weerasena's misconduct and lack of insight made him unfit to continue practicing medicine, stating that his actions posed a risk to public safety and the profession's reputation. Dr. Weerasena was referred to the General Medical Council in 2014 following a review of pediatric care services at Leeds Teaching Hospitals NHS Trust. This review was prompted by concerns about the safety of the children's heart surgery unit at Leeds General Infirmary, which had been temporarily suspended in 2013. A subsequent report concluded that the unit did not have unusually high mortality rates. Dr. Weerasena did not attend the hearing but provided a written explanation, alleging that he was the victim of a "cover-up."
In 2011, British artists sold 11.7% of all albums in the US, an increase from 9.8% in 2010. Adele's album "21" was the top seller with 5.8 million copies, followed by Mumford and Sons' "Sigh No More" with 1.4 million. Thirty British albums sold over 100,000 copies in the US. This marked the highest share for British artists since records began in 2000, potentially the highest since the 1980s. Other popular British albums included Coldplay's "Mylo Xyloto," Susan Boyle's "Someone to Watch Over Me," and both albums by Florence and the Machine. Adele's debut album "19" sold over 900,000 copies, and Marsha Ambrosius' debut album "Late Nights and Early Mornings" was also successful. Geoff Taylor, BPI chief executive, believes boybands like One Direction and The Wanted will continue this trend, stating that investments in British talent help artists achieve international success and boost the UK economy.
Residential sales within five miles of the new train stops have increased in the last three years, but the growth is less than in the wider Midlothian and Scottish Borders areas. House prices along the route have also risen since 2006, but again, the increase is lower than the council-wide figures. Since 2012, residential sales within five miles of the new stops have increased by 50.2% in Midlothian and 26.3% in the Borders. The corresponding figures for the whole council areas are 60.6% and 30.1% respectively, while the Scottish average is 28.2%. The Registers of Scotland (RoS) also found that house prices have remained largely unchanged. From 2006, when the bill to reopen the railway was approved, average prices in the Midlothian area have grown 6.1% to £166,639, while average prices within five miles of the new Borders stations have increased by 4.5% to £143,283. Over the same period, council-wide area figures are 13.4% in Midlothian and 9.5% in the Borders, with the Scottish increase at 16.1%. Rhona Mackay, head of data at RoS, stated: "We have seen an increase in sales around the new Borders railway stations, but this is slightly lower than the volumes seen in the local authority areas as a whole. Prices are also up, but again this is lower in the five-mile catchment areas than the wider local authority areas where the increase is more substantial. It is widely expected that the new line will stimulate interest in the property market in the areas close to the stations, and there have already been a number of new-build developments within the railway catchment areas in recent years. It will be interesting to revisit these statistics once the new link is up and running."
Glyptodonts, which inhabited South America for millions of years until the last Ice Age, could grow as large as cars. Their resemblance to armadillos, particularly their protective shells, led scientists to believe they were closely related. However, new research suggests that glyptodonts are not just cousins of armadillos, but a subfamily within the armadillo order. Researchers used computer models to predict DNA sequences of ancient armadillo ancestors based on the genes of living species. They then created RNA "bait" from these sequences and used it to extract glyptodont DNA from a fossilized shell sample at a Buenos Aires museum. This technique allowed them to confidently identify genuine DNA sequences from the ancient species. The team successfully reconstructed the complete mitochondrial genome of Doedicurus, a particularly large glyptodont species that reached up to 4 meters in length and weighed 1.5 tonnes. These herbivorous giants grew progressively larger over time until their extinction at the end of the Ice Age 10,000 years ago. This extinction coincides with the demise of other large South American mammals, such as elephant-sized ground sloths and giant saber-toothed cats. The study confirms that glyptodonts are a distinct lineage within the armadillo order, likely originating 35 million years ago. One key difference between glyptodonts and their modern relatives is their solid, dome-shaped shell, which lacked the segmented plates characteristic of armadillos. Researchers believe this unique shell evolved alongside the glyptodonts' remarkable size increase, explaining why some older glyptodont species had banded shells.
In January, the Home Office granted posthumous pardons to thousands of men who have since died. However, living individuals must apply to have their convictions expunged. Stonewall Cymru stated that these men were "unjustly persecuted," and many were unaware they could apply for clearance. This Wednesday marks the 50th anniversary of The Sexual Offences Act of 1967, which decriminalized sex between two men over 21 in England and Wales. Approximately 65,000 men were convicted under the previous law, with 15,000 still alive. Mabli Jones, campaign, policy, and research manager at Stonewall Cymru, believes men should not have to live with convictions no longer considered valid following the 2012 legal change. She emphasized the significance of the law change but highlighted that many men were unjustly persecuted and deserve to have their convictions removed. Jones noted that some individuals remain unaware of their ability to apply for a pardon and that the charity supports automatic removal of these convictions from records. A Home Office spokesperson explained that convictions are not automatically deleted because the government needs to ensure pardons are granted only to those convicted of offenses involving consensual behavior with individuals over 16 years of age. The spokesperson also expressed pride in the government's record of promoting equality. The latest ONS figures from 2015 show that 1.6% of people in Wales identify as gay, lesbian, or bisexual, compared to 1.8% in the UK. While Ms. Jones acknowledged improvements in attitudes towards LGBT individuals in Wales, she pointed out that many, particularly young people, still face discrimination. Stonewall Cymru's 2017 School Report revealed a decrease in anti-LGBT bullying and language in British schools since 2012. However, a survey of 3,700 LGBT pupils found that 45% still experience bullying due to their sexual orientation or gender identity. Ms. Jones stated that the charity advocates for mandatory teaching of all types of relationships in schools, along with information on safe sex within same-sex relationships. She mentioned their collaboration with Education Secretary Kirsty Williams, who has established an expert panel to advise the Welsh Government on creating more inclusive schools. Ms. Jones expressed optimism about significant progress in this area, recognizing its importance for young people.

The Aperture Spherical Telescope (FAST), spanning 500 meters and the size of 30 football fields, cost $180 million (£135 million) to develop. This satellite project will be used to explore space and search for extraterrestrial life, according to Xinhua news agency. Advancing China's space program is a top priority for Beijing. Scientists are beginning to debug and test the telescope, Zheng Xiaonian, deputy head of the National Astronomical Observation under the Chinese Academy of Sciences, told Xinhua. FAST will surpass the Arecibo Observatory in Puerto Rico, which measures 300 meters (984 feet) in diameter, to become the world's largest telescope. While China maintains that its space program is for peaceful purposes, the US Defense Department has stated that it is working to "prevent adversaries from using space-based assets in a crisis," as reported by Reuters. Beijing plans to launch the core module of its first space station in 2018.
A survey of 250 school, youth, and health staff in England indicates that many children are not eating enough during the school day. The Children's Food Trust poll found that 68.1% of respondents had observed an increase in families struggling to feed their children in the past two years. The survey suggests lunchboxes now contain less fruit and more unhealthy food. Nearly half (47.5%) of school staff reported seeing a change in children's lunchbox contents as household budgets tightened. Staff described seeing "poorer quality sandwich fillings" and "fewer processed items - more leftovers or store-cupboard items." However, some noted that healthier choices were being replaced with less nutritious options like "cold cooked rice or cold chips with fish fingers." There were also reports of increased junk food, sweets, and chocolate in lunchboxes, along with a decrease in fruit. The survey also found that 84.6% of participating professionals had witnessed children going without enough to eat. Of those who reported this, 84.8% stated it applied to roughly a third of the children they worked with. Linda Cregan, Children's Food Trust chief executive-designate, emphasized that many who work with children are going above and beyond to protect children from the effects of hunger and poor diet. She acknowledged the responsibility of parents to ensure their children eat well but stressed the reality of the struggle many families face. Cregan urged local authorities to prioritize funding for children's nutrition within their public health plans, suggesting this could be used for various initiatives such as cooking skills training, subsidizing healthy school meals, breakfast clubs, and holiday meal programs. Students at Priory School in Lewes shared their perspectives with the BBC's School Report project. They generally reported that their lunches were good, with some noting the availability of chips on Fridays. Students emphasized the convenience and enjoyment of hot school dinners, while others found packed lunches more cost-effective due to bulk purchasing.

Scientists plan to use a grant to reconstruct the face of Ta-Kush, a mummy at Maidstone Museum, and learn more about her background. Ta-Kush is believed to be a 14-year-old girl who died around 2,700 years ago. However, a CT scan of the mummy and coffin could change historical understanding. The £78,700 grant will also be used to combine the museum's Egyptian and Greek collections in a new ancient civilizations gallery, with Ta-Kush as the centerpiece. Recently, studies by an Egyptologist in Cairo revealed Ta-Kush's true name. For over 150 years, she was known as Ta-Kesh, but her name is now understood to be Ta-Kush, meaning "the Kushite lady." The mummy, at the museum since the 1850s, will be scanned by experts at Kent Institute of Medicine and Surgery (Kims) and analyzed by researchers at Liverpool John Moore's University, who will digitally reconstruct her face, head, and shoulders. This reconstruction will personalize Ta-Kush for visitors. The scientific analysis will also help confirm her age and sex. While currently believed to be a 14-year-old girl, previous scans of female mummies have been identified as male. Research has narrowed down Ta-Kush's origin to the area around Thebes. Details about her parents and name were gleaned from hieroglyphics on the coffin. The scan itself will take five minutes, but analysis and reconstruction will take considerably longer.

GP Energy has applied for permission to conduct exploratory drilling near Commonwood Farm in Borras. The company insists this is solely for exploration and they have no intention of fracking, a controversial method of gas extraction. However, local residents are concerned. Wrexham council will review GP Energy's plans to drill a borehole to assess coal bed methane deposits underground. The site is two miles from the River Dee, and opponents fear the test drilling could lead to hydraulic fracturing (fracking). Luke Ashley, a spokesperson for the newly formed North East Wales Against Fracking Action group, expressed concerns that the drilling could mark the beginning of industrialization in the countryside east of Wrexham. He explained that the test bore aims to determine the amount of gas present in coal seams extending from Flintshire through Wrexham, Cheshire, and Shropshire. Ashley warned that if full-scale extraction follows, it could involve injecting thousands of liters of water and chemicals into the ground to release the gas, potentially contaminating the River Dee's drinking water supply. The group plans to lobby council members before the application is discussed by the planning committee, likely later this year. A public meeting was scheduled for Tuesday evening. Fracking involves injecting water and chemicals under high pressure into shale rock to extract gas. It was temporarily banned in the UK after causing two earthquakes in Blackpool in 2011. Opponents argue that the water used in fracking will become contaminated and could enter domestic supplies. However, a UK government review concluded that fracking is safe if properly monitored. Dart Energy, which owns GP Energy, stated last week that they have no plans for fracking in the Wrexham area and will seal the well after exploration is complete. A company spokesperson emphasized that the planning application is for a temporary coal bed methane exploration well, which will be fully cemented upon completion. The purpose, they said, is to extract a core sample from the coal seam to test the methane content in a laboratory. They reiterated that Dart Energy has no intention of fracking this well and is not seeking permission to do so from the local authority or the Environment Agency. Wrexham council stated that the application will be considered by the planning committee in due course.

Approximately 21,000 British soldiers, including 7,000 Scots, died in the Battle of Loos in 1915. The 4th Black Watch, based in Dundee, suffered heavy losses. Prince Charles and Camilla joined around 1,000 people at a service in Dundee to honor the fallen. As the Royal Colonel of the 3rd Battalion (The Black Watch), The Royal Regiment of Scotland, Prince Charles's great uncle, Captain Fergus Bowes-Lyon, perished on the third day of the battle, which took place in northern France. On September 25, 1915, the first day of the battle, approximately 8,000 lives were lost within the first four hours of fighting. The centenary commemorations began on Friday with a beacon lighting at dawn atop Dundee's war memorial. Saturday's service featured a parade of 250 active-duty personnel and 300 veterans. City Square was transformed into a "cathedral" with four ministers representing various denominations. Prince Charles, known as the Duke of Rothesay in Scotland, laid a wreath alongside the First Minister and Lord Provost of Dundee at a specially commissioned memorial stone. During the reception, he unveiled a commemorative plaque, stating that the units involved in the Battle of Loos represent Scotland's finest regiments. He praised their courage and sacrifice for freedom in a foreign land. First Minister Nicola Sturgeon emphasized the battle's impact on every Scottish community, with around 30,000 Scots serving at Loos. Lord Provost Bob Duncan acknowledged the battle's profound effect on Dundee, particularly the heavy losses among local Black Watch battalions. He highlighted the weekend as an opportunity to remember their heroism and honor their memory. Charles and Camilla will attend a reception for armed forces personnel and veterans, and view an exhibition on the Battle of Loos created by Dundee school pupils.
A new plan to enhance type 1 diabetes management includes increased funding. This condition affects approximately 30,000 individuals in Scotland. A key focus of the plan is to expand the use of insulin pumps, which can eliminate the need for multiple daily injections. Currently, 3,200 insulin pumps are in use across Scotland. Additionally, more continuous glucose monitoring equipment will be made accessible. Karen Addington, the chief executive of the diabetes charity JDRF, stated that evidence demonstrates the effectiveness of this type 1 diabetes technology when combined with proper support and training. She welcomed the funding increase aimed at improving access. Health Secretary Shona Robison emphasized that insulin pumps and continuous glucose monitoring kits significantly simplify type 1 diabetes management for some individuals. She underscored the importance of proper control in preventing complications and improving outcomes. This new investment will expand the availability of this equipment to more people, facilitating healthier lives.
The Sun, the largest object in our Solar System, accounts for 99% of its total mass. Its diameter is approximately 109 times greater than Earth's, though it could contain over a million Earths within its volume. Scientists believe the Sun and the planets originated from the solar nebula roughly 4.59 billion years ago, making it a very ancient star. The Sun's surface temperature is 5,500 degrees Celsius, while its core reaches a scorching 15.6 million degrees Celsius. Temperature variations exist on the Sun's surface, with "sun spots" appearing as cooler regions. Its composition is primarily hydrogen, with smaller amounts of helium and trace elements like oxygen and iron. Light from the Sun takes just 8 minutes to travel to Earth. A reminder: never look directly at the Sun, as it can cause serious eye damage.
Anne Dufourmantelle entered the water at Pampelonne beach near St Tropez on Friday after two children got into difficulty in strong winds. Lifeguards rescued the unharmed children. French culture minister Françoise Nyssen described Dufourmantelle as a "great philosopher who helped us live". Witnesses said the 53-year-old philosopher was 50 meters from the children when an orange warning flag changed to red, prohibiting swimming due to dangerous conditions. She immediately tried to reach them but was swept away by a strong current. Resuscitation attempts were unsuccessful, according to France 3. It is unclear if Dufourmantelle knew the children. Her funeral will be held in Ramatuelle, southern France, on Tuesday. Dufourmantelle wrote extensively about the importance of taking risks and accepting the inherent dangers of everyday life, as explored in her 2011 book "Praise of Risk". Ms Nyssen wrote on Twitter that Dufourmantelle "helped us to live and think about the world today". Fellow philosopher Raphaël Enthoven expressed his sadness at her death, noting that she "spoke so well of dreams". In a 2015 interview with Liberation, where Dufourmantelle later became a columnist, she stated that the idea of "absolute security - like 'zero risk' - is a fantasy". She argued that facing genuine dangers can lead to personal growth and action. Dufourmantelle believed fear can be used as a political tool to control freedoms. She contended that promises of increased security can reinforce control and limit personal liberties. She also argued that visible security measures, such as armed officers, can increase fear. Dufourmantelle earned a doctorate in philosophy from Paris-Sorbonne in 1994 and later practiced psychoanalysis. She received the Raymond de Boyer Prize of Sainte-Suzanne for philosophy in 1998.

Based in Edinburgh, Ecometrica has developed software capable of rapidly analyzing satellite imagery to identify threats to tropical forests, such as illegal logging and expanding agriculture. A contract from the UK Space Agency will have Ecometrica collaborating with experts in six countries to protect endangered ecosystems. The "Forests 2020" project seeks to enhance management and conservation efforts across 300 million hectares of tropical forests. Ecometrica will partner with organizations in Brazil, Colombia, Ghana, Indonesia, Kenya, and Mexico, establishing earth observation laboratories to assess rainforest threats and guide conservation resource allocation. Ecometrica will subcontract experts from the University of Edinburgh, the University of Leicester, and another Edinburgh company, Carbomap. Funding comes from the UK Space Agency's International Partnership Programme (IPP), which leverages British space technology and expertise to benefit developing nations. Ray Fielding, head of the IPP at the UK Space Agency, stated, "The program will uncover innovative applications of space technology to address this crucial issue, recognized by the UN as vital for sustainable development. Our aim is to make a tangible difference for those on the front lines of forest conservation." Dr. Richard Tipper, Executive Chairman of Ecometrica, remarked, "We all recognize the critical role tropical rainforests play in the global ecosystem's survival. However, many are only now realizing the need to utilize technology to ensure conservation efforts are effective and properly directed. Earth Observation platforms will enable earlier detection of threats like fires and illegal logging, leading to faster and more cost-effective responses on the ground."

Hold Security discovered approximately 360 million compromised account credentials, including email addresses and passwords, along with 1.25 billion email addresses without passwords. The origin of these credentials, found over three weeks, is unknown, but they encompass major email providers. Experts consider this data breach exceptionally large. Graham Cluley, an online security consultant, described it as "Godzilla-sized, a monster." He believes it presents a "complete treasure trove" for cybercriminals, potentially containing duplicates but still a significant trove. Hold Security attributes these findings to "multiple breaches" under independent investigation. They call the numbers "mind-boggling" and urge action regarding online security. Cluley suggests these details could be used to access not only associated accounts but potentially others due to common password usage across multiple sites. He explains that cybercriminals can use common passwords found in the database to crack into additional accounts. Reuters highlights the potential risk this breach poses to consumers and companies, exceeding that of stolen credit card data. The stolen user names and passwords could compromise online bank accounts, corporate networks, health records, and various computer systems. Alex Holden, Hold Security's chief information security officer, emphasizes the overwhelming volume of data. He states that the credentials originated from breaches yet to be publicly disclosed. Companies affected may remain unaware until notified by third parties who uncover evidence of the hacking. Hold Security is actively working to identify the victims. The breach also includes unpaired email addresses, valuable for launching spamming and phishing attacks.

They lost their children who were just beginning their adult lives, and they will never know the people they could have become or the families they could have had. The pain of their loss may lessen over time, but it will never disappear, and their families will always feel incomplete. Tragically, his actions had devastating consequences. An investigation revealed that fake borrowers had "read" thousands of books at a library. Library staff were suspended while officials investigated whether the fabricated readers resulted in the libraries receiving more state funding. Staff claimed their actions saved money by keeping popular books in circulation, which would have needed to be repurchased if discarded. Officials from the Lake County public resources department, which oversees libraries in Florida, said they were alerted to the existence of the fictitious readers at the East Lake County library by an anonymous source. A Lake County municipal staff investigation found that senior library staff had created several bogus identities, one of which was named after retired major-league baseball player Chuck Finley. Records showed Mr. Finley had checked out over 2,600 books in nine months, with some titles being checked out and returned within an hour. The official report estimates that the fake readers increased readership numbers at East Lake by almost 4%. Readership figures are significant because they determine the amount of state funding libraries receive. Last year, libraries in and around Lake County received approximately $1 million from local government. In a statement, the head librarian at East Lake stated that the fake borrowers were created solely to retain popular titles. He argued that following the computer system's recommendations to discard unread books would necessitate the libraries repurchasing recently discarded titles. He also claimed that many other libraries used "dummy" reader cards for the same purpose. Digital activist Cory Doctorow stated that the logging system had become a "straitjacket" that disregarded the opinions of staff who understood reader preferences. He asserted that the librarians had not manipulated the system for personal gain but rather to improve it by teaching it to prioritize circulation data based on on-the-ground knowledge.

The footage shows her last seen at the Broomielaw, by the River Clyde, at 3:16 am on Sunday. She had removed her high heels and was walking quickly and purposefully. The CCTV footage shows her walking from Sauchiehall Street to Argyle Street and onto James Watt Street before reaching the Broomielaw, where she was last seen on camera. The footage confirms she was wearing the same clothing as in the photograph released to the media, taken on Saturday evening. Ms. Aitchison is described as white, a Deaf/BSL user, approximately 5 feet tall, slim build, with long dark hair. She was wearing a black top, cream skirt, and in the CCTV images, she is seen carrying her black high-heeled sandals and walking barefoot. Patients diagnosed with the disease are supposed to receive follow-up eye examinations. However, it has been discovered that nearly 500 patients have not been tested since 2010, despite tests being able to detect retinal damage caused by high blood sugar levels. Health officials have contacted these affected patients to schedule new appointments. Diabetic retinopathy, a common complication of diabetes, occurs when elevated blood sugar levels damage the cells at the back of the eye. Untreated, it can lead to blindness. Systems intended to ensure patients received referrals for specialist eye examinations at local clinics failed to function properly. The University Hospitals of Morecambe Bay NHS Trust is investigating the cause of these errors. Diabetes UK expressed concern that patients had not been regularly checked. A spokesperson stated that diabetic retinopathy often lacks noticeable symptoms in its early stages, meaning individuals may not realize they have a problem until the damage is significant. Sheila O'Brien of Cumbria Societies for the Blind commented that while the situation is unfortunate, it is positive that the issue has been identified and is being investigated. She emphasized the importance of preventing similar occurrences in the future. An estimated 160 people in the Morecambe Bay area, 143 in North Cumbria, and nearly 200 in the Blackpool area were affected. George Nasmyth, deputy medical director of The University Hospitals of Morecambe Bay NHS Foundation Trust, stated that they are aware of the issue affecting several diabetes patients and their annual eye screening appointments. They are working with the Diabetic Eye Screening Programme, NHS England, and other NHS Trusts to determine the cause and prevent recurrence. All affected patients have been notified and invited for screening appointments. Individuals with concerns are encouraged to contact 0845 0500 109.
The Bank of England's Prudential Regulation Authority and the Financial Conduct Authority aim to discourage irresponsible risk-taking and short-term thinking. Under the new rules, senior managers could have bonuses recouped for up to 10 years in cases of misconduct. Martin Wheatley, the FCA's head, stated that these rules are part of a broader effort to establish an accountable culture in the financial sector. He emphasized their importance in rebuilding public trust in financial services. These rules, following a near-year-long consultation, apply to banks, building societies, and certain investment firms. Key rule changes include: However, the Bank of England clarified that buyouts of unpaid bonuses by new employers will not be prohibited, although managers will not be able to access the funds earlier than if they had remained at their previous company. Andrew Bailey, the Bank's deputy governor for prudential regulation and head of the PRA, explained: "Effective financial regulation involves creating appropriate incentives to encourage individuals to take greater responsibility for their actions. Our aim is that those in positions of responsibility are rewarded for behavior that fosters a culture of effective risk management, thereby promoting the safety and soundness of individual institutions."

The services admitted they could have done more to protect the child. A serious case review stated that risk factors were not adequately addressed. He acknowledged that there were instances during the baby's first three months and earlier during the mother's pregnancy when front-line staff could have acted differently. While they couldn't say for certain that the abuse wouldn't have happened, they believed many agencies could have responded better. The report revealed that the child's mother was vulnerable, having experienced neglect and violence in her own childhood. She was 17 when she became pregnant, and the father was 34. The baby also lived with its grandfather and a 14-year-old uncle, both with histories of violence and criminal behavior. Although the family was suspected of gang and drug-related activity, social workers did not take these risks seriously enough, according to the serious case review. The review found that a focus on meeting targets and deadlines led to insufficient critical analysis during assessments. Baby D was seen by doctors on April 29, 2013, and the possible rib fractures were believed to have occurred when the baby was one month old. The baby was placed in care, and the parents were arrested for suspected abuse or neglect. However, the criminal case was dropped due to issues with the key witness. Top wheelchair tennis players, including Grand Slam winners and Paralympians, competed in the inaugural Surbiton Wheelchair Tennis Tournament. This tournament served as a Wimbledon warm-up, allowing players to experience grass-court competition before heading to SW19, where three British players will defend their titles. Wheelchair tennis utilizes a standard court, balls, and rackets but features specialized wheelchairs and allows the ball to bounce twice on each side of the net. Lucy Shuker, a double Paralympic bronze medalist, took up wheelchair tennis after a motorbike accident left her paralyzed from the waist down. She has represented Great Britain at three Paralympics, winning bronze in doubles at both London 2012 and Rio 2016 alongside Jordanne Whiley. Shuker faced initial skepticism about her ability to compete but has overcome challenges by adapting her chair and straps to support her body. She credits the sport with making her stronger and improving her overall well-being. Leading wheelchair tennis players travel the globe to compete in tournaments, including the four Grand Slams held in Melbourne, Paris, Wimbledon, and New York. Alfie Hewett, Britain's reigning French Open men's singles champion, aims to defend his Wimbledon men's doubles title alongside Gordon Reid, the world number one. Hewett began playing in 2005 and made his Paralympic debut in Rio, where he won two silvers in doubles with Reid and singles, losing to Reid in the final. He recognizes the importance of the Surbiton tournament for gaining valuable grass-court experience before Wimbledon. Gustavo Fernandez, the reigning Australian Open champion and world number two, defeated Hewett on the first day of the Surbiton tournament, shortly after Hewett's French Open victory. The Tennis Foundation, which organized the event, hopes the warm-up tournament will give players a competitive edge at Wimbledon, where wheelchair events begin on July 13th.
Phil Everly died in Burbank, California, from complications related to lung disease, his wife Patti told the Los Angeles Times. She attributed the disease to a lifetime of smoking. Phil and his brother Don formed the Everly Brothers, a prominent pop group in the 1950s and early 1960s. Don expressed his grief in a statement to the Associated Press, saying he loved his brother deeply and was shocked by his passing. The Everly Brothers achieved success with numerous close-harmony hits, including "Wake Up Little Susie," "Cathy's Clown," "Bye Bye Love," and "All I Have To Do Is Dream." Duane Eddy, a friend and fellow rock pioneer, described the loss as "terrible" for everyone. Phil Everly died on Friday from chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, according to his son Jason. Rolling Stone magazine considers the Everly Brothers the most influential vocal duo in rock history. They grew up performing on their parents' country music radio show and achieved 19 Top 40 hits between 1957 and 1962. Their music influenced artists like the Beatles and the Beach Boys. Despite a decade-long estrangement following an onstage breakup in 1973, Phil later stated that their relationship remained strong. The Everly Brothers were inducted into the Rock 'n' Roll Hall of Fame in 1986 and received a lifetime achievement Grammy Award in 1997. Singer-songwriter Charlie Daniels expressed his condolences on Twitter, remembering the joy the Everly Brothers brought to music fans.

A demonstration took place outside Walsall Council House earlier. The council explained that regulations are necessary to prevent "places of remembrance from becoming disorganised and chaotic." A section of the cemetery is a special memorial for children, and the council has permitted edgings and ornaments to remain in that area. Mr. Matloob said, "What we're saying is 'you are discriminating against us and the wider community on age.' It should be one rule for everybody." Senior police officers in the state announced on Wednesday that they had arrested a militant after an armed confrontation. Local media reported that the militant had been overpowered by two civilians he had taken hostage and subsequently handed over to the police. A senior police official stated that the man confessed to being a Pakistani national who had crossed into India through the Line of Control, the de facto border between the two countries. Indian media outlets drew comparisons between Wednesday's incident and the capture of Qasab in 2008. Qasab was a member of a well-trained 10-member militant unit that arrived in Mumbai by sea on November 26th and attacked several locations, including the iconic Taj Mahal hotel. The attacks on the railway station, luxury hotels, and a Jewish cultural center resulted in 166 deaths, with nine gunmen also killed. Qasab was captured by a Mumbai policeman and executed in November 2012. Thursday's headlines in Indian newspapers focused on this comparison, with some referring to the arrested gunman as "Kasab II." Indian media outlets also highlighted official statements that the arrest demonstrates Pakistan's support for "anti-India" terrorist groups. "Intelligence officials say the capture of a Pakistani terrorist after an attack on a security convoy in Jammu and Kashmir's Udhampur today could lead to new evidence of repeated terror attacks against India organized across the border," a news report on the NDTV website stated. The Times of India viewed the arrest as a "big catch" for India, stating, "The captured terrorist is the first big catch after the 26/11 Mumbai attacker Ajmal Kasab was taken alive." However, an article on the Firstpost website argued that the "second Kasab's" arrest would not aid India's efforts in proving the involvement of Pakistan-based groups in terror attacks. "India will find it hard to prove before the international community that the 'second Kasab' was actually sent by the Pakistani establishment, just as it hasn't been able to prove before the world that the state was involved in sending Kasab across the border," it stated. Coverage of the arrest in Pakistan has been relatively low-key, with few media outlets mentioning the story. Geo News, one outlet that covered the story, criticized the Indian media for calling the arrested man a Pakistani national "without any concrete proof."
In September 2017, ministers plan to revamp the funding system for health worker training and implement tuition fees. The Royal College of Nursing and Unison express concern that this could discourage individuals from pursuing NHS careers and exacerbate staffing shortages. Student nurses, midwives, and other healthcare professionals like physiotherapists will be affected. Currently, they receive bursaries ranging from £4,500 to £5,500 if residing in London, along with an annual grant of £1,000 during their studies, and have their course fees covered. However, the government proposes eliminating these benefits and introducing university fees, aligning healthcare staff with other students. Ministers contend that this will increase the number of nurse students by approximately 10,000, as the number of applicants currently exceeds available places by a ratio of two to one due to a cap on enrollment. Unions, however, warn that this could deter potential students. Unison and the National Union of Students commissioned London Economics to analyze the potential impact, and their modeling suggests a 6.5% decrease in students starting courses, dropping from 31,000 to 29,000 annually. They argue that these courses differ significantly from traditional studies, as nurses spend half their time working in the NHS and lack extended holidays for paid work like other students. RCN general secretary Janet Davies states that fees could discourage individuals, particularly older students entering nursing later in life, who are crucial for workforce diversity. She considers this a risky move given the existing staff shortage. Earlier this month, the Public Accounts Committee reported a shortfall of approximately 50,000 front-line NHS staff. Davies advocates for a pause in the government's plans and a comprehensive risk assessment to evaluate the potential impact. Her warning coincides with nurses and other staff preparing for a rally in central London to protest the proposals. Unison head of health Christina McAnea expresses concern that the move could worsen an already challenging situation, potentially jeopardizing patient safety due to insufficient staffing. A Department of Health spokesperson states that the changes aim to increase training numbers and provide students with greater financial support through loans. They encourage the profession to submit concerns during the consultation period. The spokesperson emphasizes the need for more home-grown nurses to reduce reliance on expensive agency staff or overseas nurses.
