The Markit/CIPS service sector purchasing managers' index (PMI) increased to 54.9, up from 53.3 in the previous month. This figure, which represents growth in the sector, marked the first improvement in four months, though Markit noted the pace remained "relatively subdued". Following the release of the data, sterling reached its highest point against the euro since mid-August at 70.78 pence and rose to $1.5446 against the dollar. The survey revealed a five-month high in job growth among service companies, which comprise about three-quarters of activity in the UK's private sector. This positive service sector report followed strong readings from similar surveys of the manufacturing and construction sectors. Markit chief economist Chris Williamson stated that the improvement, coupled with the revival in hiring indicated by the three surveys, might encourage more policymakers to vote for an interest rate hike before the year's end. However, he also pointed out that dovish policymakers would likely focus on the ongoing absence of inflationary pressures in October, suggesting no immediate need to raise rates. Most economists anticipate the Bank of England will not increase rates until the second quarter of next year.

The drone, which carried munitions, was flying close to a de-confliction zone in southeastern Syria, near the borders of Jordan and Iraq. No coalition forces were injured. The "best preserved Bronze Age dwellings" in the UK, discovered at a Cambridgeshire quarry, date back to approximately 1,000-800 BC. Preserved in silt after falling into a river during a fire, these dwellings are the subject of an ongoing excavation. The two or three circular wooden houses, built on stilts, were part of a settlement partially destroyed by fire 3,000 years ago. The Must Farm quarry site near Whittlesey is considered "unique" by David Gibson of the Cambridge Archaeological Unit, as it has yielded timber remains, including stilts, roof structures, and walls, unlike most Bronze Age sites which only have post-holes. The fallen roof is believed to have covered the house contents, preserving them as they sank into the Fenland silt. Currently, archaeologists are examining a small section of one house interior. Early discoveries include a "very small, delicate wooden box that is mostly complete," described as a "thrilling" find. The box's contents are still intact and will be examined next week. An intact "fineware" pot and animal bones have also been unearthed, requiring meticulous cleaning and documentation. The cluster of fish and animal bones found inside may have been kitchen waste from the time period. The eight-month dig, half-way complete, aims to uncover the secrets of the site and its inhabitants. While still in the early stages of examining the house interior, the quality and quantity of discoveries have generated significant excitement among archaeologists. The site holds the potential for more uncommon household objects, including tools, cutlery, and even furniture. The excavation is jointly funded by Historic England and quarry owner Forterra.
Following a ruling that the police should cover the £60 million bill for damage to a Sony warehouse during the 2011 riots, Mayor Boris Johnson appealed to the Supreme Court. Insurance companies argue that the 1886 Riot Damage Act covers all losses resulting from the fire at the Enfield warehouse. The Supreme Court anticipates a final decision in a few months. The warehouse was attacked on August 8, 2011, during widespread riots sparked by the death of Mark Duggan in Tottenham. More than 1.5 million CDs were destroyed in what lawyers described as "the largest arson attack in Europe." At a High Court hearing, Mr. Justice Flaux determined that losses exceeded £60 million, including £9.8 million for lost profits and £1.6 million for lost rent. The Supreme Court has been informed that the Riot Damage Act mandates that "damage by riot" be paid for using police funds. The High Court ruled that the police should cover the damage but not consequential losses like lost profit and rent. However, insurers won a subsequent appeal at the Court of Appeal, which determined that the Metropolitan Police should cover all costs. Following this ruling, the BBC's legal correspondent, Clive Coleman, stated that the decision could impact other businesses affected by the riots. Mr. Johnson, whose office funds the police, requested a Supreme Court ruling after the two previous hearings.
A UK athlete and activist aimed to meet with Russia's Minister of Defence, Sergey Shoygu, who also heads the Russian Geographical Society, to discuss establishing a marine reserve around the Ross Sea. Despite global tensions, Mr. Pugh believes their conversation could facilitate a global agreement on the proposed protected zone. The Ross Sea, claimed by New Zealand, has been a launchpad for polar expeditions by famous explorers like Scott, Shackleton, and Amundsen. It is considered one of the last pristine regions in the world's oceans, but international disagreements have stalled its designation as a protected area. Mr. Pugh, the UN Environment Programme Patron of the Oceans, stated that this marked the first time Antarctica had been discussed at such a high level. He expressed optimism about the potential for dialogue and peace, emphasizing the importance of direct communication. However, he acknowledged the complexity of the situation, particularly given the strained relationships between the US, Russia, and the UK due to the crisis in Ukraine. Mr. Pugh recently set a new record by swimming further south in the Ross Sea than anyone before, describing the Bay of Whales as the most terrifying place he'd ever swum in. The average sea temperature was -1C, with an air temperature of -37C. The Ross Sea Marine Protected Area (MPA) has been proposed for four years but has failed to gain approval four times, with Russia and China vetoing the last proposal. The MPA, which would be the size of the UK, Germany, and France combined, requires consensus from 24 countries and the EU. Mr. Pugh hopes Antarctica can serve as a bridge for finding common ground, drawing inspiration from the 1959 Antarctic Treaty, which established the continent as a zone of peace and science during the Cold War. He believes that any progress made in Antarctica could have a positive ripple effect globally.

HP is suing Michael Lynch and Sushovan Hussain for alleged fraud, seeking $5.1 billion in damages. Separately, Lynch and former Autonomy management plan to sue HP for over £100 million, alleging false and negligent statements. HP acquired Autonomy in 2011 for $11 billion but later reduced the value of the purchase by three-quarters. Industry experts suggested potential issues with due diligence before the acquisition. HP aimed to expand its software presence through the purchase but claimed Autonomy misled them about its true value shortly after the deal. The Serious Fraud Office closed its investigation into Autonomy's sale earlier this year, finding insufficient evidence for a conviction and transferring jurisdiction to US authorities. Lynch and Hussain consistently deny any wrongdoing. HP filed its lawsuit in London's Chancery Division High Court. Lynch and his team plan to file their claim in the UK. Last summer, Autonomy filed papers in a San Francisco court accusing HP of mismanagement during the takeover. Sushovan Hussain, Autonomy's former CFO, alleged HP attempted to conceal its mismanagement of the integration. HP dismissed Hussain's complaint as "preposterous."

This is the latest protectionist trade action by US President Donald Trump. He aims to revise the North American Free Trade Agreement and has threatened tariffs on foreign products, including steel. He also withdrew from the 12-nation Trans Pacific Partnership agreement in January. South Korea is a major US trade partner, with the US exchanging approximately $144.6 billion in goods and services with the country last year. The Obama administration stated that the 2012 free trade agreement - considered the most significant in 16 years - would benefit US businesses. However, exports of American goods to South Korea have declined nearly 3% since the agreement's implementation to $42.3 billion. Simultaneously, imports from South Korea have risen by about 23%. "We can and must do better," stated US Trade Representative Robert Lighthizer. The US trade deficit for goods and services with Korea was approximately $17 billion last year, although separately, the US had a surplus for services exports. In a letter on Wednesday, the administration designated South Korea "an important ally and key trading partner" but expressed "real concerns about our significant trade imbalance." Mr. Trump, in an April interview, was more direct, calling it a "horrible deal" and a "one-way street." The administration has requested a joint session on the matter within 30 days. South Korean representatives were unavailable for comment.

Adele, a new mother, was named the most played independent artist at the ceremony, which took place at The Brewery in east London. The awards celebrate the achievements of artists signed to independent record labels in the UK. Collins, who is 53, was left with mobility, speech, and reading impairments following a stroke and two hemorrhages in 2005. Despite these challenges, he has made a remarkable recovery, returning to music production, recording, and publishing a book of his illustrations of British birds. At the event, Collins received an outstanding contribution award from his friends and collaborators, Vic Godard and Frankie and the Heartstrings. Daniel Miller, founder of Mute Records, was also presented with an honorary award. The event, hosted by BBC DJs Huw Stephens and Steve Lamacq, saw The Prodigy named best live act and Enter Shikari's "A Flash of Colour" awarded independent album of the year.

Pilar Carbonell, who oversees tourism in the Balearic Islands, including Mallorca and Ibiza, has urged Spain and the European Commission to implement an alcohol limit in airports and on flights. This proposal was presented in Brussels on Tuesday following several high-profile incidents involving alcohol abuse. One such incident involved a group of stag partygoers fighting on a Ryanair flight from Manchester to Palma, Majorca. Upon landing, three individuals were arrested, according to the Manchester Evening News. Carbonell stated that an alcohol limit would enhance security and address anti-social tourism. It remains unclear if this measure would apply solely to flights to the Balearic Islands or the entire European Union. The stated goal is to improve passenger safety and the safety of security personnel at airports and on planes in the islands, who frequently encounter intoxicated passengers.
Lee Goddard, a 40-year-old resident of Hambridge, admitted to opening the Slabgate sluice from December 20th to 23rd. Taunton Magistrates' Court learned that his actions directed an estimated 45,000 cubic meters of water towards Thorney village. Goddard stated his intention was to divert floodwater away from his property. While prosecutors acknowledged Goddard's actions raised water levels in Thorney, they were unable to confirm if this directly resulted in property damage. The Parrett Internal Drainage Board initiated the private prosecution against Goddard. His act of opening the sluice violated both a local by-law and the Land Drainage Act. Following the court proceedings, Nick Stevens, representing the drainage board, emphasized: "If such actions were repeated throughout Somerset, it would lead to chaos, and this is not how we manage flood risk in Somerset." Goddard was also required to cover £1,500 in prosecution costs and the expense of repairing the sluice gate. The 2013-14 winter months witnessed severe storms impacting large areas of the Somerset Levels, forcing the evacuation of hundreds of residents from their homes.

Known for blurring the lines between menswear and womenswear, Anderson previously streamed a menswear show on Grindr. Titled "Disobedient Bodies," the exhibition will run from March 18 to June 18, 2017.
The Court of Appeal upheld the eviction order against the protesting group, who are demonstrating against issues such as the war in Afghanistan. London Mayor Boris Johnson, who initiated legal action to remove the protesters, expressed his pleasure with the ruling. The protesters' lawyers stated they would not appeal the decision. Chris Knight, a protest organizer, emphasized that this is part of a larger movement and they intend to continue their protest elsewhere. The protesters established "Democracy Village" in central London's park, using tents and flags. Critics argue that the square has been vandalized and other groups have been prevented from protesting there. Concerns were also raised about public health due to a lack of toilet facilities. Lord Neuberger, delivering the ruling, explained that while the Crown owns the land, the Mayor of London has the authority to manage the square. He acknowledged the protesters' right to express their views and assemble for over two months, but stated that they have effectively excluded others from using the space. Mayor Johnson urged the protesters to respect the court's decisions and leave peacefully. He commended London's right to protest but criticized the protesters' treatment of the park, calling it "nauseating" and "serious damage to a world heritage site." He promised a peaceful eviction process, avoiding any resemblance to the G20 protests. Last month, the High Court issued eviction orders, but these were delayed pending an appeal. The protesters' lawyer argued that the mayor lacked the authority to evict them as he does not own the land, which belongs to the Queen. However, the mayor's lawyer countered that Parliament Square Gardens is a public space, and the judge's decision was justified. Colin Barrow, leader of Westminster City Council, expressed satisfaction with the ruling, stating that it will allow ordinary Londoners and visitors to use the square again.
A new report recommends shifting away from current guidelines that only suggest medication for high blood pressure above a certain threshold. Experts recognize the importance of lifestyle factors in lowering blood pressure. Published in The Lancet, the report highlights the established link between high blood pressure and an increased risk of heart disease and stroke. Current guidelines from England's National Institute for Health and Care Excellence recommend medication only when blood pressure reaches 140 mmHg. Even high-risk individuals, such as stroke survivors, are only monitored, not prescribed medication, until this point. A global team of experts now urges doctors to prioritize individual risk factors rather than rigid blood pressure thresholds. Analyzing over 100 large-scale trials involving 600,000 people from 1966 to 2015, they found that those at highest risk, including smokers with high cholesterol and individuals over 65 with diabetes, would benefit most from treatment, reducing their risk of heart attacks and strokes. The report also suggests that blood pressure levels could be lowered further than current targets once treatment is initiated. The study adds to growing evidence that patients may benefit from lowering their blood pressure regardless of their initial levels, either through lifestyle changes or medication. However, it shows that the benefit decreases as a person's starting blood pressure is lower. The authors caution against prescribing medication to everyone, emphasizing the need to weigh potential side effects against the likely benefits. Professor Liam Smeeth of the London School of Hygiene and Tropical Medicine acknowledged the importance of the findings for high-risk individuals but warned that not everyone can tolerate low blood pressure levels, and the potential drug side effects must be balanced against the potential benefits. Dr. Tim Chico, a heart specialist at the University of Sheffield, emphasized that medication is not the only solution. He highlighted that lifestyle changes, such as healthy eating, regular physical activity, reduced alcohol intake, and maintaining a healthy weight, can effectively and safely lower blood pressure.
An avalanche on Bidean Nam Bian at approximately 2 p.m. trapped a group of six climbers, three men and three women. One male climber alerted authorities. A female climber is in serious condition at a hospital in Fort William. The group was descending a south-side peak when the slope gave way near Church Door Buttress. Five climbers were swept down the mountain and buried in snow and ice. One female climber was found alive but suffered serious head injuries. The search was coordinated by the Glencoe mountain rescue team. The initial call came from two climbers who discovered a casualty in the snow. They then learned from a male survivor of the climbing party that more people were missing. John Grieve, leader of the Glencoe mountain rescue team, stated that the first call was from two other climbers who found someone injured near their climbing route. They initially believed it was a single casualty, but realized more people were missing after speaking with the surviving male climber. Prayers for the victims will be held during Sunday service at St Munda's Church. Reverend Moira Herkes described it as the worst accident in the area in many years, stating that it deeply affects everyone involved as people come to the area for enjoyment. Scotland's First Minister Alex Salmond called it an "appalling tragedy," expressing condolences to the families of those lost and offering support from the Scottish government. He also commended the efforts of the police and mountain rescue team.
Markus Reichel confessed to providing over 200 documents to the CIA over a four-year period in exchange for at least €80,000. These documents included the real and alias names and addresses of German agents working overseas. The 32-year-old was also convicted of treason for supplying three documents to the Russian intelligence service. Reichel had been employed at the Federal Intelligence Service (BND) since 2007, working in the mailroom where he had access to classified information. He also held clearance for handling top-secret material, according to the Sueddeutsche Zeitung. During his trial, which began in November, Reichel stated that he shared the intelligence for both the excitement and the recognition. He felt undervalued at the BND, while the CIA offered him more respect. His department was responsible for protecting soldiers stationed abroad, as reported by Reuters. Between 2008 and 2014, he received €10,000 to €20,000 annually in cash for the secrets he divulged at covert meetings in Austria. Reichel would copy sensitive documents at work, take them home to scan, and then send them to his handler, as reported by AFP. After several years of this activity, he desired a change and offered his services to the Russian consulate in Munich. However, his email containing classified documents was intercepted, leading to his exposure. In his final court statement, Reichel expressed remorse and sought forgiveness, according to Sueddeutsche Zeitung. His arrest in 2014 occurred amidst strained relations between the US and Germany following leaks by former CIA agent Edward Snowden that revealed the extent of US surveillance activities in Germany.

The oil company has announced it will now utilize existing equipment on land and offshore to process gas from the west of Shetland. This decision comes after the company had planned to build a gas "sweetening" plant over the next three years, a project that would have employed approximately 300 workers during construction and created around 30 full-time jobs. Local contractor DITT Construction, responsible for preparatory work at the terminal, will complete its involvement next month. Currently, the firm has 20 employees working on the development, with about a dozen facing potential redundancy. Shetland councillor Andrea Manson expressed disappointment but not surprise at the news, stating that with current oil prices, BP is likely seeking cost reductions, potentially making the gas sweetening plant a victim of these efforts. New rules, approved at the Football Association's Annual General Meeting on Wednesday, will impact players in the top eight tiers of English football. Currently, players are prohibited from betting on competitions involving their club. The new rule expands this to prevent players from sharing inside information with someone who uses it for betting. Andros Townsend of Tottenham, Cameron Jerome (who was on loan at Crystal Palace from Stoke last season), and Dan Gosling of Newcastle (who will join Bournemouth next season) are among those who have violated existing betting regulations. Townsend was fined £18,000 by the FA in June 2013, Jerome £50,000 last August, and Gosling £30,000 in March. The new regulations, effective August 1st, encompass betting on all global matches and "football-related matters" such as transfers, managerial appointments, and team selection. These rules will apply to players in the Premier League, Championship, Leagues One and Two, Conference, and top Northern, Southern, and Isthmian leagues.
A climate change march organized by Stop Climate Chaos Scotland took place as part of a global campaign preceding the United Nations meeting in Paris. The campaign is calling on politicians to establish plans for reducing carbon emissions and to provide support for developing nations disproportionately affected by climate change. Marchers assembled at The Meadows before proceeding to the Ross Bandstand in Princes Street Gardens for a rally, braving rainy weather while wearing bright colors and uniting under the slogan "Climate, Justice and Jobs." Organizers estimated that approximately 5,000 people attended the event, which also included Environment Minister Aileen McLeod. Minister McLeod expressed Scotland's desire for an ambitious and equitable climate treaty at the conference, highlighting the Scottish government's "high ambition" for climate change, aiming for a 42% reduction in emissions by 2020 compared to 1990 levels. Tom Ballantine, chairman of Stop Climate Chaos Scotland, a coalition of 60 organizations, emphasized the collective responsibility of Scotland and the UK, which benefited greatly from the fossil fuel era, to address the global climate crisis. International leaders will convene in Paris next week to negotiate a deal on reducing greenhouse gas emissions. Patrick Harvie MSP, co-convener of the Scottish Greens, asserted that Scotland can lead the transition from fossil fuels to a low-carbon economy. He stressed the urgency of a bold agreement in Paris to protect society, emphasizing the disproportionate impact of rising global temperatures on the world's poorest populations and the responsibility of developed nations for the pollution causing climate change. Harvie reiterated the scientific consensus that leaving fossil fuels in the ground is essential to mitigate the damage caused by climate change. Sarah Boyack, Scottish Labour's environmental justice spokeswoman, pledged that her party in government at Holyrood would implement regulatory changes to make energy-efficient homes a reality, addressing both climate change and fuel poverty. She called on the SNP government to prioritize tackling climate change and provide political support for low-carbon heat and power, enabling Scottish companies to become leaders in this globally important technology.
A report on the state of Britain's dormice reveals that hazel dormice are extinct in 17 English counties. Researchers analyzed over 100,000 records collected across the UK over 25 years, concluding that the dormouse is now vulnerable to extinction in Britain. Since 1998, trained volunteers have been gathering data on the tiny hazel dormouse (Muscardinus avellanarius) as part of one of the longest-running small mammal monitoring projects globally. These creatures primarily inhabit hedgerows and woodlands, constructing ball-like nests from bark during summer and hibernating on or near the ground between October and May. They are exceptionally difficult to find, with only four dormice inhabiting an area the size of two football fields. A telltale sign of their presence is a nibbled hazelnut. The report indicates that they are more elusive than ever before, with a nearly 40% population decline over the past 16 years. Populations are now restricted to the Welsh borders and southern England, with no records in Scotland or Northern Ireland. Ian White, the dormouse officer at the People's Trust for Endangered Species, who has monitored the species for a decade, describes the decline as "dramatic." He states, "Dormice have existed for 40 million years, but their future in Britain is now uncertain, and urgent action is needed to ensure their long-term survival." While dormice were present in 49 English counties in 1885, they are now known to inhabit 32 counties, excluding those where reintroduction efforts have taken place. Mr. White attributes their decline to several key factors, including habitat loss due to woodland and hedgerow removal, habitat fragmentation hindering dispersal, and changes in farmland and woodland management making survival more challenging. They are also susceptible to weather fluctuations, particularly wetter springs and summers, which make foraging difficult, and warmer winters that disrupt successful hibernation. Despite the concerning data, Mr. White expresses optimism, stating, "This is a somber tale, but my personal experience is that there is a growing interest in finding solutions and a lot of public enthusiasm for helping." Efforts have been made to reintroduce hazel dormice to the wild, with over 850 animals released at 26 sites, including the latest release at Aysgarth in Wensleydale in June. While five of these release sites have seen population decline, others have shown signs of success, such as breeding and dispersal to new

His team has struggled this season, winning only four of fourteen RaboDirect Pro 12 games and losing all six European matches. Bradley, 50, expressed disappointment with the team's performance. Defense coach Billy McGinty has also resigned. His responsibilities will be shared by Bradley, who will coach until May, and forwards coach Neil Back. Bradley took over as head coach in May 2011 after Nick Scrivener returned to Australia. Scrivener had served as interim head coach following Rob Moffat's dismissal in January 2011. Moffat replaced Andy Robinson, who left to coach Scotland in 2009. Bradley led Edinburgh to the Heineken Cup semi-finals last season, defeating Toulouse in the quarterfinals. He expressed gratitude for the support he received during his tenure and emphasized the importance of building a strong club identity. Scottish Rugby has begun the search for a new coaching team for next season. Chief executive Mark Dodson acknowledged Bradley and McGinty's contributions, particularly their role in last year's Heineken Cup run. He stated that this season's results fell short of expectations and that a fresh coaching team is needed.

BAE Systems will conduct 17 autonomous flights using a modified Jetstream 31 aircraft. While human pilots will manage takeoff and landing, the aircraft will fly autonomously for 300 miles between Lancashire and Inverness. This trial will evaluate the aircraft's piloting software and sensor systems, which monitor weather conditions and other aircraft. The Jetstream will utilize satellite data and onboard identification systems to track radio signals from other planes' transponders for navigation. Additionally, it is equipped with a camera capable of detecting other aircraft, even those not broadcasting warning signals. This camera also scans the surrounding airspace for adverse weather, enabling the aircraft to adjust its route to avoid turbulence and other challenging conditions. The autonomous flight will follow a predetermined path through less congested airspace at approximately 15,000 feet and is estimated to take about 90 minutes. BAE Systems is collaborating with the UK's National Air Traffic Services to assess how well the autonomous aircraft interacts with human-piloted aircraft in shared airspace. The trials aim to pave the way for operating unmanned systems in a controlled environment within the UK.
The body of a 20-year-old woman, missing since April, was discovered at a location provided by a 32-year-old man. Police report that the man confessed to abandoning the body and suspect he killed her, though he has not been charged with murder. This case is expected to exacerbate discontent in Okinawa regarding the large US military presence and crimes committed by personnel. It occurs a week before US President Barack Obama's visit to Japan for a G7 summit. Police identified the man, Kenneth Franklin Shinzato, as a contractor at the US Kadena Air Base. Prime Minister Shinzo Abe expressed strong anger, stating, "I have no words to express, considering how the family feels. We urge the US side to take thorough measures to prevent the recurrence of such events." Foreign Minister Fumio Kishida summoned US ambassador Caroline Kennedy to formally protest, conveying his strong indignation at the incident. Ms. Kennedy assured full cooperation with Okinawan police and the Japanese government, pledging doubled efforts to prevent future occurrences. However, Japan's ambassador to the US, Kenichiro Sasae, emphasized that this tragic event should not overshadow the alliance's fundamental objectives. The US currently has approximately 50,000 military personnel in Japan, with half stationed in Okinawa, as part of the postwar security arrangement. Prime Minister Abe recently agreed to suspend construction for the relocation of a US Marine Corps base within Okinawa, following years of local opposition. Many residents desire the base's complete removal, citing environmental concerns and associating US bases with accidents and crime.
The F-Rating, introduced in 2014 by Holly Tarquini, director of the Bath Film Festival, has been adopted by over 40 UK cinemas and festivals. IMDb's Col Needham praised the F-Rating as a great way to highlight women both on screen and behind the camera. Tarquini stated that over 21,800 films have been tagged with the F-Rating on IMDb, which receives over 250 million monthly visitors. Tarquini explained that the F-Rating aims to spark conversations about women's representation both on and off screen. She expressed excitement about new organizations joining the campaign to shed light on the excellent work women are doing in film and the film industry's lagging progress in providing equal opportunities for women. Tarquini emphasized that the ultimate goal is for the F-Rating to become obsolete when 50% of stories on screen are told by and about women. The F-Rating is a classification for any film that meets the following criteria: The Barbican Cinema in London is the latest adopter of the campaign. Curator Gali Gold described the rating as a simple and effective way to highlight films where stories are told by and about women and to address the issue of gender imbalance in the film industry. Anna Navas from Plymouth Arts Centre added the F-Rating as soon as she learned about it, finding it a clear, fun, and accessible way to emphasize women on screen. The F-Rating was inspired by US cartoonist Alison Bechdel's 1985 comic strip "Dykes to Watch Out For," which featured two women discussing movies. The comic became known as the "Bechdel Test," with the characters deciding a film should include at least two women who talk to each other about something besides a man. Some films meet all three criteria with significant female characters who are also written and directed by women.
Many Labour MPs view Momentum as a separate faction within the party, a platform for the far-left, and a tool to remove those who oppose Corbyn's leadership. Momentum, however, is not easily characterized as a hard-left group. Their members, like Steve, Samira, Nadine, and Roland, are united by their enthusiasm for Corbyn's leadership. Steve, a self-described "middle-aged BBC Radio 4 listener," joined the Labour Party immediately after Corbyn's victory. Samira also joined after his win, stating that for the first time, she felt she had a leader she could support and trust. Momentum, with approximately 60,000 supporters, operates primarily through social media and has around 50 local groups across the UK. It is free to join and aims to create a mass movement for progressive change. However, its open membership has raised concerns about potential infiltration by far-left groups like the Socialist Workers Party (SWP). Labour MP Emma Reynolds views Momentum as a parallel organization to the Labour Party and questions its intentions, particularly regarding its potential involvement with far-left groups. She expresses fears that Momentum's ultimate goal is to remove MPs from the party. Some far-left groups, like the Trade Unionist and Socialist Coalition (TUSC), have expressed interest in collaborating with Momentum. Dave Nellist, TUSC chairman and former Labour MP expelled for his Militant membership, believes Momentum was created to provide Corbyn with an alternative power base to advance a left-wing agenda and "democratize" the Labour Party. This would involve making the party conference the supreme policy-making body, restoring Clause 4 of the party's constitution, committing to public ownership of industry, and re-introducing mandatory re-selection of MPs, a tactic seen by opponents as a means to purge "moderate" MPs. The issue of re-selection has caused significant anxiety among Labour MPs. Despite Nadine's insistence that Momentum is not targeting sitting MPs, she acknowledges that opportunities may arise due to boundary changes and MPs retiring. Momentum clearly aims to bring about change, both by creating a broad anti-austerity movement outside the Labour Party and by transforming the Labour Party itself into a more Momentum-like entity, a Corbyn Labour Party. This prospect is what frightens many Labour MPs.
Carwyn Jones stated that the Welsh government will continue with the £600 million Metro project but acknowledged it might take longer to complete. He expressed uncertainty about the UK government's commitment to protect EU funding, essential for the project. Andrew RT Davies, the Welsh Conservative leader, criticized Jones for using Brexit as a cover-up. Subsequently, Economy Secretary Ken Skates informed AMs that construction on the Metro will commence in 2019, with services starting as soon as possible, contingent upon a suitable solution. The Metro aims to improve train services, accelerate bus travel, and introduce light rail or tram services in Cardiff and the surrounding valleys. Last year, £600 million was allocated for the current phase of the Metro, including £125 million from the UK government and £150 million in European regional development funding. In his statement outlining government priorities, Jones stated that despite uncertainty surrounding EU funding for the Metro project, the Welsh government will proceed with its development. He acknowledged that the project might not proceed as initially planned, requiring alternative funding models and a potentially extended timeframe. However, he emphasized its significance as a signal to businesses that Wales remains open for investment. Jones had intended to publish a detailed program for the government's five-year term before the summer recess but postponed it until September due to Brexit. He reiterated his demand for the UK government to guarantee replacing the approximately £600 million in annual EU funding received by Wales. Without this assurance, he warned of a substantial gap in future budgets. A fire broke out in a rented property in Mill Hill at approximately 4:30 GMT on Saturday. The blaze spread to two adjacent family homes. While no injuries were reported, the rented house was completely destroyed, and the other two sustained damage. Twitter is shutting down Vine, its six-second looping video app. The company did not provide a specific reason for the closure but announced earlier Thursday that it would be laying off 9% of its workforce due to slow growth. Twitter acquired Vine in 2012 for $30 million before its official launch. While the company integrated a video feature into its main platform and acquired the livestreaming app Periscope, Vine remained a separate entity. Twitter stated that the Vine app, website, and user-generated content will remain accessible for the time being. Users will be notified before any changes are made to the app or website. An analyst at IHS Markit, Ian Fogg, noted that Twitter aimed to constrain communication with Vine, similar to its 140-character limit on tweets. However, Vine struggled to keep pace with innovations from competitors like Snapchat and Facebook. Vine, launched in 2013, seemed like a natural extension of Twitter's concise communication style. However, it faced stiff competition from Snapchat and Instagram's short-form video features. With Twitter downsizing its workforce, maintaining a separate app for Vine became less justifiable. It remains unclear if Twitter plans to incorporate Vine-like features into its main app. Despite having a dedicated fanbase and prominent Vine creators, Vine's content was often shared more widely on other platforms, potentially contributing to its decline.
In 2016-17, Cardiff Foodbank distributed 14,568 three-day food supplies, with over a third going to children. Across Wales, food bank usage increased by 11%, with 43% of recipients citing benefit issues as the primary cause. While the Department for Work and Pensions acknowledged the complexity of the reasons behind food bank reliance, they cautioned against attributing it to a single factor. Statistics from the Trussell Trust network, which includes Cardiff Foodbank, revealed a nationwide rise in emergency food parcel distributions. In Wales, this figure climbed from 85,656 in 2015-16 to 95,190 in 2016-17. The top reasons for food bank referrals were benefit delays, low income, and benefit changes. Cardiff Foodbank expressed concern over the impact of Universal Credit, particularly the six-week or longer wait for the first payment, which they believe could contribute to debt, mental health issues, and rent arrears. The trust praised the collaborative approach taken in some areas, like Torfaen, where local authorities engaged food banks and charities in working groups prior to Universal Credit implementation. Wales was the first nation to complete Universal Credit rollout for single jobseekers and is now transitioning to couples and families. The Trussell Trust's director for devolved nations, Tony Graham, expressed interest in Scotland's plan to mitigate Universal Credit's effects through fortnightly payments and direct housing payments to landlords. He also indicated a desire to discuss similar strategies with the Welsh Government. Helen Bull, Cardiff Foodbank's partnership and fundraising manager, highlighted the continued increase in three-day emergency food supply requests in Cardiff, emphasizing that anyone could face food insecurity due to unforeseen circumstances such as illness, benefit delays, or unexpected bills. A Department for Work and Pensions spokesperson reiterated the complexity of food bank usage, avoiding attributing it to a single cause. They emphasized their close collaboration with local authorities to provide support, including budgeting assistance, benefit advances, and direct rent payments to landlords.

Parking fees at Glan Clwyd Hospital in Bodelwyddan have been eliminated following the expiration of an external contract. This decision aligns with a Welsh Assembly Government commitment to provide free parking at nearly all NHS hospitals in Wales by the end of 2011. Hospitals managed by private companies were required to lower costs until their contracts ended. At Glan Clwyd, the change coincides with a new parking system designed to improve parking convenience for patients and visitors. Parking for patients and visitors will remain in the front of the hospital, near the main public entrances, with additional parking available in the car park on the right side of the access road. Designated parking areas for renal, cancer, and maternity units will persist without time limits or charges. Accessible parking arrangements for disabled individuals will remain unchanged. New signs will guide the public to designated parking areas. The remaining parking spaces will be allocated to staff. A hospital spokesperson stated, "We recognize that the new system will require time to fully implement. Our immediate focus is to minimize disruption to patients and visitors during this transition."

Tidal Lagoon Power revealed to BBC Wales that it received a letter from Energy Secretary Amber Rudd on the same day an independent review of the Swansea Bay tidal lagoon project was ordered. Chief executive Mark Shorrock stated that the letter indicated the UK government is open to spreading the project's cost over 90 years instead of the previously proposed 35 years. The UK government declined to comment on the letter. The Swansea lagoon is planned to be the first of six tidal lagoons around the UK coastline, potentially generating 8% of the country's electricity over their 120-year lifespan. The company also aims to develop lagoons at five other UK sites, including three in Wales. Shorrock expressed confidence in the project's viability and believes the review will demonstrate the economic sense of tidal energy for the UK. He stated that the review will validate the availability of affordable, home-grown power from the Severn and Liverpool Bay tides. The review is expected to conclude in the autumn. Construction has already been delayed by a year due to stalled negotiations regarding public funding. The company had requested a decision on a guaranteed price for the energy produced by next month. However, the review, commencing this spring, will not provide any definitive answers before late 2016 at the earliest. Energy Minister Lord Bourne emphasized the review's importance in determining the cost-effectiveness of tidal lagoons and their potential impact on energy bills, both presently and in the long term.

A new report indicates that increasing prices by 20% or more leads to decreased consumption and improved nutrition. While the global health group has previously recommended reducing sugar intake, they haven't explicitly supported tax measures. Several countries, including Mexico and Hungary, already impose taxes on added sugar products, and South Africa will introduce a sugar tax next year, making it the only African nation to do so. The World Health Organization (WHO) aims to reduce consumption of "free sugars," which they believe will lower the occurrence of obesity, diabetes, and tooth decay. "Free sugars" encompass all types of added sugars in the diet, excluding those naturally found in fruit and milk. Dr. Francesco Branca, the WHO's nutrition director, stated that nutritionally, people don't require any added sugar in their diet and recommended keeping sugar intake below 10% of total calorie intake, ideally below 5%. However, the group suggests that taxation or other financial measures should be applied only to items with healthier alternatives. These findings are based on a meeting of global experts and a review of existing evidence. The report also found that government subsidies for fruits and vegetables, which lower prices, can increase consumption. Additionally, the same tax measures implemented on sugary drinks appear to be effective for other sugary foods, as well as those high in saturated fats, trans fats, and salt.
A decorated gourd, believed to hold a handkerchief stained with King Louis XVI's blood after his execution in 1793, has been analyzed. Scientists who sequenced the DNA from the sample say it's unlikely to belong to the monarch. The study, published in Scientific Reports, suggests the gourd and its contents may be a 18th-century forgery created to capitalize on revolutionary souvenirs. Professor Carles Lalueza-Fox from the Institute of Evolutionary Biology in Barcelona, Spain, explained that during the French Revolution, access to the guillotine was likely easier when less significant individuals were executed. "They thought nobody was going to recognize whether the blood was from the king or not," he said. The blood-stained cloth was kept inside a hollowed gourd adorned with revolutionary imagery and inscribed with the claim that Maximilien Bourdaloue dipped his handkerchief in Louis XVI's blood after his beheading on January 21st. Early forensic tests had hinted at a possible royal connection, but this new research casts doubt on the sample's authenticity. The scientists examined sections of the sample's genome linked to physical traits. Portraits of Louis XVI depict him with blue eyes, but the DNA suggests a much higher probability of brown eyes. Professor Lalueza-Fox stated, "The probability of this guy having blue eyes is very low - it is about 3%." Historical records, including letters from Louis XVI's wife Marie-Antoinette, also mention the king's exceptional height. While the average height at the time was around 167cm (5ft 5in), Louis XVI was believed to be at least 185cm (6ft 1in) tall. The genetic markers for extreme height were not found in the sample. Genetic ancestry analysis further supports the conclusion that the sample is not from Louis XVI. The DNA indicates roots primarily in France and Italy, while Louis XVI's ancestors hailed from Germany and Poland. The team concludes that the likelihood of the sample belonging to Louis XVI is extremely low. This finding aligns with a previous study from last year that compared the sample to two living relatives of Louis XVI, also raising questions about its authenticity.

Eighteen individuals have been convicted of forcing girls in Newcastle to engage in sexual acts. The convicted individuals were primarily British-born and came from Pakistani, Bangladeshi, Indian, Iraqi, Iranian, and Turkish backgrounds. Ms. Rudd expressed her satisfaction that justice had been served and emphasized that political and cultural sensitivities should never hinder the prevention and uncovering of such crimes. She highlighted the government's investment in enabling police to identify and prosecute offenders, leading to increased police activity and a rise in prosecutions and convictions. Operation Sanctuary, initiated by Northumbria Police in 2013 to investigate claims of sexual abuse against young girls and women, resulted in convictions for offenses including rape, conspiracy to incite prostitution, and supplying drugs. Northumbria Police emphasized the importance of societal willingness to engage in this discussion. Earlier, Labour MP Sarah Champion argued that the fear of being labeled racist prevents authorities from investigating the underlying reasons for child abuse cases. Ms. Champion, representing Rotherham where at least 1,400 children were found to have been exploited between 1997 and 2013, asserted that exploring potential "cultural issues" in such cases is not racist. She stated on BBC Radio 4's Today programme that gang-related child sexual exploitation predominantly involves Pakistani men who repeatedly engage in these offenses.
Yorkshire's four batting bonus points keep all three title contenders in the running for Friday's championship. Somerset, led by captain Chris Rogers, declared at lunch after reaching 313-5 with Rogers scoring his second century of the match. Nottinghamshire, facing a target of 541, were bowled out for 215, losing by 325 runs. Rogers, who scored his 76th first-class century, announced his retirement after the game. Somerset now await the outcome of other matches, as a win for either Yorkshire or Middlesex at Lord's would deny them the title. Yorkshire, thanks to Tim Bresnan's 142 not out, secured the same bonus point tally as Somerset, meaning a victory would secure their third consecutive championship. At Taunton, Notts openers Steven Mullaney and Jake Libby put on 48 runs before losing three wickets for seven runs, including two run-outs. Roelof van der Merwe dismissed Michael Lumb lbw, and Somerset accelerated towards victory when Jack Leach removed Chris Read and Brett Hutton in quick succession. Billy Root's unbeaten 66 was not enough to prevent Somerset's sixth Championship win of 2016. Earlier, Ryan Davies and Peter Trego supported Rogers' century with swift half-centuries as Somerset scored at over six runs per over in the morning session. The final day of the season will also decide which team joins Nottinghamshire in relegation to Division Two. Warwickshire, Lancashire, and Hampshire are battling to avoid the drop. Warwickshire are in a strong position after reducing Lancashire to 28-3 in their second innings, requiring another 319 runs to win. However, a Hampshire win would relegate the loser of the Warwickshire-Lancashire match. A draw at Edgbaston and a Hampshire win would see Warwickshire relegated, while an unlikely Lancashire victory would mean a draw would be enough for Hampshire to stay up at Warwickshire's expense. In that scenario, both Hampshire and Warwickshire would finish on 160 points, with the same number of wins, but Hampshire would remain in Division One due to fewer defeats.
William Thomas (WT) Cosgrave, Ireland's first Prime Minister, served from 1922 to 1932. He led the new nation through its difficult early years after the Irish War of Independence. Cosgrave's headstone was vandalized at Goldenbridge Cemetery in Inchicore, along with 12 other headstones dating back to the Victorian and pre-Victorian eras. This is not the first time his grave has been targeted; it was also vandalized in 2014. Cosgrave came to power during the Irish Civil War, a conflict between those who supported the 1921 Anglo-Irish Treaty and those who desired a fully independent republic. He had fought for independence in the 1916 Easter Rising but later supported the treaty and defended the new Free State. Under his leadership, 77 republican prisoners were executed during the Civil War, more than the 16 rebels executed by the British after the 1916 rebellion. Cosgrave's legacy is complex, with some viewing him as ruthless and others as a strong leader and architect of Irish independence. He died in 1965 and was buried at Goldenbridge Cemetery after a state funeral. His son, Liam Cosgrave, who also served as Prime Minister, visited the grave to see the damage, and the family released a statement calling the incident "regrettable." The cemetery is maintained by the Glasnevin Trust, whose chairman, John Green, noted that the 2014 attack coincided with the publication of Cosgrave's biography, suggesting a possible connection. However, he stated that the motive for the latest incident is unclear. While the trust has no evidence of political motivation, a child's grave connected to the Easter Rising was also targeted, leading the trust to classify the incident as vandalism. Green highlighted recent anti-social behavior at the cemetery, including drug use and dealing, and local opposition to plans to increase public access. The trust intends to request government assistance for repairs due to the national significance of the Cosgrave plot. Police are investigating the incident as "criminal damage" and have made no arrests.

He admitted to feeling overwhelmed by emotion, attributing it to the fans and his loved ones. Despite the loss, North expressed pride in his team's effort, noting they faced a formidable opponent and never gave up. He highlighted the team's spirit as something he admired.

The mayor lacks the authority to ban pedicabs, so he is pursuing government legislation to achieve this. He also aims to decrease the number of minicabs, a move supported by the Licensed Taxi Drivers Association. The association attributes the surge in private hire drivers in London in 2014, reaching an estimated 13,000, partly to the popularity of affordable ride-hailing apps like Uber. The mayor has advocated for a pedicab ban since December 2012, citing their congestion of city roads and consistent failure to ensure passenger safety. Pedicabs, also known as rickshaws, are a common sight in London's West End, attracting tourists and some businessmen. Despite the presence of responsible operators, authorities have long sought to eliminate them due to concerns about safety and exorbitant fares. In 2002, black cab drivers unsuccessfully attempted to ban pedicabs in court. Pedicabs operate under a loophole in the 1869 metropolitan public carriage act, classified as stage carriages rather than Hackney cabs, allowing them to solicit fares. To ban pedicabs, the mayor must amend this legislation, a process that will likely be time-consuming and face legal challenges.
Lindsay Newell, from Burton-upon-Trent, was given a two-year conditional discharge for neglecting the welfare of several emaciated animals. She was also banned from owning certain animals and ordered to pay £2,000 in costs. The judge ruled that the neglect was unintentional and dismissed 25 charges of failing to feed the animals. The court determined that Newell did not monitor the weight loss of six animals at Burton Wildlife Rescue and Animal Centre in Etwall, Derbyshire. One of these animals, a pig named Babe, who had been rescued from a Derby council house and received national attention, died in front of RSPCA officers during a police raid in November 2012. District Judge David Taylor stated that Newell, a 27-year-old veterinary nurse from Lincoln Road, Stapenhill, took on too many animals, putting herself under financial strain. He emphasized that her actions were not deliberate but a result of her desire to help animals. Newell had spent £300 a week on food and bedding for the animals from her own earnings, but she subsequently lost her job. Her defense lawyer, Nigel Weller, said that Newell now aims to dedicate her life to running the sanctuary. Newell was disqualified from owning sheep, goats, pigs, and equine animals for five years. The rescue center remains open, housing hundreds of animals including cats, pigeons, chickens, rabbits, and crows. The RSPCA reported that the investigation and court proceedings cost £20,000. Newell is required to pay £2,000 in costs.
Ireland's Six Nations title defense kicks off with strong winds predicted and Wales preparing for an aerial battle. James returns to the team for the first time in over five years, and Edwards has been diligently working with him. Edwards stated, "Tom is the player I've focused on most this week because he's the new player." He added, "The wind is expected to blow in, so we've been practicing our aerial game. It's something we consistently work on because it's a crucial element of modern rugby. The understanding between the back three and their cohesiveness is extremely important." Wales suffered a decisive defeat in their last Six Nations visit to Aviva Stadium in 2014, where Ireland's kicking game proved instrumental. However, Joe Schmidt's team will be missing several key players, including Rob Kearney, a specialist in chasing kicks. Wales has fewer injury concerns, but their back three lacks extensive experience, with full-back Anscombe and wing James having only 13 combined international appearances. Anscombe participated in the 2015 World Cup, while James' last international match was against New Zealand in November 2010. Edwards mentioned that like any outside back, James has dedicated considerable time to defensive training. He emphasized, "I work more with wingers because defending on the wing is incredibly challenging. If they make a mistake, it's highly noticeable because it either leads to a line break or a try, so the concentration level in that position is crucial. That's why Tom's return to the team has meant I've spent more time with him." Despite Ireland's injuries, Edwards doesn't believe Wales will be favored to win the match or the tournament. He stated, "I think it's a toss-up game. Ireland were strong favorites before Christmas, and that's what makes the Six Nations so fantastic—there's no clear favorite."
William Stewart, a doctor, testified at the trial of Steven Jackson and Michelle Higgins in Glasgow. He stated that Kimberley MacKenzie's brain showed bruising, bleeding, and swelling on the right side. Stewart examined the brain on December 10th, 2015, and observed a bone fragment that appeared to have been embedded during impact. He estimated MacKenzie's survival time after the initial head injury to be around an hour, possibly less. He stated that with medical intervention, the head injury could have been survivable. The defense argued that the brain changes could be attributed to decay, suggesting MacKenzie died shortly after being injured. Stewart refuted this, stating that the brain did not mask the changes. Jackson and Higgins deny murdering and dismembering MacKenzie in Montrose on October 27th, 2015. The prosecution previously presented evidence that MacKenzie had been struck on the head at least eleven times and stabbed approximately forty times. The couple is accused of using a hammer or similar object to repeatedly strike MacKenzie on her head, neck, and body, and then stabbing her with a knife. They are also accused of attempting to conceal the crime by dismembering her body and hiding the parts in bins throughout Montrose. The trial continues before Judge Lady Rae.

Concerns regarding the inquiry's progress and direction prompted his decision, although he had no other plans to leave. The inquiry declined to comment on specifics, and Mr. Fisher chose not to provide a statement. Mr. Fisher had served as the first junior counsel, the second-highest ranking barrister on the inquiry, and previously worked on two high-profile investigations: one concerning Lord Janner and another regarding alleged abuse in Westminster. He informed the inquiry of his intention to step down over two months prior. Mr. Fisher's departure followed approximately two weeks after Dame Lowell Goddard, the inquiry's third chair, resigned and days after Newsnight revealed that the inquiry had been notified of an alleged sexual assault by Ben Emmerson QC, the inquiry's lead counsel. The alleged victim did not wish to formally complain about Mr. Emmerson's alleged actions in a lift, but the inquiry faced criticism for failing to investigate broader concerns about his behavior and for permitting him to resign but continue working for two months at a daily rate of £1,700. The BBC understands that Matrix, the legal chambers to which Mr. Emmerson belongs, has initiated its own independent inquiry into the allegations, to be conducted by an external individual. Mr. Emmerson vehemently denies allegations of sexual assault, bullying, or any other misconduct. Mark Frost, a 70-year-old former English teacher, confessed to abusing nine children in Thailand between 2009 and 2012. He pleaded guilty to 23 charges at the Old Bailey, having previously admitted 22 charges last year. Six of the charges stem from his abuse of two pupils at a Worcestershire school during the 1980s and 1990s. Frost is scheduled to be sentenced on Thursday. Previously known as Andrew Tracey, Frost was apprehended following a global investigation involving the UK's National Crime Agency (NCA), as well as authorities in Thailand, Spain, and the Netherlands. Frost sexually abused impoverished Asian boys, enticing them with money, sweets, computer games, and swimming in his pool. He fled Thailand to avoid prosecution but was extradited from Spain last year after Dutch police uncovered his activities on the computer of a man in the Netherlands. Frost filmed the boys, aged 10 to 14, performing sexual acts on webcam, having them give thumbs-up signs and make heart gestures. The court also heard details of Frost's abuse of two pupils at a Worcestershire school in the late 1980s and 1990s. He engaged in sexual acts with the boys in a school storeroom, during breaks, at his home, and at a car park in Woking, Surrey. One of these victims has since passed away, while the other stated in a statement that he had lived in fear every day since the abuse. Frost admitted charges including multiple rapes, sexual assaults, inciting children to engage in sexual activity, and creating indecent images. Prosecutor Sally-Ann Hales revealed that images and web chats seized from Frost's Dutch contact featured 15 boys, nine of whom were identified and interviewed. The boys' mothers expressed their sorrow upon learning that the man they considered a "kind and generous foreigner" had abused their children. One of the youngsters was "too ashamed" to tell his mother and had transformed from a "bubbly, happy boy" to being "withdrawn and sometimes aggressive," the court heard. The court learned that Frost had subscribed to the now-defunct Paedophile Information Exchange (PIE), an international organization involved in the exchange of obscene material. In 1986, he attempted to adopt a son through the Catholic Church but was rejected, only to succeed through a local authority. The pair are now estranged

Police in Bethesda have attempted to address the issue of large groups congregating and leaving behind broken glass and litter. However, the situation has recently deteriorated. PCSO Gareth Davies of North Wales Police stated that the behavior of these groups is negatively impacting the community. Some residents have expressed concern that young people in the town lack recreational facilities. North Wales Police has implemented a dispersal order, authorizing officers to remove individuals from the area if their conduct is deemed anti-social. Mr. Davies emphasized to BBC Radio Wales' Good Morning Wales that the aim is to avoid prosecution or the imposition of dispersal orders that could have long-term consequences for residents. He noted that the individuals involved are at a transitional age where they feel too old for youth clubs but too young for pubs. A resident, who wished to remain anonymous, expressed worry about her young son's safety due to the presence of broken glass in the parks. Conversely, another resident expressed skepticism that the dispersal order would be effective, fearing it would criminalize young people in the town. She argued that the root cause is a lack of activities and places for them to go, suggesting that providing safe spaces would be a more constructive solution.
The new BBC Three logo features two white bars and an exclamation mark on a pink background. It has received criticism on Twitter for resembling "BBC 2!" in Roman numerals. Nikki Carr, head of marketing for BBC Three, acknowledged the criticism but stated she is not concerned. She emphasized the logo's intention to be bold and forward-looking, designed to endure for years. Carr also noted the logo's need to function effectively both on television and as an app icon, citing Snapchat as an example of a successful brand without its name in its logo. Carr admitted that the BBC faces criticism regarding its marketing efforts, referencing the satirical show W1A. Many have observed a resemblance between the new logo and a sketch in W1A where a team proposes replacing the BBC logo with three bars. However, Carr explained that the three bars represent the channel's three core principles: "make me think," "make me laugh," and "give me a voice," which aims to empower young people. Carr stated that BBC Three will involve young people in decision-making processes and content creation. The channel will launch a collaboration with 50 young creatives in the coming weeks, encompassing various content formats such as short films, blogs, animation, and picture-led stories, delivered daily through new platforms. In November, the BBC Trust approved the move of BBC Three online as a cost-saving measure. The agreement stipulated that the channel's longer-form programs would be broadcast on BBC One and Two, totaling approximately two hours per week. Starting on February 16th, BBC Three will primarily function as a promotional channel, directing viewers to online content. The traditional BBC Three television channel will cease broadcasting in the following weeks.
Researchers presenting findings about a possible train discovery in Walbrzych, Poland, stated that while a tunnel might exist, there was no evidence of a train. Despite this, one of the individuals who claimed to have found the train maintained his belief in its existence. The train was allegedly hidden underground near Wroclaw as Soviet forces advanced in 1945. The Nazis constructed extensive tunnel networks near Walbrzych during World War Two. In August, Deputy Culture Minister Piotr Zuchowski expressed 99% certainty that a German military train equipped with gun turrets was buried near Walbrzych based on ground-penetrating radar images. However, Professor Janusz Madej from Krakow's Academy of Mining, after conducting a geological survey, stated that no train was found. Local legends claim an armored train carrying gold from Wroclaw disappeared near Ksiaz castle, 3km from Walbrzych, as Soviet forces approached at the end of World War Two. Earlier this year, Piotr Koper (Poland) and Andreas Richter (Germany) informed authorities about the train's alleged location and requested 10% of the value of any recovered items through their lawyers. At a press conference, Mr. Koper disputed the survey methods and insisted that the train was present.
Isabelle Sanders, 51, was fatally stabbed by 20-year-old Paul McManus during a robbery at her Crookston home. McManus received a life sentence with a minimum of 26 years. Ms. Sanders' brother, James Dougall, advocates for whole life sentences to protect the public from violent criminals. During a Glasgow High Court trial last year, the jury learned that Ms. Sanders was stabbed 37 times during the robbery. McManus was also convicted of attempting to murder her 86-year-old partner, Norman Busby, and two other robbery-related stabbings. It was revealed after the trial that McManus had been released from a previous sentence just weeks before the murder. The sentencing judge, Lord Armstrong, emphasized McManus's "significant" criminal history, which included convictions for theft, assault, robbery, and weapon use. He noted that the 20-year-old had already served seven detention sentences. Ms. Sanders' family has initiated a petition with Holyrood's petitions committee, hoping for widespread public support. Mr. Dougall told BBC Scotland, "It might not apply to every violent offender, but certainly for violent repeat offenders who get the chance of early release and then reoffend, disregarding that chance, and commit violent murder specifically. I think (in these cases) the whole of life sentence is an appropriate option for the judiciary, and guidance should be given to them that they will be allowed to impose that type of sentence." He added, "So the petition we have started is 'life means life' - that judges have that option for whole of life sentencing in specific circumstances." While not used in Scotland, whole life sentences are employed by judges in England. The Scottish government has pointed out that judges already have the power to impose a sentence exceeding a person's remaining lifespan. A government spokesman stated, "Scottish courts have the power to impose the equivalent of a 'whole life tariff' in any given case. The independence of Scotland's judiciary is a fundamental part of the Scottish legal system. As such, sentencing is a matter for judges who operate independently of Scottish ministers, and it is for our courts to decide what sentence to impose in each case before them. The Scottish government has previously announced the creation of a specific Scottish Sentencing Council which will promote consistency and transparency around sentencing and encourage better understanding of sentences across Scotland, as well as producing sentencing guidelines for the judiciary."
Torfaen council's education department was placed under supervision by Estyn in February 2013 due to insufficient progress following a 2011 inspection that revealed serious shortcomings and an unsatisfactory rating. Subsequent checks led to the implementation of necessary measures, resulting in their removal on Friday. In 2013, Torfaen became the sixth Welsh council to be placed in special measures, joining Anglesey, Blaenau Gwent, Pembrokeshire, Monmouthshire, and Merthyr Tydfil. Currently, only Merthyr Tydfil remains under special measures. Education Minister Huw Lewis expressed satisfaction with the progress made by local authorities exiting special measures, hoping this would pave the way for further success. Estyn assistant director Clive Phillips, in a letter to Torfaen's chief executive Alison Ward, stated that inspectors reviewed seven recommendations from the 2011 and 2013 inspections. These recommendations emphasized the need for the council to enhance secondary school standards and provide more support for school leavers who did not pursue jobs or training. The authority also faced criticism for GCSE standards, with over half of schools falling in the bottom quarter for performance in core subjects (English or Welsh, maths, and science). No school exceeded the average for pupils achieving five GCSEs at grades A* to C. Following the imposition of special measures, the Welsh government mandated the creation of a recovery board to oversee and support the council's efforts to address the issues. Mr. Phillips's letter acknowledged the collaborative work of the council, elected members, and the recovery board in improving these areas. It concluded that Her Majesty's Chief Inspector of Education and Training in Wales determined the authority no longer requires special measures and would be removed from further follow-up activity. Torfaen council's executive member for education, David Yeowell, expressed delight at the news, attributing it to the collective efforts of school staff and the education service.
Bio-scientist Samantha Ho, 39, was found dead at her home in Curlew Place, St Neots, on August 29th. Her head and torso were discovered separately, and a post-mortem examination revealed she died from cuts to her neck. Her husband, Timothy Allen, 40, also of Curlew Place, pleaded guilty to manslaughter by diminished responsibility. Allen, who appeared via video link at the Old Bailey, had previously denied murder at a hearing in December. He will be sentenced on March 17th. Police were initially called to the house by the ambulance service following a report of concern for a man. Armed officers entered the house, where Ms. Ho's body was found. Allen was arrested on suspicion of murder and taken to the hospital with a serious but not life-threatening injury. He was later charged with murdering his wife. Detective Chief Inspector Richard Wall of Cambridgeshire Police described it as "a tragic case where a woman lost her life and families have been torn apart." In a statement, Ms. Ho's family described her as "well-loved... with a bright future ahead of her with lots of aspirations." The former Nottingham Trent University student had worked at bio-science firm Asterand in Royston, Hertfordshire, for over 10 years. Previously, she was employed at Pharmagene, part of Asterand, and Cambridge firm Cytocell. "She wanted to be a great scientist, have time to travel the world, realise her dreams and spend more time with family," Ms. Ho's family said. "However, on that fateful day... Samantha was tragically taken away from us. That day was a day where a part of us died."

He believes a commitment from retailers to Scottish lamb could improve the industry's situation. The union described the current period as extremely challenging for sheep farmers, with slow sales in autumn and no pre-Christmas price increase. Over the past six months, they have been regularly assessing supermarkets' commitment to domestically produced lamb. Morrisons was found to consistently stock only Scottish or British lamb. The union also acknowledged branding efforts by Marks and Spencer and Sainsbury's. Mr. Miller emphasized the need for a confidence boost from major customers, the UK supermarkets, indicating a clear desire for their product. Asda issued a statement affirming their commitment to buying Scottish lamb whenever possible, with approximately 60% of their current range sourced from Scottish farms.
A memo revealed that information about a possible attack on Ghana came from a man detained in Ivory Coast regarding the March 13th attack on the Grand Bassam beach resort. The memo also warned of a potential attack on neighboring Togo. Ghana and Togo have not experienced attacks by militant Islamists previously. The Grand Bassam attack, which killed at least 18 people, was the first of its kind in Ivory Coast and was claimed by Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb (AQIM). President Mahama stated that the leaked memo should not have been made public and urged the public to remain calm while maintaining vigilance. He also mentioned the risk of homegrown militants in Ghana. A March 9th memo from Ghana's Immigration Service requested increased security along the northern border with Burkina Faso. The memo, based on intelligence from Ghana's National Security Council Secretariat (NSCS), stated that a terrorist attack on Ghana was a real possibility. The memo, widely circulated in local media, suggested that Ghana was targeted to challenge the perception that only French-speaking countries are vulnerable. The memo described the Grand Bassam attack, which occurred about 40km from Abidjan, as carried out by gunmen who entered from Mali using vehicles registered in Niger. Weapons and grenades were concealed in the spare tire compartments, cushioned to prevent noise. AQIM was responsible for two similar attacks in recent months targeting hotels in Mali and Burkina Faso.

Sir John attributed the delay in his Iraq Inquiry report to the "Maxwellisation" process, which allows those criticized to respond. He acknowledged that some responses had led to new lines of inquiry. Lawyers representing families of those who died in Iraq stated they would take further legal action. Sir John initiated the inquiry in 2009 but has not set a publication date. While he expressed understanding for the families' anguish, Matthew Jury of McCue and Partners criticized Sir John for refusing to impose a timetable on the Maxwellisation process, preventing a deadline for the report. Reg Keys, whose son died in Iraq in 2003, expressed disappointment, arguing that witnesses should have had a six-month limit to respond. He fears the process has become a "farce" and the delay is unbearable for families. Sir John has faced criticism, including from Prime Minister David Cameron. In his statement, Sir John maintained that the inquiry has managed its deadlines, stating that some responses required complex analysis. Clare Short, who was international development secretary during the 2003 invasion, doubted the Maxwellisation process was the primary cause of the delay, suggesting the draft report might be extensive and require significant revisions. Crispin Blunt, chairman of the Foreign Affairs Select Committee, stated that Parliament cannot impose a deadline for publication as it would jeopardize the inquiry. He expressed hope for a conclusion within six months and urged Sir John to provide clarity on the process.

On Saturday, the Lord Mayor of Bradford unveiled a stone near the village of Serre. The stone, overlooking trenches used by soldiers in the battle, is a replica of one in Bradford's Memorial Gardens. Pals battalions, formed when friends and colleagues enlisted together, were common in northern towns. The stone is inscribed with: "To the memory of the soldiers of the Bradford Pals and the other servicemen of West Yorkshire who served in the Great War 1914-18. 'And lo a mighty army came out of the North.'" Quarried in Bradford, the stone was transported to France. The first Bradford Pals battalion was formed at the Bradford Mechanic's Institute in 1914, and a century-old union jack that flew above the institute was used in the unveiling ceremony. Local dignitaries, representatives of veteran groups, and the Commonwealth War Graves Commission attended. Councillor Geoff Reid, the Lord Mayor of Bradford, stated: "We owe a tremendous debt of gratitude to these brave men from Bradford who gave their lives during the First World War."

Can's Facebook page announced the passing of Jaki Liebezeit this morning due to sudden pneumonia. He died peacefully surrounded by loved ones. The band, known for pioneering the Krautrock movement, blended rock, electronic, and world music. Liebezeit originated the band's name, which stood for "communism, anarchism, nihilism." He remained a constant member throughout various lineup changes, performing on all their albums, including "Tago Mago," "Ege Bamyasi," and "Future Days." Although they released up to three albums annually in the 1970s, Can achieved cult status, influencing artists like Roxy Music, Julian Cope, The Flaming Lips, and The Horrors. They had an unexpected UK hit with "I Want More," reaching number 26 in 1976 and leading to their only appearance on Top Of The Pops. Liebezeit described Can and fellow Krautrock bands like Neu! and Kraftwerk as seeking their own unique path. He compared it to an artist refusing to imitate others and instead striving for originality. A student of the free jazz scene in Dresden, Germany, Liebezeit co-founded Can in 1968 with Irmin Schmidt, Holger Czukay, and Michael Karoli. Malcolm Mooney joined later that year. Liebezeit had a long career, collaborating with artists like Depeche Mode, the Eurythmics, Jah Wobble, and Brian Eno, in addition to his work with Can. His dynamic drumming style served as the rhythmic core of Can's music, grounding their experimental compositions with a precise, hypnotic pulse. This style, later termed "motorik," evokes the repetitive and relentless feeling of driving on a motorway. Liebezeit was scheduled to perform with Sonic Youth's Thurston Moore, Can's Schmidt, and Mooney at London's Barbican in April for "The Can Project." Jah Wobble, bassist for Public Image Limited, expressed his grief, calling Liebezeit a wonderful person and the best European drummer. Brian Eno shared links to Can's music and interviews with Liebezeit.

Pitman expressed his desire to represent Jersey if they form a team.
