In November 2015, during the filming of a documentary in the city, Hugh McGrattan lost control of his vehicle, causing serious injuries to pedestrians. McGrattan, a 25-year-old from Whiterock Drive, pleaded guilty to five counts of causing grievous bodily harm by dangerous driving. The crash occurred on Apollo Road, a 30mph zone, where CCTV footage revealed McGrattan driving at 52 to 69 mph before hitting a group of up to 15 people filming for RTÉ. One woman sustained a spinal cord injury, resulting in paralysis and the need for a wheelchair. McGrattan admitted to speeding at the scene, telling police he thought he would overturn. The prosecutor stated that some victims were trapped under the car and its downed fencing. Witnesses described the car failing to negotiate a bend due to excessive speed, comparing its impact to a bowling ball or a snow plough. The prosecutor informed the court that some victims still require medical treatment due to the severity of their injuries. The defense argued that McGrattan, diagnosed with post-traumatic stress disorder, deeply regrets the incident and suffers nightmares. They claimed he made a "significant misjudgement" rather than showing off, and remained at the scene, offering assistance and confessing to police. Forensic evidence suggested McGrattan's speed upon hitting the curb was approximately 25 mph. The judge requested time to consider the case and scheduled sentencing for the following week. McGrattan was released on bail.

A new system mandates motorists to obtain a check code from the DVLA website, enabling companies to verify a driver's penalty points. This code replaced the paper counterpart license, which previously contained this information. Initially, the code was valid for only 72 hours. Due to complaints from car hire companies about queues and drivers about lacking internet access abroad, the DVLA extended the validity period to 21 days. A phone number will also be available for those without internet access, although it is not a 24-hour service. The DVLA stated that the change addresses industry feedback regarding the short lifespan of the code. Motoring organizations expressed concern over the lack of awareness about the new system and its complexity. The DVLA maintains that using the system is straightforward, directing users to gov.uk and searching for "view driving licence." With the elimination of the paper counterpart, all driving endorsements and convictions are now stored online. Existing paper licenses, including those with endorsements, remain valid. This system does not apply to drivers in Northern Ireland.

They attributed the accident to unpredictable gusts of wind and denied any mechanical failures in his car. The 33-year-old Spaniard remains hospitalized for observation. A decision regarding his participation in the final pre-season testing session, starting later this week, has yet to be made. Alonso's manager posted a picture of him smiling in his hospital bed on social media, thanking supporters. Alonso sustained a concussion after hitting a wall while exiting a right-hand turn at the Circuit de Catalunya. While the track allows speeds of 150 mph at that point, Sebastian Vettel, who was following Alonso, estimated his speed to be around 90 mph. McLaren provided further details about Alonso's immediate treatment after the crash on Sunday. He was transported to the circuit's medical center, where he received first aid and was sedated before being airlifted to the hospital. At the hospital, he underwent CT and MRI scans, which showed no abnormalities. He is being kept in the hospital for further observation and recovery from the sedation medication. McLaren's analysis of Alonso's damaged car revealed that it veered off course at the entry of Turn Three, running onto the Astroturf lining the outside of the track. This resulted in a loss of traction, causing the car to spin back towards the inside, where it regained traction and collided with the wall. McLaren also refuted rumors that Alonso lost consciousness due to an electrical fault, stating that their data shows he was downshifting and applying full brake pressure until the initial impact, which would not have been possible if he were unconscious. Alonso left Ferrari at the end of last season to join McLaren and Honda, believing they offered a better chance at winning his third world title. However, McLaren's preparations have been hampered by recurring engine reliability issues, limiting their testing mileage.
A legendary comedy film was banned in Bournemouth in 1980 and given an X rating by the local council. The 19-seat Bournemouth Colosseum recently screened the film after the council relaxed its stance. Cinema owner Paul Whitehouse called the event "a bit of fun" and provided a three-course meal. It is believed to be the first public showing of the film in the town since the controversy over its rating. Whitehouse recalled that in the early 1980s, people had to travel to Southampton to see the film. He added that the film's phrases have become part of British language. Attendee Dave Harvey said it is one of his favorite films and that he was excited to see it at its first screening in Bournemouth. He was unaware that he was making local history. "Life of Brian," which tells the story of a man born near Jesus Christ and mistaken for the Messiah, faced accusations of blasphemy upon its release. Bournemouth council's environmental services committee viewed the film and upgraded its rating from AA to X, restricting viewership to those over 18. Distributors stated the film could only be shown in areas with an AA rating. Train driver Adrian Cox recently requested the council change its stance so he could host a private screening for his birthday. The film was only allowed to be shown after licensing officers agreed. Colin Robinson gave the film a 9.5 out of 10 rating, expressing his confusion over the ban and comparing the viewing experience to his student days. He praised the Monty Python team for bringing freedom and humor to life.

Eurostat estimates show a slight slowdown in price growth for food, alcohol, and tobacco compared to November, while the decline in energy prices was also less pronounced. The overall rate was lower than the 0.3% increase predicted by economists. This data may pressure the European Central Bank to take further action to stimulate the weak European economy. Last month, the central bank's attempts to revive the economy fell short of market expectations, leading to disappointment. These inflation figures are preliminary flash estimates from Eurostat and are not broken down by individual member states. However, they provide a general overview of price trends across product categories. Food, alcohol, and tobacco prices were estimated to increase by 1.2% year-on-year in December, down from 1.5% in November. Energy prices were estimated to decrease by 5.9% annually, compared to a 7.3% drop the previous month. Service prices rose by 1.1%, compared to 1.2% in the preceding month.
A 3,100-meter runway will be constructed 1.6 kilometers north of Dublin Airport's main runway and is anticipated to be operational in 2020. Planning permission for a new runway was granted in 2007 but was delayed due to the economic downturn and a decline in passenger numbers. The announcement follows a record 25 million passengers last year, surpassing the previous record of 23 million set in 2008. There is speculation that the airport may increase passenger charges to fund the new runway. Kevin Toland, the airport's chief executive, stated that the expansion would benefit business and tourism, enhancing Ireland's connectivity and supporting trade, foreign investment, and tourism. He emphasized the airport's commitment to balancing national and business needs with the concerns of local communities. Paschal Donohoe, the republic's minister for transport, tourism, and sport, highlighted the new runway's significance for Ireland's economic recovery, emphasizing its potential to create thousands of jobs.
SpaceX aborted a Falcon 9 launch from Cape Canaveral due to a low thrust alarm triggered by rising oxygen temperatures. This was the third attempt in five days to launch a satellite for SES, a Luxembourg-based operator. A fourth attempt is possible later this week, but engineers need time to analyze the data and determine the cause of the abort. The US Air Force also requires time to prepare the Florida range. Elon Musk, SpaceX CEO, tweeted that the alarm was caused by rising oxygen temperatures due to a hold for a boat and a helium bubble. Earlier that evening, the countdown was paused and reset because a vessel entered the restricted zone under the rocket's flight path. SpaceX is resuming routine launches after last year's Falcon 9 failure. They successfully deployed an Orbcomm satellite payload in December, marking the first landing and recovery of an orbiting rocket stage. In January, SpaceX launched the Jason ocean altimeter mission for US and European space and meteorological agencies. The company is now using an upgraded, higher thrust Falcon 9 that uses super-chilled liquid oxygen (LOX) as one of its propellants. Recent countdowns have shown that handling the new LOX presents challenges in loading and maintaining its temperature.

Walter Newall, an architect whose work is still visible throughout Dumfries and Galloway, was born in 1780. He began his career as a cabinet-maker but later transitioned to architecture after his business failed. Newall's buildings reflect his understanding of Greek Revival and Picturesque Gothic styles. He designed a variety of structures, including churches, country houses, villas, and farmsteads, many for the Buccleuch estates. He also played a role in the development of Dumfries' New Town and the transformation of a windmill into an observatory. Newall remained unmarried and died in 1863. Photographer Margeret Elliot is currently documenting all of Newall's buildings in the region and will be giving a talk about his life and work at Moat Brae in Dumfries, a building he designed in 1823. Moat Brae is believed to have inspired J.M. Barrie, author of Peter Pan, who lived in the town. The building is being redeveloped into a national center for children's literature.

Bionic legs, also known as micro-processor limbs, will be available to wounded service personnel from Iraq and Afghanistan. These advanced prosthetics will enhance stability and mobility for approximately 160 members of the armed forces. The Help for Heroes charity praised the funding, stating it would "transform lives." Defence Secretary Philip Hammond emphasized the government's commitment to providing the best possible care and support for troops. Chancellor George Osborne, who is allocating funds from the Treasury's special reserve, expressed the government's obligation to support those who serve their country, particularly those injured in the line of duty. He highlighted the significance of this cutting-edge technology in improving the lives of veterans. Experts believe these bionic legs, similar to those used by Paralympic athlete Derek Derenalagi, will significantly enhance the quality of life and rehabilitation for amputees. The technology offers better stability, increased mobility, and improved ability to navigate obstacles, stairs, and move backward safely. The limbs will be fitted at the Headley Court military rehabilitation centre in Surrey, where appropriate. Paralympic rower Captain Nick Beighton, who lost both legs in an explosion in Afghanistan, welcomed the new technology as a significant advancement. He expressed his enthusiasm for the opportunity to utilize this latest technology, which will grant him greater freedom, functionality, and independence. Surgeon General Air Marshal Paul Evans underscored the proven benefits of this next-generation micro-processor knee for certain amputees, stating that it will enhance quality of life and rehabilitation. Bryn Parry, chief executive of Help for Heroes, emphasized the importance of providing the best prosthetic solutions for wounded service personnel, acknowledging the need for personalized care.

Russia claims that 22 of these samples belong to their athletes, with two being cleared after their B samples tested negative. Russia's Sports Minister, Mutko, stated that all samples tested at the laboratory in question should be invalidated and that the laboratory should be stripped of its accreditation. The IOC is retesting these samples at a World Anti-Doping Agency-accredited laboratory in Lausanne, Switzerland, focusing on athletes who may compete in the upcoming Rio Olympics. Since November, Russian athletes have been suspended from international competition by the IAAF. The IOC will decide on June 17th whether Russia will be allowed to compete at the Rio 2016 Olympics. A documentary by German broadcaster ARD alleged that Mutko was involved in covering up doping cases and allowed a disgraced walking coach to return to the Russian team. A spokesman stated that this case is connected to a criminal investigation in France into alleged extortion of athletes by former IAAF president Lamine Diack and other officials.

Gavin Barwell, the Housing Minister, stated that there would be minimum tenancies and an increase in rental homes, marking a shift from previous Conservative Party policy. He emphasized that the government hasn't abandoned its goal of homeownership for all. Labour criticized the government's inaction in assisting renters dealing with private landlords. During an appearance on BBC's Sunday Politics, Barwell explained that the government needed to address the concerns of renters facing unaffordable costs. He announced a package of measures outlined in a White Paper to be released on Tuesday, aimed at encouraging investment in affordable rental homes, defined as at least 20% below market rates. Councils are also encouraged to play a more active role. Previously, the government announced a ban on letting agents in England charging fees to tenants. While the Conservative Party's last general election manifesto pledged that "everyone who works hard should be able to own a home of their own," Barwell affirmed the government's commitment to reversing the decline in homeownership. He acknowledged, however, that policies are also necessary to support the estimated 4.3 million private renters. Barwell attributed the rising housing costs to insufficient government housing construction over the past 30 to 40 years, stating that the White Paper aims to rectify this. He acknowledged that recent figures showing a 24-year low in new affordable homes were "embarrassing" but attributed it to the start of a new five-year program. Despite being "behind schedule," he reiterated the government's commitment to building one million new homes in England by 2020. He stated that the government would not alter current green belt rules, which permit building only in "exceptional circumstances," rejecting the notion that significant green belt reductions are necessary to solve the housing crisis. Labour, however, argued that the proposed build-to-rent initiatives fall short of what's required. Shadow housing secretary John Healey criticized the government for its lack of support for private renters and its opposition to Labour's efforts to regulate costs and provide renters with security.

While the tax status will not be eliminated, individuals residing in the UK for 15 of the past 20 years will lose their eligibility to claim it. The non-domicile rule permits certain wealthy UK residents to minimize taxes on foreign earnings. The chancellor stated that modifications to the non-dom rules will generate an additional £1.5 billion in annual tax revenue. These changes will also mean that starting in April 2017, someone born in the UK to UK-domiciled parents, who moves away and then returns to reside in the UK, will no longer be able to claim non-dom status. Additionally, the government announced that from April 2017, new rules will prevent individuals who own UK residential property and would otherwise be subject to inheritance tax from avoiding it by holding the property in an offshore company. This is intended to curb "abuses" of the rule by some with non-dom status. The chancellor explained that he chose not to abolish the non-dom tax status entirely because it "would cost the country money," emphasizing that many individuals who utilize the tax status "make a considerable contribution to public life." However, he added, "It is not fair that people live in this country for very long periods of their lives, benefit from our public services, and yet operate under different tax rules from everyone else." Separately, the chancellor announced that HM Revenue & Customs will receive an additional £750 million to combat tax evasion. Mr. Osborne stated that this extra funding will assist HMRC officials in raising £7.2 billion in additional tax by 2020.

Three firearms agents stated they were instructed to monitor the weapons' movement but not to make any arrests. US lawmakers expressed anger about Operation Fast and Furious' details. This news follows a report indicating Mexican drug cartels have armed themselves with US weapons. The report suggests that 70% of firearms recovered from Mexican crime scenes in 2009 and 2010 and traced back to the US. On Wednesday, congressional lawmakers concluded that Fast and Furious, intended to track small-time gun buyers to major weapons traffickers along the southwestern US border, never resulted in any major traffickers' arrests. Guns tracked by Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco, Firearms and Explosives (ATF) agents were allegedly used in numerous killings in Mexico. House of Representatives Oversight Committee lawmakers demanded answers from the Obama administration about why no arrests were made while investigators tracked the firearms. "We monitored as they purchased handguns, AK-47 variants, and .50 caliber rifles, almost daily at times," ATF agent John Dodson told the committee. He added that although he wanted to "intervene and interdict these weapons," his supervisors instructed him not to make any arrests. Prior to the panel hearing, Senator Chuck Grassley of Iowa stated "hundreds upon hundreds of weapons" destined for Mexican cartels were purchased in Arizona gun shops. Operation Fast and Furious aimed to track weaponry as it moved from small-time gun buyers to major traffickers, who have often evaded prosecution. In December, two US assault rifles were found at the scene of a shootout where Customs and Border Protection agent Brian Terry was killed. "We ask that if a government official made a wrong decision, they admit their error and take responsibility for their actions," Robert Heyer, the deceased agent's cousin, told the panel on Wednesday. In January, the Mexican government released figures suggesting at least 34,612 people had died in drug-related violence in Mexico since December 2006, although speculation exists that the figure may now exceed 40,000.
Melissa Pesticcio, 23, and Sophie Taylor, 22, were involved in a high-speed crash. Pesticcio had been "following and continuing to follow" Taylor in her car through city streets after seeing her outside her boyfriend Michael Wheeler's, 22, home. The two had been feuding since Wheeler left Pesticcio for Taylor, but Wheeler returned to Pesticcio recently. After a disagreement over money, Pesticcio and Wheeler pursued the young woman through streets in the Adamsdown area. Wheeler's Vauxhall Corsa "forced" Taylor's BMW into a wall, causing her death and leaving another passenger in the car with life-changing injuries. Wheeler admitted to causing serious injuries by dangerous driving and causing death by dangerous driving, but Pesticcio denied being involved in the incident on August 22 last year. She was found guilty of causing Taylor's death by dangerous driving and causing serious harm to her passenger Joshua Deguara at the end of a 10-day trial at Cardiff Crown Court. She was found not guilty of another charge of dangerous driving in a separate incident. The three cars were driving quickly through Cardiff streets, with CCTV footage showing Pesticcio's car in the lead with Wheeler following closely behind Taylor's car. Wheeler drove on the wrong side of the road twice to get closer to Taylor's car. He drove into her car, causing it to lose control and crash into a wall. Sophie Taylor died and Mr Deguara suffered serious injuries, spending "many months" in hospital. In a statement, Taylor and Deguara's families said "both families have been devastated forever." Taylor was described as "our beautiful princess," and the statement added: "This is now a life sentence that both families have to endure daily." Pesticcio, from Cardiff, will be sentenced on Friday. She has been remanded in custody.
Earlier this year, plans to introduce a non-native species for the first time were announced. A public consultation revealed divided opinions, with some wildlife experts expressing concerns about a "devastating impact" while others supported the idea. The government stated there were "no conservation grounds" to move forward. Environment Minister Richard Ronan, after considering all perspectives, concluded it would be "detrimental" and announced the decision to postpone the introduction. Over 100 individuals responded to the consultation. A spokesperson for the Manx Wildlife Trust warned that the introduction could severely damage habitats already under environmental stress. Wildlife experts argued that the red squirrel is not at risk of extinction in the UK and found no scientific justification for creating a refuge for it. Grey squirrels, introduced from North America in the 19th century, have threatened red squirrels in the UK through habitat competition and the spread of the deadly parapoxvirus. Although not endangered, the Red Squirrel Survival Trust in the UK claims they could vanish within a decade without conservation efforts.

A veteran musician, speaking to Rolling Stone magazine, stated that those who criticize his use of quoted material in his music are "wussies and pussies." He believes that quoting is a valuable tradition in folk and jazz, though he acknowledges that he operates under different rules. Dylan's 35th studio album, Tempest, was released recently. Earlier this year, the 71-year-old singer received the Medal of Freedom, America's highest civilian honor, from President Obama. Dylan has been accused of borrowing from 19th-century poet Henry Timrod on his 2006 album Modern Times and from Japanese gangster memoir Confessions of a Yakuza by author Junichi Saga on his 2001 album Love and Theft. In response to these accusations, Dylan questioned the relevance of Timrod in contemporary times and challenged critics to quote him themselves if they believe it is so easy. He compared the accusations against him to the controversy surrounding his 1965 album Bringing It All Back Home, where he was labeled "Judas" for using an electric guitar. Dylan, born Robert Allen Zimmerman in 1941, began his career in 1959 playing in Minnesota coffee houses. He adopted his stage name from poet Dylan Thomas. Much of his most famous work dates back to the 1960s, where he became known for his commentary on American social issues through songs like Blowin' In The Wind and The Times They Are A-Changin'. His decision to switch from acoustic to electric guitar in the mid-1960s sparked controversy among some folk music fans. Dylan defended his songwriting process, stating that it involves melody, rhythm, and ultimately, the freedom to make everything his own.
A government-developed online tool predicts that crop yields must increase by 40-60% by 2050 to meet the 2C target for avoiding dangerous climate change. This Global Calculator, which uses data reviewed by international experts, models the world's energy, land, and food systems. It suggests that living standards can be maintained but only through significant changes in agriculture, transport, food, and fuel. The model indicates that hundreds of millions of electric cars would be needed by 2050, and CO2 emissions per unit of electricity must decrease by at least 90%. Consumers may also need to adopt diets high in vegetables or consume meat from intensively farmed animals. Energy and Climate Change Secretary Ed Davey stated that the calculator demonstrates how everyone can prosper while limiting global temperature rises to 2C. He emphasized the urgent need to act now to change energy and land use practices. Dr. Mike Cherrett of Climate-KIC, a co-leader of the project, added that the calculator shows meeting the 2C target while maintaining good lifestyles is possible, but requires ambitious targets and innovation. The Global Calculator builds on a UK calculator published in 2010 and is being offered to other governments for use in upcoming climate negotiations in Paris.
Real hourly pay for 22 to 39-year-olds had decreased by 11% from its peak, while workers in their 50s experienced a 5% decrease and those in their 60s saw a 2% decrease. Average weekly earnings declined by 0.4% in the first quarter of 2017. Part-time workers were the only group whose typical earnings had returned to pre-2008 levels. The Resolution Foundation, established in 2005 with the aim of improving living standards for low and middle-income earners in Britain, stated that the pay squeeze had returned at the start of 2017 and was expected to persist throughout the year. They expressed concern that wages had not fully recovered from the previous pay squeeze before this latest decline. However, the foundation acknowledged the positive impact of the National Living Wage, which had been introduced two years prior, resulting in a 12% increase over that period. In response to the report, a spokesperson for HM Treasury highlighted the record number of people in employment and emphasized government efforts to increase take-home pay through tax cuts for working individuals. They also pointed to the National Living Wage's £1,400 annual pay rise for low-wage earners and measures to reduce the cost of living, such as freezing fuel duty and expanding free childcare.

The Chennai High Court dismissed petitions seeking the prosecution of author Perumal Murugan. The judges stated that Murugan should be allowed to "resurrect" to his profession of writing. Murugan had stopped writing in 2015 following protests against his novel, Madhorubagan. The court ruling is considered a victory for freedom of expression by supporters. The court also ruled that a "settlement" between Murugan and a Tamil Nadu peace council, which involved an apology for the novel and the withdrawal of unsold copies, was not legally binding. Madhorubagan, set in southern India a century ago, tells the story of a childless woman who participates in a sex ritual during a temple festival to conceive. Murugan claims the scenario is based on historical fact. Local groups protested the book, alleging the fictionalized extramarital sex ritual depicted in the plot insulted the town, its temple, and its women. Protests included book burnings, shop closures, and a petition demanding Murugan's arrest. These events led Murugan to post a statement on Facebook declaring, "Perumal Murugan, the writer is dead." He renounced writing, instructed his publishers to stop selling his works, and asked readers to burn their copies. He also stated he would no longer attend literary festivals. Murugan is widely regarded as one of the most talented writers in the Tamil language.
O'Hare broke John Robson's record, clocking 3:33.61 in Monaco on Friday, surpassing Robson's 1979 time of 3:33.83. This sets the British champion and double European medallist up for the World Championships in London next month. In the women's 3,000m, fellow Scot Laura Muir was defeated by Kenyan Hellen Obiri. This race served as good preparation for O'Hare, who will be part of an all-Scottish GB and NI team for the World Championships in the 1500m alongside Jake Wightman and Josh Kerr. Muir, the European indoor 1500m and 3,000m champion, faced her rival Obiri, the 2016 Olympic 5,000m silver medallist, in the 3,000m. Muir, who will compete in both the 1500m and 5,000m at next month's World Championships, was outpaced by the in-form Kenyan. Obiri finished strongly, clocking 8:23.14 for a significant victory over compatriot Beatrice Chepkoech. Muir ran a personal best of 8:30.64 but placed third. Fellow Scot Eilish McColgan also achieved a lifetime best, finishing fourth in 8:31.39. Lynsey Sharp came sixth in 1:58.01, a season's best performance.
Seven novels remain on the shortlist for the sixth Walter Scott Prize, down from an initial longlist of 15. The shortlist for the £30,000 award was announced at 50 Albermarle Street in London, the former home of Walter Scott's publisher, John Murray. The winner will be revealed this summer in the Borders. The seven shortlisted novels represent the diverse styles within historical fiction, ranging from poetic to experimental, satirical to adventurous. According to chair of judges Alistair Moffat, the judges considered a record number of entries and had to make difficult choices due to the extensive longlist. He believes the shortlist reflects the vibrancy of historical writing published last year, showcasing a range of global settings from colonial India to 14th Century China, Europe during the two World Wars, and 17th Century England. The winning author receives £25,000, while each shortlisted author receives £1,000, making the total prize worth £31,000.
The dramatic increase in lung cancer risks has been linked to habit changes like starting smoking earlier and smoking more cigarettes. A study published in the New England Journal of Medicine revealed that death rates from lung cancer in women have now equaled those in men. The study analyzed data from over two million women in the US. The first generation of female smokers began in the 1950s and 1960s. During that time, women who smoked were almost three times more likely to die from lung cancer compared to non-smokers. Medical records from women between 2000 and 2010 showed they were 25 times more likely to die from lung cancer than their non-smoking counterparts. This trend mirrors that observed in men, who reached a similar level of risk in the 1980s. Lead researcher Dr. Michael Thun stated that the steep increase in risk among female smokers has persisted for decades, even after the serious health risks of smoking were widely recognized. He also noted that women primarily smoked cigarette brands marketed as lower in tar and nicotine. Thun suggested that these "Light" and "Mild" brands did not prevent the significant increase in risk for women and may have even worsened the increase in deaths from chronic obstructive lung disease in male smokers. This is because the diluted smoke from these cigarettes is inhaled deeper into the lungs to maintain the desired nicotine absorption. Previous research indicated that lifelong female smokers died a decade earlier than those who never smoked. However, women who quit by age 30 almost entirely avoided the risks of premature death from tobacco-related diseases. Those who quit by age 40 died only a year earlier than non-smokers. Following that study, Prof. Sir Richard Peto of Oxford University remarked, "If women smoke like men, they die like men."

Due to ongoing repairs on damaged steelwork, vehicles over 7.5 tonnes are currently prohibited from using the Forth Road Bridge. On Tuesday morning, traffic heading northbound was briefly halted to allow a heavy goods vehicle (HGV) to be safely turned around. The bridge was closed to all vehicles for nearly three weeks before reopening on December 23rd. However, HGVs will continue to face a lengthy diversion until at least mid-February. Police Scotland stated that around 10:40 am on January 5th, an articulated HGV attempted to cross the bridge despite the restrictions in place. Officers stopped northbound traffic temporarily to facilitate the safe turning around of the HGV. The male driver was subsequently charged with using a prohibited vehicle on a restricted road. Police Scotland expressed gratitude to the public for their patience and cooperation. The bridge was initially closed to all traffic on December 4th after a crack was discovered in a truss beneath the carriageway. Engineers have implemented a temporary repair, allowing lighter vehicles to use the bridge. Transport minister Derek Mackay expressed hope that the crossing will reopen to all vehicles in mid-February, contingent upon favorable weather conditions and the absence of further defects.

MB Aerospace has acquired Centrax Turbine Components, located in Newton Abbot. While the Gas Turbines division remains within the Centrax Group, a family-owned company, the Devon-based firm, which employs 430 people, will now be part of MB Aerospace. Centrax has a nearly 70-year history of supplying engine parts, specializing in rotating components for the compressor and turbine sections of aero-engines. They will continue to manufacture components requiring automated machining, grinding, and robotic polishing. Centrax's clients include engine manufacturers Rolls-Royce, Snecma, and Solar, who in turn supply aircraft manufacturers Airbus, Boeing, and Embraer. With headquarters in Motherwell, North Lanarkshire, MB Aerospace now has manufacturing facilities in Connecticut, Michigan, Poland, and the UK. Blackstone private equity acquired a majority stake in MB Aerospace in 2013, and this acquisition marks their first major deal since receiving that investment. Centrax CEO Robert Barr stated, "In MB Aerospace, we have found a partner who shares our values and commitment to the region, as well as the significant pool of high-tech manufacturing expertise we have developed over time."
Several business owners have accused Wyvern Media (WM) of repeatedly billing them for unwanted advertisements. One owner, a dog breeder from Lincolnshire, stated she was charged over £500 for advertisements she hadn't authorized after initially agreeing to two adverts for £40. Another business owner, who ran a bespoke travel company, claimed she was forced to close her business due to charges exceeding £5,000. She asserts she only agreed to one advertisement but was later informed by WM that she had signed up for over £5,000 worth of advertising. She stated she never received any published copies or receipts for these orders. Her bank reimbursed her after she filed a successful complaint. A former WM employee, who wished to remain anonymous, confirmed receiving complaints from advertisers who claimed unauthorized payments were taken from their accounts. She stated that employees were afraid to speak out due to fear of job loss. In response to these allegations, WM managing director Jonathan Rivers denied the company's practice of taking unauthorized payments, claiming that all sales calls are now recorded and complaints have decreased to three or four per month. He added that customers can reclaim unauthorized payments through their credit cards.
Since 2014, the G postcode area has produced more than 21 million pound winners, more than any other area in the UK. Glasgow ranks second in the UK for millionaire wins since 1994, with 106 wins, trailing only Birmingham. However, the PA postcode area, which encompasses towns in Renfrewshire, Inverclyde, and Argyll and Bute, is considered the luckiest in Scotland for wins of £50,000 or more per capita. Andy Carter, senior winners' advisor for The National Lottery, stated, "Scotland has been experiencing a lot of National Lottery luck recently, with residents of the Paisley postcode celebrating the most significant wins. Glasgow has solidified its position as the country's Lottery millionaire capital, producing nearly two millionaires per month in recent years." He added, "There are now 4,250 National Lottery millionaires across the UK, and with more than six new millionaires created every week, another local winner is likely to emerge soon." In Scotland, Edinburgh follows Glasgow with 62 millionaire wins since 1994, followed by Motherwell with 43 and Aberdeen with 41. Over the past two years, the PA postcode area has seen 30 top-tier prizes of at least £50,000, including four Lottery millionaires. Since the National Lottery's inception, the area has recorded 182 major prizes of £50,000 or more.

Steven Thomas, 19, was driving at speeds up to 100mph when he crashed into a tree near Hagworthingham, Lincolnshire, in May while evading a police pursuit on the A158. The crash resulted in serious injuries for his two passengers: a girl suffered back fractures, and a woman sustained a head injury. Thomas, from Alfreton, was sentenced to 12 months detention. Prosecutor Phil Howes stated that Thomas's excessive speed led to him losing control of his car, causing significant damage and serious injuries to his passengers. He added that the pursuing officer estimated Thomas's speed at 100mph and that he appeared to be accelerating away. The court heard that Thomas sustained a serious brain injury in the crash and requires surgery to insert a plate over his brain, as well as therapy for speech and balance issues. Recorder Paul Mann QC, during sentencing, expressed astonishment that anyone survived the crash after viewing the wreckage. Thomas pleaded guilty to two counts of causing serious injury by dangerous driving and was banned from driving for two and a half years, with an extended driving test required upon his reinstatement.
Pollen analysis on pottery buried with a young woman over 4,100 years ago has revealed the presence of medicinal plants. Discovered 30 years ago at Achavanich in Caithness, the woman, known as Ava, is the focus of an ongoing research project led by archaeologist Maya Hoole. The pollen findings, which include birch, pine, hazel, alder, heather, grasses, meadowsweet, and St John's wort, have raised questions about their significance. The discovery of meadowsweet, previously found in other Bronze Age Scottish burials, and St John's wort, both with medicinal properties, suggests a possible deliberate inclusion of flowers in the burial. Archaeologist Ms Hoole questions whether this was intentional and if it had any connection to the cause of Ava's death. Ava's remains and artifacts are housed at Caithness Horizons museum in Thurso. Notably, she was buried in a rock-cut pit, and her skull has an unusual shape, possibly due to deliberate binding. Believed to be part of the Beaker people, a widespread European group, further research aims to provide more insights into Ava and her burial.
Efforts to establish a power-sharing executive before Monday's deadline are intensifying. Normally, the Stormont Estate is used by joggers and tourists, but on Saturday, politicians and officials will be there as negotiations continue. The 4 p.m. BST deadline on Monday has created a sense of urgency, with talks extending late into Friday night. Saturday's discussions are expected to continue into the evening, with the process described as being at a critical juncture. Two major obstacles remain: reaching an agreement on legacy issues and establishing an Irish language act. One source close to the talks suggested that without a shift in positions, a consensus on legacy issues by Monday is unlikely. However, the source indicated that some progress is being made on budgetary matters and a program for government. While previous deadlines have been missed or extended, this time is different. If a deal is not reached by late Monday and a First and Deputy First Minister are not in place, Secretary of State James Brokenshire is required to promptly call a new assembly election.
Russia expressed outrage over the report, calling it an "arbitrary judgement." A Chinese official echoed this sentiment, emphasizing the importance China places on combating all forms of trafficking and urging the US to adopt a more objective stance. The US State Department's annual report placed Russia and China in its lowest category, alongside Iran and North Korea. This tier also includes Uzbekistan, which saw a downgrade in the report released Wednesday. Countries in Tier 3 face potential sanctions, including restrictions on cultural and educational programs, and the US may withdraw support for World Bank or International Monetary Fund loans. Russia's foreign ministry human rights envoy, Konstantin Dolgov, condemned the report's methodology, accusing it of grouping countries based on their alignment with Washington. He emphasized that Russia would never be dictated to in its fight against organized crime, including trafficking. China's foreign ministry spokeswoman, Hua Chunying, stated China's commitment to combating trafficking and called for an objective assessment of China's efforts. The report criticized Russia for its lack of a concrete system for identifying or assisting trafficking victims and highlighted China's ongoing issues with forced labor, sex trafficking, and the demand for foreign brides fueled by the country's one-child policy. While acknowledging some progress in raising awareness and collaborating with international organizations, the report concluded that China had not demonstrated sufficient commitment to comprehensively prohibiting and punishing trafficking.

President Obama stated that some, but not all, hacking originating from China is state-sponsored, while downplaying congressional discussions of a cyber war. This statement followed US intelligence chiefs declaring that cyberattacks have surpassed terrorism as the primary security threat. China refutes these accusations, claiming to be a victim of such attacks. During an ABC News interview broadcast on Wednesday, President Obama was questioned about claims by US lawmakers that the extent of attacks on American companies and infrastructure constituted a cyber "war" with China. He emphasized the distinction between cyber espionage or attacks and a conventional war. He acknowledged a consistent increase in cybersecurity threats, some originating from state actors and others from criminals. The President asserted that the US has communicated to China and other state actors their expectation to adhere to international norms and rules. He indicated that strong discussions on this issue have already taken place. China has been long suspected of involvement in cyberattacks, prompting Pentagon warnings about the need to protect against a "digital Pearl Harbor." A US congressional report last year identified China as the most significant threat in cyberspace. This issue has become a growing point of contention between Washington and Beijing. On Tuesday, US Director of National Intelligence James Clapper informed a Senate committee that cyberattacks and espionage had overtaken terrorism as the nation's top security concern. A day earlier, US National Security Advisor Tom Donilon urged Beijing to take action against cybercrime. Last month, a report by US security firm Mandiant alleged that a unit of China's People's Liberation Army had conducted data raids on over 140 organizations, primarily based in the US. US state department officials have stated that hacking is raised "in virtually every meeting" with Chinese officials. In January, The New York Times reported being subjected to cyberattacks from China following its publication of a story about the wealth of former Premier Wen Jiabao's family.
A company reported evidence of "limited, targeted attacks" and advised users to update their software immediately. Flash, commonly used on the web for multimedia content, has a vulnerability that allows hackers to take control of computers. This vulnerability affects Windows, Mac, and Linux systems. Users can check their Flash version on the Adobe website; the latest version is 18.0.0.194. Users of the latest versions of Google Chrome and Microsoft Internet Explorer will have Flash automatically updated. Adobe Flash has a history of requiring security updates and is considered a security weakness by some researchers. Along with Java, Flash is frequently targeted by hackers using zero-day exploits, previously unknown security vulnerabilities. Mark James, a security specialist from ESET, stated that Flash's widespread use makes it a prime target for vulnerabilities. He explained that hackers aim to affect as many people as possible, and Flash, due to its popularity, is a suitable target. Security blogger Brian Krebs disabled Flash on his computer as an experiment and suggested that users consider whether they truly need Flash Player installed.

Recording a suicide verdict, Michael Singleton stated that Jackie Williams, 42, was not properly managed at Royal Blackburn Hospital. Williams died by suicide in an A&E cubicle on January 27th. The East Lancashire Hospitals NHS Trust and Lancashire Care NHS Foundation Trust asserted that patient safety has improved. Williams was admitted to the hospital late on January 26th after being found unconscious and intoxicated by a canal in Burnley. The inquest revealed that hospital staff knew she was suicidal, but Singleton stated that it was not sufficiently clear that the cubicle door should remain open. The Blackburn Coroner explained that Williams not only had suicidal thoughts but was placed in an environment where she could act on them – a room with potential ligatures and ligature points. He also said there was no effective referral of the patient to the hospital's mental health liaison team and a clear misunderstanding between nursing and mental health staff that resulted in Williams being left unattended and not seen by an appropriate specialist. Singleton is preparing a Regulation 28 report, which allows coroners to highlight concerns to prevent future deaths, but said he was already impressed by how both hospital trusts had addressed some issues. Both trusts stated that patient areas had been reviewed to reduce the risk of self-harm with improved observation of high-risk patients. Williams's husband, Wayne, was asked if he was satisfied with the inquest outcome but said he was not.
An unannounced inspection of HMP Bristol in Horfield earlier this year revealed a decline in conditions. Nick Hardwick, the chief inspector of prisons, stated that a sense of purpose had been lost since a positive report in 2010. The National Offender Management Service pledged support to improve the prison's performance. The 2010 inspection had praised HMP Bristol, a category-B prison with a capacity of 600 inmates, for its leadership and direction. However, this progress was found to be unsustainable. The recent inspection found staff to be dismissive of inmates, exhibiting delinquent behavior such as using abusive language. Fewer prisoners at HMP Bristol reported being treated with respect compared to similar prisons. Inspectors discovered dirty cells with damaged or missing furniture, offensive graffiti, and cockroach infestations. There were also concerns about a lack of clean clothes and adequate bedding. Inspectors witnessed a prisoner being arbitrarily punished by being denied full meals. The prison stated that disciplinary action had been taken against the staff involved. Mr. Hardwick described the prisoner experience as poor and identified priorities for improvement, including the environment, staff culture, and providing inmates with meaningful activities. Positives noted by inspectors included proportionate security procedures and the prison's success in addressing resettlement challenges. Michael Spurr of the National Offender Management Service acknowledged the prison's recent restructuring and the challenges posed by high prisoner turnover. He assured that the prison and its staff would receive necessary support to create a safe and constructive environment. The governor, Andrea Albutt, was unavailable for comment.
Stephen Paul Lynch, a 34-year-old from Shanlea Drive, appeared in Antrim Magistrates Court facing charges of arson endangering life with intent. On April 23rd, a house on Killyglen Road was severely damaged when a car was deliberately set ablaze and left at the front door. Lynch is also accused of threatening to kill a man and intimidating a woman and two children to leave the house. Police footage shows Lynch crashing his silver Peugeot into the house at approximately 4:40 PM BST. The footage allegedly depicts him setting fire to a jacket and placing it in the car's footwell before the vehicle became engulfed in flames. He is also seen kicking the house window and shouting at the property, although no one was inside at the time. Lynch subsequently called the police to report his car stolen. The occupants of the house had previously reported several incidents, including men wearing balaclavas outside their home and Lynch and another man making a "gun sign" to the male resident and threatening his life. On April 16th, the male resident woke to find his car and his wife's car on fire. Police opposed bail due to concerns about witness interference and the likelihood of re-offending, citing Lynch's disregard for his own safety and that of others. They believe he is involved with the UVF in Larne and an ongoing criminal feud, expressing fears for lives. Lynch's lawyer acknowledged the CCTV footage of the arson but denied the threats and intimidation allegations. They argued the arson was the act of a heavily intoxicated man, and Lynch accepts he will likely go to jail. The lawyer stated Lynch is on the periphery of the ongoing dispute and feels pushed to the edge. The judge denied bail, prioritizing public safety, and adjourned the case for two weeks.
Lewis Coaches, a company based in Ceredigion with 30 vehicles, will cease operations on Friday, putting 40 jobs at risk, according to the Confederation of Passenger Transport Cymru. This marks the third bus company in Wales to collapse in the past two months. In July, Wrexham-based GHA Coaches went into administration, resulting in the loss of over 300 jobs and service disruptions across north Wales, Cheshire, and Shropshire. In June, Pembrokeshire's Silcox Coaches, which had operated for 134 years, ceased trading after providing school transport, local bus services, and tours. Ceredigion council confirmed on Wednesday that Lewis Coaches, located in Llanrhystud, had also entered administration. The council stated that the 701 commercial bus service connecting Aberystwyth to Carmarthen and Cardiff would be discontinued. Simon Thomas, Plaid Cymru's Mid and West AM, wrote to the Welsh Government seeking assurances regarding the bus route. He emphasized the crucial role of bus services in connecting communities in the west, enabling workers, students, and tourists to travel. He stressed that this essential link should not be allowed to disappear. On its website, Lewis Coaches announced its closure with sadness, expressing gratitude to its loyal customers and hoping to minimize any inconvenience caused by the closure.
A UN convoy was attacked approximately 15km west of a Malian city late Friday. The UN took over security responsibilities in Mali after French-led forces drove Islamist militants out of northern cities in 2013. However, since July 2013, at least 35 UN troops have been killed and 140 wounded in attacks by al-Qaeda-linked militants who retreated to the Sahara desert. In the latest attack, gunmen halted the convoy, killed two drivers, and set the trucks ablaze. One other convoy member was injured. On Wednesday, a suicide bombing at a UN base in Ansongo killed three civilians and wounded nine Nigerien peacekeepers. Al-Mourabitoun, led by former al-Qaeda fighter Mokhtar Belmokhtar, claimed responsibility for the attack. The UN has approximately 9,000 personnel in Mali. Northern Mali has been a conflict zone since the country gained independence from France in 1960, with Tuareg rebels fighting for independence or greater autonomy. The rise of jihadist groups has further complicated the conflict.
Samsung predicts a significant increase in operating profit, nearly 80% higher than last year, reaching 7.3 trillion won. This projection surpasses analysts' estimates of around 6.8 trillion won and signifies the company's first quarterly profit growth in two years. Despite facing competition from Apple in the premium smartphone segment and Chinese brands like Xiaomi in the budget market, Samsung attributes its strong performance to robust chip sales and favorable exchange rates. The company's semiconductor division, the world's largest memory chip maker, is anticipated to be the top revenue generator for the fifth consecutive quarter. A weaker won has made Samsung's products more affordable internationally and enhances its profitability when earnings are repatriated. Samsung anticipates a 7.5% increase in sales to 51 trillion won during the same period. Following the positive earnings guidance, Samsung's Seoul-listed shares surged nearly 9%. The guidance does not provide a division-specific breakdown or net income figure. The complete audited results are expected later this month.
In 2014, Darren Sykes, 44, killed his sons Jack and Paul, aged 12 and nine, by setting their Penistone home on fire. The children were visiting their father during a weekend access visit after the family separated. Their mother, Claire Throssell, stated that she had previously warned a judge about her ex-husband's potential to harm the boys, expressing concern about his mental state and urging for the children's safety. A petition, supported by Women's Aid, has gathered over 40,000 signatures, calling for government and family courts to prevent future child deaths resulting from unsafe contact with domestic abusers. An inquest revealed that the boys were lured to their father's home with a new train set before the fire. Darren Sykes and Paul died in the fire, while Jack died six days later in the hospital. The coroner ruled that both boys were unlawfully killed and determined their father's death as a suicide.
At the age of 25, Cox became the first British athlete since 1988 to win medals in two different sports at the same Paralympics. She achieved this by winning the cycling time trial and the T38 400m. Cox previously participated in skeleton and expressed a desire to compete in both the winter and summer Olympics. While bobsleigh has been provisionally included in the 2022 Beijing Olympics, Cox has stated she cannot commit to the 2020 Tokyo Paralympics due to her multiple sclerosis. The unpredictability of her condition makes it difficult to plan so far in advance. Before her stroke in 2014, Cox was a talented sprinter. In Rio, she set a new world record of 34.598 seconds in the C4-5 cycling time trial. She also won silver in the T35-38 4x100m relay and bronze in the T38 100m. Cox expressed her desire to compete in Tokyo to defend her titles and aim for gold medals. Focusing on shorter-term goals, Cox is targeting the 2017 Para Athletics World Championships in London and the 2018 Commonwealth Games on Australia's Gold Coast, which will feature 38 para-sport medal events. Due to her condition, Cox prefers to focus on yearly goals rather than four-year cycles. Her immediate aim is London 2017, followed by the Commonwealth Games, and then she will consider Tokyo.

This win occurred on the same night Leicester lost 1-0 to Burnley and Middlesbrough drew 1-1 against West Bromwich Albion at home. Swansea, Leicester, and Middlesbrough are currently tied with 21 points, leading manager Paul Clement to remark that the table "looks more healthy." Swansea sits in 17th place, one spot above the automatic relegation zone, with Leicester in 18th and Middlesbrough in 15th. Swansea's recent success includes a stunning 3-2 win at Liverpool followed by the victory against Southampton at Liberty Stadium, secured by goals from Alfie Mawson and Gylfi Sigurdsson. Clement noted that the results have intensified the relegation battle. Clement's tenure began with an FA Cup defeat to Hull and a 4-0 home loss to Arsenal. However, the wins against Liverpool and Southampton have bolstered Swansea's hopes of survival, bringing their total wins to six, one more than Leicester and two more than Middlesbrough. This weekend, Tottenham Hotspur will host Middlesbrough on Saturday, while Swansea travels to Manchester City on Sunday at 13:30 GMT. Leicester will host Manchester United at 16:00 GMT. Sigurdsson scored the winning goal for Swansea after Shane Long equalized Mawson's opener. Clement praised the Icelandic midfielder, highlighting his set-piece delivery and work ethic. Clement expressed satisfaction with Swansea's performance, acknowledging it was mixed but ultimately a significant result against a strong opponent. He noted that Southampton had recently defeated Leicester 3-0 and won at Anfield in the EFL Cup, making the victory even more impressive. While acknowledging a shaky start, Clement praised Swansea's resilience and defensive efforts in the second half.
In the past five years, 272 organ donors in the UK with a history of cancer made donations that resulted in 675 transplants. Eye donation is a key area where these donors have helped. NHS Blood and Transplant data also shows that 1,033 people who had some types of cancer donated their eyes but not other organs. Officials state that there is a misconception that people with a history of cancer cannot be organ donors, but there are circumstances where donation is possible. The Advisory Committee on the Safety of Blood, Tissues and Organs says the risks of cancer transmission must be weighed against the risks of dying without a transplant. They state that organs from deceased donors with some cancers can be safely used for transplantation. The risk of donor-transmitted cancer in the UK is currently assessed as 0.06%. Prof John Forsythe, associate medical director for organ donation and transplantation at NHS Blood and Transplant, encourages people not to let health conditions or past illnesses prevent them from registering as donors. He emphasizes the importance of everyone, regardless of their health status, registering their decision to donate and informing their families of their wishes. NHS Blood and Transplant works to minimize risks to recipients by carefully evaluating all potential organ and tissue donors. About 70 cornea donations per week are needed to meet the demand for sight-saving transplants. However, one in ten people on the NHS Organ Donor Register do not want to donate their eyes. Alison Cooney, an aspiring midwife, died in 2010 at age 28, six weeks after being diagnosed with bowel and liver cancer. Her mother, Ann Cooney, agreed to donate her corneas, which helped save the sight of two people. She explained that while her major organs could not be donated due to the aggressive nature of the illness, her eyes could be used. Ann Cooney emphasizes that although not many people donate their eyes, it is one of the most successful procedures. She states that despite the initial difficulty of accepting the situation, they focused on achieving something good from something bad.
Shawn Tyson was found guilty of murdering James Cooper, 25, and James Kouzaris, 24, in Sarasota, Florida on April 16, 2011. Although an appeals court upheld his conviction, his sentence was overturned because he was 16 years old at the time of the crime. The case was sent back to a lower court for a new sentencing hearing. Associate Senior Judge James R. Case stated that Tyson's guilty verdicts were affirmed without further discussion. However, he emphasized that Tyson's status as a juvenile homicide offender necessitates a reassessment of his sentence. Judge Case cited a Supreme Court ruling that prohibits mandatory life sentences without parole for juveniles, as it violates the constitutional prohibition against "cruel and unusual" punishment. Mr. Cooper, from Hampton Lucy, Warwickshire, and Mr. Kouzaris, from Northampton, were killed after they drunkenly entered the Newtown area. Tyson, tried as an adult, was convicted of first-degree murder in March 2012.
Thirty volunteer bell-ringers were informed by York Minster that bell-ringing activities were suspended indefinitely at a meeting on Tuesday. Peter Sanderson, the ringing master for the past ten years, expressed his desire to understand the reason behind the decision. Rev. Vivienne Faull, the Dean of York Minster, stated that the suspension was due to health, safety, and security concerns. The Minster announced that the bells would remain silent until Easter, with plans to recruit a new head bell-ringer and peal team in the new year. A petition advocating for the reinstatement of the bell-ringers to ring the bells on Remembrance Sunday, Christmas Day, and New Year's Eve has garnered over 12,000 signatures. Sanderson described himself as "devastated, frustrated and bewildered" following the meeting. He urged the Dean to engage in dialogue to develop a workable plan that addresses the Minster's concerns, enabling the bells to ring again promptly. He believes that training a new team to meet the Minster's standards within three months would be an extraordinary feat. In an interview with BBC Radio York, the Dean explained that the bell-ringers were currently an independent organization that needed to be integrated into the Minster's structure. She emphasized the importance of prioritizing health, safety, and security in bell-ringing activities, stating that the Minster was reviewing all its staff and volunteer teams to ensure these aspects were properly managed.

GSK declined to comment on whether the SFO's investigation relates to any specific incident. The pharmaceutical giant, one of the UK's largest companies, also faces inquiries into similar allegations in Poland and Iraq. If proven, GSK's actions could violate both the UK Bribery Act and the US Foreign Corrupt Practices Act, which prohibit US and UK companies from bribing foreign government officials. The SFO confirmed it had launched a criminal investigation into GSK's commercial practices and its subsidiaries. The agency emphasized the value of whistleblowers and encouraged anyone with relevant information to come forward. In April, BBC Panorama featured Jarek Wisniewiski, a former GSK sales representative in Poland, who alleged doctors were paid to promote the company's asthma drug Seretide. Eleven doctors and a GSK regional manager in Poland were charged with corruption between 2010 and 2012, according to BBC Panorama. Last July, Chinese authorities began investigating GSK, detaining four Chinese executives. GSK has previously confirmed informing the SFO, the US Department of Justice, and the US Securities and Exchange Commission about the Chinese investigation.

Approximately 800 officers have been trained to administer immediate punishments to first-time offenders, diverting them from the criminal justice system. Restorative justice has been implemented in the county since 2011, with 190 offenders processed through the system to date. The system is only utilized when the crime victim consents. This alternative to traditional arrest and punishment is founded on three principles: acknowledging wrongdoing, making amends, and reintegrating into society. Inspector Dominic Human explained, "You have to acknowledge that what you've done is wrong, you do some reparation to put it right, and you reintegrate into society. So you move on very quickly, accepting that you've been given a once-in-a-lifetime opportunity not to become a criminal. On many occasions it's simply that a victim just wants an apology, and restorative justice allows that to happen." Trained officers consult with victims of minor crimes to determine appropriate punishments, then assess the fairness of the proposed solution. "We're not judge and jury at all," stated Insp Human. "We are simply facilitating an opportunity for someone to immediately say: 'I've done something wrong and I want to put it right'." Under the restorative project, youth offenders who vandalized a window cleaned all the windows on the house. A 16-year-old shoplifter who stole £3 worth of goods cleaned 100 shopping baskets, and a 12-year-old who caused £75 worth of damage to a car sent a written apology, with the parents paying for the repairs. Insp Human emphasized that the system saves "a massive amount of time" compared to arrest and court proceedings. "We can get the same outcome, in a very short time, at the scene. The motive for introducing restorative justice is because it's about doing the right thing for the victim. In the cases so far, we've saved over 1,000 operational policing hours which we can then use to concentrate on other important issues within the community." Cambridgeshire Constabulary plans to train all officers in restorative justice methods in the future.

Italy's third-largest bank, Monte dei Paschi, must secure €5 billion in new capital by the end of the month. A private sector bailout has not been arranged, so a state rescue could occur as early as this week. Considered the weakest major EU bank due to its bad loans, Monte dei Paschi faces pressure on the new government, led by Prime Minister Paolo Gentiloni, as EU bailout rules would leave private investors liable for any losses. Gentiloni characterized the potential rescue as a "precautionary measure" to protect savings and called on all political parties to share this responsibility. However, Economy Minister Pier Carlo Padoan emphasized that the funds would ensure banking system liquidity and support other struggling banks. Officials are also considering a scheme to compensate retail investors for potential losses. Gentiloni's predecessor, Matteo Renzi, resigned following a referendum defeat and was often criticized for his close ties to the banking sector.
Between 2010 and 2012, over 400 complete skeletons were unearthed during construction work at St John's College. These remains, dating back to the 13th to 15th centuries, belonged to individuals buried at the Hospital of St John the Evangelist. A four-year, £1.2 million study aims to shed light on the health of the urban poor during the Black Death epidemic. Additionally, approximately 1,000 body parts were discovered, making this one of the largest medieval hospital burial grounds ever found in Britain, according to a St John's College spokesperson. The majority of those buried were common townspeople rather than clergy, encompassing men, women, and children. The Black Death decimated millions across Europe. Researchers from archaeology, history, and genetics will collaborate to directly examine the epidemic's impact on a medieval town population before, during, and after the outbreak. The Hospital of St John the Evangelist, an Augustinian charitable institution, provided care to the public from approximately 1200 to 1500. The Wellcome Trust and St John's are funding this research.

A pilot study involving 261 teenage girls from three secondary schools found that a six-lesson body image course significantly improved their body image and self-esteem compared to the regular curriculum. Psychologists at King's College London conducted the study. One in three teenagers report dissatisfaction with their bodies. Poor body image has been linked to depression, eating disorders, excessive cosmetic surgery, obesity, and unhealthy weight loss practices. Dr. Helen Sharpe and her colleagues at the Institute of Psychiatry developed the positive body image course for teenage girls. The six-part program addressed beauty standards, negative peer interactions like "fat talking," and practical strategies for enhancing mood and self-esteem. Teachers delivered the course at three state-funded girls' schools. Dr. Sharpe stated that the research, published in The British Journal of Psychiatry, demonstrated the effectiveness of school-based lessons in improving body esteem. She expressed hope that further research will refine the program and potentially reduce eating disorders such as binge eating and unhealthy weight loss. Researchers emphasized the wide reach and low cost of teacher-led interventions, noting that some schools rely on unproven and potentially harmful programs. Last year, MPs recommended mandatory body image and self-esteem lessons for all schoolchildren. An inquiry by the All-Party Parliamentary Group on Body Image revealed that over half the population had a negative body image. The report stated that girls as young as five expressed concerns about their appearance, while cosmetic surgery rates had increased by nearly 20% since 2008.
Skiplagged finds cheap fares by searching for flights with layovers in a traveler's desired destination. United and Orbitz allege that Skiplagged engages in "unfair competition" and are seeking to recover lost revenue. The site's creator, 22-year-old Aktarer Zaman, maintains that he is exposing "inefficiencies" in airline ticketing and has not profited from Skiplagged. Skiplagged works by identifying longer flights with stops in major cities en route to other destinations. For example, a traveler could fly from New York to Lake Tahoe with a layover in San Francisco. If the traveler's actual destination is San Francisco, booking this flight could be cheaper than a direct flight from New York. Skiplagged claims travelers can save 40% or more on fares using this method, but it only works with one-way flights and requires passengers to travel without checked luggage. Zaman has launched a fundraising campaign to cover legal expenses against United and Orbitz. United and Orbitz claim in their legal filing that Skiplagged intentionally interferes with their business and violates contracts with partners. They argue that "hidden city" ticketing poses logistical and public safety risks and is prohibited, making Skiplagged's operation unlawful. The airlines are seeking at least $75,000 in damages for alleged revenue losses.
A memorial in Langstone commemorates the end of a four-year project that began with an effort to explore the lives of six World War Two servicemen. Research conducted by Langstone council uncovered details about ten additional men who had perished in World War One. Bishop of Monmouth, Right Reverend Richard Pain, dedicated the granite memorial in May on Saturday. The project was initiated when two Langstone council members sought to learn more about the six men listed on a World War Two memorial plaque in the village hall. Edward Jeffery, the community council chairman, not only uncovered the stories of those six but also revealed the identities of ten more men from Langstone, Llandevaud, Llanmartin, and nearby areas who died during World War One. The memorial features a time capsule filled with items reflecting the lives of children from Langstone Primary School and a QR code that provides visitors with information about the inscribed individuals.
This discovery will advance our understanding of the "strong force," which binds atomic nuclei. The Xi-cc++ particle, theoretically predicted, has been identified for the first time. Details were presented at a high-energy physics conference in Venice, based on research conducted at the LHCb experiment led by Dr. Patrick Spradlin of Glasgow University. He believes it will "shed light on a longstanding puzzle and open an exciting new branch of investigation." His colleague, Prof. Paul Soler, also from Glasgow University, calls it "a new frontier in understanding the strong force." Atoms' centers consist of neutrons and protons, which are composed of three quarks: light or heavy. There are six quark types, combining in various ways to form particles. Previously detected particles contained at most one heavy quark. This is the first confirmed existence of one with two heavy quarks. Prof. Guy Wilkinson of Oxford University notes a unique difference: unlike other particles of this type, where quarks dance around each other, a particle with two heavy quarks is predicted to act like a planetary system, with two heavy quarks orbiting each other, and a lighter quark orbiting this binary system. The research team will now measure the Xi-cc++'s properties to understand how this quark arrangement behaves and how the strong force holds it together. They anticipate finding more double heavy quark particles. Notably, this particle has two positive charges, double that of a proton, and is four times heavier. A paper detailing these findings has been submitted to the journal Physical Review Letters.
Leonel Oliviera stated that he rescued Laurene-Danielle Jackson, 19, from a crowded stairway at the Lava and Ignite club in Northampton. Both Jackson and Nabila Nanfuka, 22, were found unconscious and died from asphyxiation on October 18, 2011. Oliviera, 25, testified that he attempted CPR on Jackson but found no pulse. No criminal charges have been filed regarding the deaths. Oliviera described a fight breaking out around 3:15 GMT as people rushed to leave the club following repeated announcements about departing coaches. He said the altercation changed the crowd's mood, creating a chaotic and frenzied atmosphere. He recalled carrying an unconscious man back inside and then rescuing another unconscious woman. Jackson, a psychology student at Kingston University from Wembley, London, died at Leicester's Glenfield Hospital on November 6. Nanfuka, a leisure and tourism student at Northampton University from Neasden, London, died at Northampton General Hospital on October 19. Her friend Olabanji Gellatly-Olanrewaju witnessed five people falling on top of her. He said she rolled onto her back to try and free herself, and someone helped carry her outside, where she was unresponsive. The club's former manager, Spencer Fitzgerald, told the jury he tried to manage the situation by bringing additional security personnel to the cloakroom area. He described attempting to calm the crowd but ultimately feeling overwhelmed and stuck against the wall. The court heard that approximately 1,700 people attended the "Wickedest Wickedest" RnB night, traveling from across the UK. CCTV footage from inside the club was presented to the jury. The inquest is ongoing.
A Spanish magistrate decided there wasn't enough evidence to charge the 62-year-old driver with negligent homicide. Seven of the thirteen people killed in the Catalonia crash were Italian. Alessandro Saracino, the parent of one victim, expressed shock and stated that the families might appeal the decision. Catalan police previously suggested that the plane's black box showed several speed changes before the crash. However, the court in Amposta ruled there was no evidence of speeding or mechanical failure. They also stated that the driver was not impaired by drugs or alcohol. The accident occurred on March 20th near Freginals, 150 kilometers south of Barcelona. Most passengers were exchange students returning to Barcelona from a fireworks festival in Valencia. Victims ranged in age from 19 to 25; seven were from Italy, two from Germany, and one each from Romania, Austria, France, and Uzbekistan. The court's decision can be appealed.

Over the next three years, resources will be distributed among approximately 70,000 non-profits and 900 university research projects. Cloud computing refers to storing data on the internet rather than on local computers. This makes data more accessible and provides non-profits with vast computing power, usually only available to large businesses. Microsoft's chief legal officer, Brad Smith, stated that cloud services can unlock data's potential, leading to new insights and breakthroughs in science, technology, economics, and social issues. Non-profits tackling complex social problems can benefit from "big data" analysis, which requires significant processing power provided by cloud computing. Microsoft's commitment is to provide access to servers and services that businesses typically pay high fees for. Additionally, funds will be allocated to improve internet connectivity in underserved areas, aiming to bridge the digital divide. Other companies, such as Facebook, have similar initiatives. Facebook's Internet.org project uses technologies like drones to expand internet access, particularly for the "next billion" people online. However, these initiatives have faced criticism. Facebook's Free Basics program, offering free access to certain mobile sites, sparked controversy in India due to concerns about unfair competition with local businesses. Microsoft is likely to encounter similar accusations, as the donation could be seen as a strategy to integrate organizations into the Microsoft cloud ecosystem long-term. Despite this, Microsoft CEO Satya Nadella has been praised for his commitment to using Microsoft's resources to benefit developing countries, including his native India. Nadella emphasizes Microsoft's potential to provide computing power for initiatives beyond large corporations. He questions how to ensure universal access to the benefits of cloud computing and data analytics.

The report identified the need for more time and careful planning due to the project's complexity, involving a new tunnel and railway line. Consultations with landowners and stakeholders also contribute to the delay. Businesses in Reading, who see the link as crucial for attracting talent and investment, expressed disappointment. Fiona Mactaggart, Slough's Labour MP, criticized the government for the delay, calling it a "failure of leadership." Tim Brownstone, head of sportswear company Kymira, emphasized the rail link's importance for attracting external investment to the area. Initially approved by the government in 2012, the service was planned to be operational by 2021, potentially saving passengers traveling from south Wales, the west of England, and the Thames Valley up to 30 minutes.

South Yorkshire Police emphasized that combating firearms gangs was their top priority. Assistant Chief Constable Rachel Barber stated that while firearms were present, their actual use in the county was "fairly limited." ACC Barber explained that the number of gangs was comparable to the county's population and not all were actively operating. She noted that "the reality is, most of the offending is against each other." The report highlighted the force's aim to determine how guns, primarily reactivated firearms, enter the county. It also disclosed gang involvement in immigration and child sexual exploitation. The report was presented at a Public Accountability Board meeting led by Dr Alan Billings, the South Yorkshire PCC.
