A fire devastated a nursing home within a Tainan City hospital, claiming the lives of numerous elderly and mostly bedridden patients due to smoke inhalation. Injured residents received treatment at various hospitals. Authorities arrested a man suspected of starting the fire, reportedly a patient at the nursing home. The facility housed over 100 individuals, many reliant on respirators and unable to move independently. An escapee described the scene as "pitch black" with "unbearable" smoke. A 94-year-old woman used her wheelchair to flee, taking shelter on the second floor until rescuers located her. Thirty fire engines extinguished the blaze by 4:16 local time, approximately 45 minutes after its initial report. Tainan Mayor Lai Ching-te attributed the severity of the situation to the hospital's remote location and the patients' limited mobility. Firefighters arrived ten minutes after the fire began, but residents assisted in transporting some patients to other hospitals due to the large number of individuals requiring aid. This incident is considered one of Taiwan's deadliest hospital fires and a major tragedy in recent years. Premier Sean Chen expressed condolences to the victims' families.
The UK government is seeking a contractor to provide training for civil servants on trade policy. This training aims to equip staff across Whitehall with a comprehensive understanding of the UK's trade priorities, those of key partners, and relevant terminology. This initiative is driven by a perceived lack of expertise in trade negotiations following the UK's departure from the EU, as highlighted by former UK ambassador to the EU Sir Ivan Rogers. The EU previously handled trade negotiations on behalf of the UK, with many UK trade specialists based in Brussels. The training program, managed by KPMG, will focus on international trade policy and negotiations, aiming to enable officials to support and deliver the UK's trade policy agenda both domestically and internationally. While the UK cannot currently negotiate trade deals, the government is exploring potential opportunities. Ministers have indicated a high level of interest from countries like Australia, New Zealand, and Canada in signing bilateral agreements with the UK. President Trump has expressed a desire for an early trade deal with the UK post-Brexit. To bolster its trade promotion capabilities, the Treasury has provided additional funding to the Department for International Trade and the Foreign Office. The civil service is committed to training existing staff for their new roles and may also recruit external expertise. The number of UK trade policy experts has increased significantly since the referendum.
Eleven-year-old Moira Anderson vanished in February 1957 after leaving her grandmother's house in Coatbridge to go to the shops. Alexander Gartshore, a bus driver and convicted pedophile who died in 2006, is suspected of her murder. Police are currently investigating an area of Monkland Canal in hopes of finding her remains. Moira was last seen on February 23, 1957, during a heavy snowstorm. She boarded a Baxter's bus driven by Gartshore while running an errand. Later that year, Gartshore was imprisoned for raping a 17-year-old babysitter. In 1999, convicted child abuser James Gallogley implicated Gartshore as Moira's killer. Gartshore's daughter, Sandra Brown, believed he was responsible and campaigned for his prosecution. In 2014, prosecutors announced that Gartshore would have faced trial for Moira's murder if he were still alive. A witness reported seeing a tall man carrying a large, heavy sack towards the canal the morning after Moira disappeared in 1957, but the sighting was not investigated. Four years ago, a grave in Old Monkland Cemetery in Coatbridge was exhumed during the search, but no evidence linking Moira to the grave was found.
Charlie Naysmith discovered a 600g piece of ambergris while examining seaweed at Hengistbury Head in Bournemouth. Initially, he believed it was a regular stone, but after researching it online with his father, Alex, they determined it was likely a rare type of sperm whale vomit. Ambergris, found within sperm whales, is used in perfumes after being expelled. Its rarity and ability to float in the ocean for years before washing ashore make it highly sought after. Mr. Naysmith, from Christchurch, estimated the lump to be around 20 years old and mentioned it could be worth up to £40,000. He stated, "It's very rare. You don't hear of it coming around very often and it's only found in small amounts usually. In olden days they used it in perfumes and some medicines. I don't know if it's necessarily worth that much these days, but fingers crossed." Charlie shared his plans to establish a nature reserve for animals in the country if they successfully sell the ambergris. Ambergris, revered for millennia, has been dubbed "floating gold" by scientists and those hoping to find a fortune in the ocean. This natural excretion, believed to aid sperm whale digestion, is expelled from their abdomen, often hundreds of kilometers from land. Giant squid beaks, a primary food source for sperm whales, are frequently found within ambergris. Initially soft and foul-smelling, it floats on the ocean surface. Over years of exposure to sunlight and saltwater, it transforms into a smooth, compact, waxy lump with a pleasant scent. While still used in perfumes, many manufacturers now opt for synthetic alternatives due to concerns about whaling and the vulnerable status of sperm whales.

International passenger numbers surged by 11.6% to 269,235, driven by new easyJet routes and an additional daily Lufthansa service to Frankfurt. Domestic passenger numbers also increased by 4.9%, with 338,488 passengers traveling through Edinburgh Airport last month. The airport attributed this growth to a rise in passengers traveling to Heathrow with Virgin Atlantic Little Red and to Gatwick with British Airways. He noted that Turkish Airlines' daily flight on larger aircraft resulted in a 97.7% increase in passenger numbers compared to the previous year. Dewar also anticipates a busy summer schedule with the launch of 10 new routes between March and June, including Chicago, Philadelphia, and Doha in May. Aberdeen Airport saw a 6.5% increase in passenger numbers, with 255,416 passengers passing through last month. The airport reported growth in both fixed-wing and helicopter operations. Len Allchurch had a long career in football, playing over 600 League matches from 1950 to 1971. He played a key role in Sheffield United's promotion to the First Division in the 1960-61 season. He was the younger brother of the famous Welsh international, Ivor Allchurch. Len earned 11 caps for Wales and was part of the squad for the 1958 World Cup, although he did not appear in any matches. Cliff Jones, a former teammate of Allchurch at Swansea and Wales, spoke fondly of him. They grew up together, played football together, and even worked together in the docks before becoming professional footballers. Jones described Allchurch as a skilled right winger with great control, temperament, and a wonderful personality. The 1950s were a remarkable period for Swansea Town, producing some of the finest Welsh footballers ever. Alongside the Allchurch brothers, John and Mel Charles, and Tottenham Hotspur legends Cliff Jones and Terry Medwin all emerged from the club. Len Allchurch played 276 League games for Swansea Town before joining Sheffield United for £18,000 in 1961. He scored five goals in his last seven games that season, helping Sheffield United achieve promotion. After leaving Sheffield in 1965, he joined Stockport County, contributing to their Fourth Division title win in 1966-67. In 1969, he returned to his hometown club, now known as Swansea City, making a further 71 League appearances before retiring in 1971. While Len Allchurch was part of the Welsh squad for the 1958 World Cup in Sweden, he did not play alongside his brother, as Wales reached the quarter-finals before losing 1-0 to Brazil, with Pele scoring the winning goal. He did, however, play in the 2-0 victory over Israel in the first leg of the play-off which secured Wales' place in the World Cup finals for the first and only time.

The British pound fell to its lowest point against the dollar since 1985, reaching $1.3058, and dropped to its lowest level against the euro since 2013 at €1.1703. Analysts attributed this decline to a disappointing report on the UK services sector and ongoing uncertainty surrounding the UK's exit from the European Union. Wall Street also exhibited caution, with shares closing lower. In London, shares of property firms and asset managers experienced a sharp decline after insurance giant Aviva became the second firm in two days to suspend trading in a UK property fund following the Brexit vote. Aviva cited "extraordinary market circumstances" for the immediate freeze. Standard Life Investments had suspended trading in its UK property fund on Monday. Mark Priest of ETX Capital expressed concern, stating that investors were becoming increasingly nervous and comparing the situation to the financial crisis. He noted that fears about the investment industry were impacting foreign exchange markets, leading to continued selling pressure on the pound after a brief period of stability following the Brexit vote. The FTSE 100 index initially fell but recovered after the Bank of England announced measures to support the economy. The FTSE 250, which comprises more UK-focused companies, also recovered from its lows but still closed 382 points, or 2.4%, lower at 15,734.68. Persimmon, a housebuilder, saw its shares fall 7.2% despite reporting a rise in half-year revenues. However, the news from Standard Life Investments and Aviva cast a pall over the entire property sector, causing shares in Barratt Developments to drop 9.8% and Berkeley Group to close 6.4% lower. Standard Life Investments stated that the number of investors requesting withdrawals had increased following the EU referendum, leading to the suspension to protect the interests of all fund investors. The £2.9 billion fund invests in a mix of commercial real estate in the UK, including office buildings, shopping centers, and warehouses.
A plane purchased for $67.9 million to combat the Afghan drug trade remains in storage in Delaware. The plane was intended for a DEA and Pentagon anti-drug program, but modification and hangar costs ballooned, exceeding the original estimate fourfold. The report states that DEA aviation officials failed to consider the time and cost of establishing a support infrastructure for the aircraft, including pilots, mechanics, trainers, and spare parts. Despite being purchased over seven years ago, the plane remains inoperable and has never flown in Afghanistan. A DEA official indicated the plane would eventually be used in the Caribbean and Latin America, a purpose that differed from the original funding intent. This revelation follows a US government watchdog's accusation of millions being wasted on poorly planned reconstruction projects in Afghanistan. One example cited involved $6 million spent importing rare Italian goats to bolster the Afghan cashmere industry, with oversight so inadequate that the goats' fate remains uncertain. Additionally, $43 million was reportedly spent on a vehicle refueling station in Afghanistan, 140 times the cost of a similar station in Pakistan, potentially due to fraud and corruption.
Following an inspection of Western Isles Hospital in Stornoway in September, the Healthcare Environment Inspectorate (HEI) requested the hospital establish a policy for handling and storing breast milk. Inspectors found no record of the temperature at which breast milk was stored and no staff policy in place. However, the HEI stated that the hospital was otherwise clean and met standards. The inspection resulted in one requirement regarding breast milk and no recommendations. Jacqui Macrae, head of quality of care for HEI, said: "This was a good inspection where we found that the ward environment and all patient equipment we inspected was clean. We also saw good staff compliance with standard infection control precautions." NHS Western Isles chairman Dr Neil Galbraith said: "The board takes a close interest in maintaining the standards of cleanliness within our hospitals because of the high correlation with patient safety. The board welcomes the inspection findings, which largely corroborate the Western Isles Hospital's own self-assessment report and provide external reassurance that our cleanliness and anti-infection regimes are effective." Dr Galbraith added: "The board notes the one requirement emerging from the inspection and will look to have the policy paper produced, and related practice implemented, with immediate effect."
LOROS, a palliative care center in Leicester, is expanding to accommodate an additional 250 patients annually. A new teaching center is also under construction. Currently, LOROS supports 2,500 people with terminal illnesses as residents or day visitors. Alan Birchenall, Leicester City club ambassador and charity patron, marked the beginning of construction. The former Leicester City player, known as "The Birch," also performed the ceremonial turf cutting when LOROS moved to its current location 31 years ago, this time accompanied by the Premier League trophy. In addition to the day therapy unit expansion, the charity, which serves Leicestershire and Rutland, is building a professional development center to share its expertise. John Knight, LOROS chief executive, stated that the center will expand upon the existing education center and provide a dedicated space for learning about palliative care. He believes this initiative will allow LOROS to extend its influence beyond its on-site services.
A BBC One nature documentary leads the nominations for the annual Craft Awards with nine, which recognize behind-the-scenes television talent. Netflix's drama "The Crown" follows with seven nominations, while the BBC's "The Night Manager" has six. The winners will be announced on April 23rd, three weeks before the Bafta Television Awards on May 14th. The nature documentary is nominated for two awards in editing: factual and sound: factual, as well as the original music prize. Its remaining four nominations are in the photography: factual category, guaranteeing at least one win. "The Crown" has nominations for direction and writing for Stephen Daldry and Peter Morgan, respectively. It is also nominated for costume design, production design, photography and lighting, visual effects, and titles. "The Night Manager" received a best director nomination for Susanne Bier and recognition for its sound and editing. Like "The Crown," it has nominations for production design, visual effects, and titles. The BBC's adaptation of Tolstoy's novel is nominated for best make-up and hair design, likely due to Paul Dano's mutton chops. It also received nominations for music, sound, production design, and visual effects. Charlie Brooker's satirical series "Black Mirror" moved from Channel 4 to Netflix last year and received nominations for photography on the episode starring Bryce Dallas Howard, costume design, visual effects, and make-up and hair design. Channel 4's topical drama about a TV personality accused of historical sex crimes was recognized for direction, editing, music, and photography.
Footage of a gamekeeper using a decoy bird near grouse shooting land in the Derbyshire Peak District in February has sparked controversy. Conservation group Raptor Persecution released the footage, captured by birdwatchers from a distance, and called for action. Derbyshire Police investigated but decided against further action. However, the National Trust launched its own investigation, stating they take the incident "very seriously." Raptor Persecution believes the decoy bird, possibly designed to resemble a male hen harrier, was intended to lure another bird, allowing the gamekeeper to shoot it. While they acknowledge no illegal activity occurred, they express concern about the potential for harm to hen harriers, which are known to be territorial and aggressive. The RSPB, while acknowledging the legal use of decoy hawks and owls for crow control, expressed worry that the footage might depict an illegal attempt to target hen harriers or other birds of prey. The Peak District National Park's chief executive described the video as "alarming and suspicious" and pledged support for the investigation. The Moorland Association, while condemning wildlife crime, maintains that no crime was committed in this instance, cautioning against making assumptions about the video's content or intentions. Derbyshire's Police and Crime Commissioner highlighted the ongoing need to protect wildlife, noting that while a man with a gun and a plastic hen harrier decoy was observed, the distance prevented obtaining clear evidence for prosecution. The RSPB considers hen harriers the most persecuted birds of prey in the UK, and their predation of grouse often leads to conflict on shooting grounds. In 2015, the disappearance of five male hen harriers from various sites in England resulted in the collapse of several nests, with the reason for their disappearance still unknown.
Bronze Age artifacts were unearthed in Wenvoe, Vale of Glamorgan, during August 2014 and April 2015. An early medieval piece, likely part of a gilt bronze or silver brooch, was discovered in Llanarmon-yn-Iâl, Denbighshire, also in August 2014. An early medieval silver ingot, found in Trelawnyd and Gwaenysgor, Flintshire, was also declared treasure. The Bronze Age axe and spearheads, found by Gwyn Rees, are believed to have been buried in a ritual ceremony approximately 3,500 years ago. National Museum Wales has expressed interest in purchasing these items. The brooch, featuring a gold baseplate adorned with a spherical blue glass bead set in a gold collar, was found by Karina Kucharski. Dr. Mark Redknap of National Museum Wales stated, "Early medieval goldwork from this period is rarely discovered in Wales. This find significantly enhances our comprehension of early medieval jewelry, its distribution, and the goldworking techniques employed during this time." The silver ingot, found by Francis Hodge, is sought after by Flintshire museums.

This sets the stage for a highly anticipated clash between the two undefeated teams at Twickenham on March 12th. Wasps second row Davies acknowledged the challenge, stating, "It's a big challenge for us as a pack, but it's the sort of thing we thrive on." He emphasized the importance of winning the set-piece battle, saying, "For me, up front is where the game is won or lost. The English pack try to dominate you so we've got to raise the standard again and hopefully take it to them." Davies expressed confidence in Wales' ability to improve throughout the tournament, noting their past successes at Twickenham, including victories in the 2015 World Cup and during their 2008 and 2012 Grand Slam campaigns. They are aiming for their first back-to-back wins at the home of English rugby since 1978. "We tend to get better the more weeks we get together," he told BBC Wales Sport. Last week, Swiss authorities initiated criminal proceedings against the 79-year-old Blatter. Tracey Crouch, the Sports Minister, stated that Blatter's departure would expedite wide-scale reforms within FIFA. Major FIFA sponsors, including Coca-Cola, Visa, Budweiser, and McDonald's, urged Blatter to resign on Friday, citing the ongoing damage to FIFA's image and reputation. However, Blatter affirmed his intention to remain in office until 2016. Swiss prosecutors accuse Blatter of signing a contract detrimental to FIFA and making an improper payment to UEFA president Michel Platini, although Blatter denies any wrongdoing. In a statement released through his lawyers, Blatter asserted that resigning prematurely would not be in FIFA's best interest or advance the reform process. Greg Dyke, chairman of the Football Association, characterized the recent developments as a significant turning point. Blatter was re-elected for a fifth term as president of FIFA in May, but the election was overshadowed by the arrest of seven FIFA officials in Zurich at the request of the United States. The US subsequently indicted 14 current and former FIFA officials and associates on charges of widespread corruption following a comprehensive investigation by the Federal Bureau of Investigation. Blatter subsequently announced his intention to step down as president in February 2016. He maintains that the £1.5 million payment made to Platini in 2011 was legitimate compensation for his services as Blatter's technical advisor between 1999 and 2002. Platini, 60, has also denied any wrongdoing and asserted that the payment was for his work as an advisor. The contract deemed "unfavorable to FIFA" by Swiss prosecutors is believed to be a 2005 television rights agreement between FIFA and Jack Warner, the former president of Concacaf. An investigation by Swiss broadcaster SRF in September alleged that the deal resulted in a substantial profit for Warner's company. Separately, Swiss authorities are investigating the bidding process for the 2018 and 2022 World Cups, which will be hosted by Russia and Qatar, respectively.
Warnings about patient safety risks due to NHS pressures were issued at both the Royal College of Nursing and British Medical Association conferences. Delegates at both events described the severity of the situation, with the RCN presenting a dossier detailing instances of frail elderly patients being moved at night and patients receiving treatment in corridors and storerooms. Doctors at their conference in Belfast emphasized the lack of hospital beds, stating that the NHS was "bursting at the seams." They cited OECD figures showing that countries like Belgium, France, Austria, Germany, and Romania have more than double the number of beds per capita compared to the UK. In England, the number of hospital beds has decreased by over 20% in the past decade to just over 103,000, leading to dangerously full hospitals, according to BMA leader Dr. Mark Porter. He expressed concerns about increased infection risks and the likelihood of patients being placed on the wrong ward. Ear, nose, and throat surgeon Mr. Michael Hardingham from Cheltenham went further, stating that patients were being harmed due to early discharges caused by bed shortages. Doctors overwhelmingly passed a motion condemning the reduction in bed capacity. Nurses at the RCN conference in Glasgow shared examples of extreme measures hospitals were taking to cope with the pressure, including the use of corridors and storerooms for patient care. RCN general secretary Janet Davies described the NHS as being in a state of "endless winter" that was creating chaos. Emergency care nurse Janet Youd from West Yorkshire expressed concerns about the safety of care in emergency departments. However, a Department of Health spokeswoman maintained that the NHS was "performing well" despite the pressures and emphasized patient safety as a priority, stating their commitment to making the NHS the safest and most transparent healthcare system in the world.
Lionsgate is remaking the 1987 film *Dirty Dancing*, which starred Jennifer Grey and Patrick Swayze. Kenny Ortega, who choreographed the original, will direct the remake. Ortega, known for directing *This Is It* and *High School Musical*, expressed his excitement about returning to the project, stating that it feels like coming home. He plans to assemble a talented team and cast to introduce *Dirty Dancing* to a new audience. Ortega praised Patrick Swayze's influence on male dance in film, comparing him to legends like Gene Kelly and Fred Astaire. The original romantic film, set in the 1960s, follows a teenage girl who falls in love with her dance instructor while on vacation with her family. Its soundtrack was a massive hit, featuring chart-topping songs like "Time of My Life" and "Hungry Eyes." The film has also been adapted into a successful stage show, which recently concluded a five-year run in the West End and will embark on a UK tour in September. Grey, 51, who played Baby in the original, won the US show *Dancing with the Stars* last year. Swayze, who played Johnny, passed away from pancreatic cancer in September 2009 at the age of 57.
Traffic at the airport decreased by 8.1% year-on-year to slightly over 300,000 in May. Helicopter traffic declined by 13.1%, while fixed wing traffic decreased by 7.2%. Airport managing director Carol Benzie stated that passenger traffic decreased across all three sectors: helicopters, international, and domestic. She attributed the drop to "the impact of cost efficiencies currently being implemented throughout the oil and gas sector." These figures contrasted with strong performance reported by both Edinburgh and Glasgow airports. Glasgow Airport experienced its busiest May in seven years, with 785,063 passengers traveling through its terminals, a 13.3% increase from the same period last year. International traffic grew by 17%, and domestic traffic increased by 9.1%. Glasgow Airport managing director Amanda McMillan said, "May has been a remarkable month with the launch of 10 new services, including several firsts for Glasgow, Scotland, and the UK. Following our record-breaking April, we've also been recognized as one of the fastest-growing airports in Europe this year by ACI Europe. Our growth in 2015 has been truly unprecedented, reflected in the fact we've enjoyed consistent double-digit increases in our passenger numbers every month since November." Edinburgh Airport recorded over one million passengers last month, marking its busiest May on record. The airport attributed the growth largely to new routes launched within the past year.

Deciding to investigate because the club was not typically used on weekends, he entered and was met by a group of women who were thrilled to see him, believing him to be the male stripper they had hired. Mr. Ober explained, "The party was about to begin and they thought I was early." He added, "I had to leave quickly." Mr. Ober said there were no cars in the parking lot and the door was open, prompting his investigation. He recounted, "As I walked in, there were loud cheers and someone shouted, 'Oh, are you the stripogram?'" Informed that he was early and to return later, Mr. Ober departed, passing the actual stripper on his way out. He humorously remarked, "It's a matter of personal taste, but the uniforms aren't very flattering, so I guess the mix-up is flattering."

The European Commission announced a plan to increase spending on military assets like drones and helicopters, while also funding innovative defense technologies. This plan aims to reverse a decade of defense spending cuts by EU members, which have amounted to nearly 12% in real terms. The plan comes amidst concerns about potential Russian aggression and President-elect Donald Trump's stance on NATO, as he has stated that he may reduce US security commitments to the alliance if member states do not contribute their fair share. Brexit has also played a role in the plan's creation, as the UK has historically opposed Franco-German efforts to enhance the EU's security capabilities. European Commission President Jean-Claude Juncker emphasized the need for increased investment and cooperation among member states. He stated, "To guarantee our collective security, we must invest in the common development of technologies and equipment of strategic importance - from land, air, sea and space capabilities to cyber security. If Europe does not take care of its own security, nobody else will do it for us." The plan includes increasing the "research window" of the new defense fund from €25m to €90m by 2020 to cover areas like robotics and encrypted software. A "capability window" aims to mobilize €5bn annually from member states to jointly purchase equipment such as drones and helicopters. The Commission will manage these tenders. The Commission highlights that while defense spending in EU countries has declined, military cooperation has not improved, resulting in a cost to the bloc of between €25bn and €100bn annually. The European Council, composed of EU heads of state and government, is expected to discuss the defense plan later this month.

Maria Sharapova, a 28-year-old five-time Grand Slam champion, announced on Monday that she tested positive for meldonium in January. The Russian player expressed gratitude for the support she received, stating that the messages of love and compassion brought a smile to her face. Facing a potential four-year ban, Sharapova emphasized her determination to return to tennis. She acknowledged the difficult situation but expressed her commitment to overcoming it. Meldonium, originally developed to treat diabetes and heart conditions, is believed to enhance stamina and endurance by increasing oxygen flow to muscles. It was added to the World Anti-Doping Agency's (WADA) list of banned substances on January 1, 2016. Sharapova, who claims to have taken meldonium for ten years for health reasons, has experienced the termination or postponement of some sponsorship deals. A pigeon covered in pink paint was found at a car wash in Blackburn on Wednesday morning. RSPCA staff are unsure if the bird fell into something or was deliberately painted. Inspector Nina Small, who has worked for the RSPCA for 15 years, said she had never seen anything like it. The pigeon's eyes were the only parts of its body that were not covered in paint. Even after a wash, the pigeon's feathers remained stained. Inspector Small expressed concern that someone may have intentionally painted the pigeon and wondered if other animals in the area were at risk. She hopes to remove all the paint and release the pigeon back into the wild, but warned that its ability to fly may be affected if its feathers are permanently damaged. There is also a possibility that the pigeon ingested toxic paint while trying to clean itself.

This initiative, a collaboration between the Labour Party and the Labour Women's Network, will train and mentor women interested in positions such as MPs, councillors, elected mayors, and other party roles. Labour MP Rachel Reeves stated that Jo Cox, who tragically died in June after being shot and stabbed, was a strong advocate for women in leadership and politics. She served as the first chair of the Labour Women's Network and actively encouraged other women to pursue parliamentary positions. The program was inspired by Cox's passion for this cause. It follows a call from female Labour MPs to party leader Jeremy Corbyn to address personal abuse during the leadership campaign, with some accusing him of not doing enough to combat intolerance and misogyny. Notably, the Conservative Party, SNP, DUP, Plaid Cymru, UKIP, and Green Party are all currently led by women. A report called 'Ready to Read' found that 65% of five-year-old children from disadvantaged backgrounds had below average vocabulary test scores, compared to 38% of more affluent children. The report, produced by the Read On Get On coalition, analyzed data from 1,955 children in 63 council wards across Northern Ireland. It recommends increased investment in early childhood education and additional support for parents. Save the Children, a member of the coalition alongside organizations like Libraries NI, Parenting NI, and Early Years, published the research. Fergus Cooper, head of Save the Children in Northern Ireland, emphasized the importance of parental involvement in a child's education for literacy development. He stated that while all parents want their children to succeed, many are unaware of the key actions to reinforce learning. Cooper stressed the significance of early language development as a foundation for good reading skills, calling for earlier investment and engagement. Black Mountain Primary School in west Belfast, with nearly 150 pupils, has collaborated with Save the Children for several years. The school encourages parents to read with their children during the school day. Stacey Brittan, whose son AJ attends Black Mountain Primary School, shared how the school's approach has benefited them both. Brittan admitted to not having enjoyed reading herself but now reads with AJ every night, resulting in a significant improvement in his reading skills and enjoyment. Elaine Crossman, another parent, highlighted the value of the school's program, allowing her to read with her son Riley despite her work schedule. She praised the opportunity for one-on-one time with her son and observed his reading progress. Principal Jill Black noted the positive impact of parental involvement on reading skills, particularly in Primary One and Two. She attributed the increased enthusiasm for reading to the initiative, which has also strengthened teacher-parent relationships and improved pupils' reading test scores. Black emphasized that parents' enjoyment of reading positively influences their children's achievement. The 'Ready to Read' report incorporates new analysis by University College London of a Northern Ireland sample from the Millennium Cohort Study, a long-term study of thousands of UK children. The UK-wide Read On Get On organization aims to ensure all children read well by age 11 by 2025.
The High Court ruled against Govia Thameslink Railway (GTR), Southern's parent company, which argued that the strike would violate customer rights. This decision allowed the first of three planned strike days to proceed on Tuesday. Southern warned that the strike would result in no services on most routes. The RMT union, which has been striking since the summer over conductor roles, also plans further stoppages around Christmas. Aslef, celebrating the court ruling, stated that GTR's actions were a waste of money for shareholders, passengers, and taxpayers. Aslef's general secretary, Mick Whelan, urged GTR to negotiate in good faith with the union to reach a deal beneficial to passengers, staff, and the company. A GTR spokesperson expressed disappointment with the decision, emphasizing their commitment to passenger well-being. They stated their intention to appeal the ruling to the Court of Appeal. Southern had claimed that the strike's consequences constituted a breach of customer travel rights under EU law. However, the judge, Sir Michael Burton, ruled that while the strikes would cause significant disruption, they were legally sanctioned and did not unlawfully restrict freedom of movement. He acknowledged GTR's attempt to circumvent union parliamentary immunity but deemed it unsuccessful. MP Nick Herbert called for the government to explore alternative measures to address "unjustifiable" strike action, potentially involving legal changes.

Sgt Bergdahl, freed by the Taliban in May after five years of captivity, faces potential court-martial proceedings. The charges stem from his disappearance in Afghanistan in June 2009, where fellow soldiers allege he intentionally left his unit. He is accused of desertion with intent to shirk duty and misbehavior before the enemy by endangering the safety of his command. Desertion carries a maximum sentence of five years, while the misbehavior charge could result in life imprisonment. A preliminary hearing will determine if he faces a court-martial, similar to a civilian grand jury proceeding. The US Army announced the charges at Fort Bragg, North Carolina, stating that the Army Forces Command reviewed the investigation into Sgt Bergdahl's disappearance before bringing the charges. His release, part of a controversial prisoner swap involving five Taliban commanders held at Guantanamo Bay, sparked a political firestorm. Republicans and some Democrats criticized the swap, arguing it violated US law and risked American lives. They also objected to Congress not being informed beforehand. The deal, brokered by Qatar, has led to death threats against Sgt Bergdahl's family and the cancellation of a welcome home party in his Idaho hometown due to safety concerns.

Schools across Scotland were closed, homes lost power, and transportation was severely disrupted due to the storm. Train, flight, and ferry services were canceled, and bridges were closed. The Met Office downgraded the alert from amber to yellow for wind and snow, which impacted much of the UK. The Scottish Environment Protection Agency issued over 40 flood warnings. Shetland experienced the strongest winds, leading Loganair to cancel 11 flights in and out of the island. In Edinburgh, a man was injured by flying debris and hospitalized in stable condition. High winds caused damage to overhead power lines, resulting in delays and cancellations on train routes between Edinburgh and London. ScotRail announced widespread cancellations and reduced services. Flooding near Pitlochry shut down the line between Perth and Inverness. The storm caused significant travel problems for drivers, with numerous bridges closed across Scotland. While some bridges, including the Forth Road Bridge and the Tay Bridge, reopened to cars, they remained closed to high-sided vehicles. Several lorries overturned due to strong winds, prompting emergency services to respond. A tree fell onto a bus in Edinburgh, but there were no reported injuries. In Aberdeen, a trampoline blown onto a flyover caused a hazard for drivers. A landslide closed a section of the A82, creating a lengthy diversion. Thousands lost power due to strong winds and lightning strikes. Scottish Hydro crews restored power to 11,000 customers, but 2,800 homes and businesses remained without electricity. West coast ferry operator Caledonian MacBrayne experienced widespread cancellations and delays. Schools on the Western Isles, Orkney, and Shetland were closed, along with several schools in the Highlands.

The spokesperson added that it remains a devolved issue and that abuse compensation is one reason Brokenshire wants to restore the Assembly. He continues to urge parties to find a solution to restore the executive so a response to Sir Anthony Hart's report can be provided as soon as possible. Margaret McGuckian, founder of Survivors and Victims of Institutional Abuse (Savia), criticized Brokenshire's comments as a "coward's way out" and accused him of ignoring victims. She stated that Brokenshire has the power and, in a meeting with the head of the Civil Service, acknowledged that emergency legislation is the only way forward. Savia will now protest in Westminster. Mike Nesbitt, an Ulster Unionist MLA and former Victims Commissioner, expressed confusion over Brokenshire's position. He noted that Brokenshire previously used Westminster legislation to address issues with the budget and the lack of a legal mechanism to restore the Assembly. Nesbitt argued that Brokenshire should provide a small interim payment to victims, which would be a negligible amount compared to the executive budget. He criticized the situation as disgraceful and stated that victims are dying, losing their homes, and struggling to care for their families. Nesbitt believes that all five main Northern Ireland political parties agree that something must be done to address the needs of victims. There have been previous calls for Brokenshire to act on compensation payments for abuse victims. Former first minister Peter Robinson stated in July that parties at Stormont would not oppose Brokenshire beginning compensation payments immediately. Sir Anthony Hart, who led the HIA Inquiry, recommended compensation, a memorial, and a public apology to abuse survivors. He suggested a tax-free lump sum payment for all survivors, including those from institutions not covered by the inquiry.

An investigation into Princess Cruise Lines began after a tip-off alleging illegal dumping of oily waste off the coast of England in 2013. The company also allegedly attempted to conceal its actions. Princess Cruise Lines has agreed to plead guilty to seven charges related to stops at ports in nine US states and two territories. The charges involve the Caribbean Princess cruise ship, which allegedly engaged in illegal discharges since 2005, the year it began operations. The ship reportedly used equipment, including a "magic pipe," to bypass pollution prevention systems designed to separate and monitor oil levels in its wastewater. US investigators initiated their probe following a report from an engineer who witnessed an illegal dumping off the coast of England in August 2013. The engineer resigned upon the ship's arrival in Southampton. The ports implicated in the charges are located in Florida, Maine, Massachusetts, New Jersey, New York, Rhode Island, South Carolina, Texas, and Virginia, as well as the U.S. Virgin Islands and Puerto Rico. Jeremy Smart, head of enforcement at the Maritime and Coastguard Agency, emphasized that this case sends a strong message to the industry that such pollution practices will not be tolerated globally. Princess Cruise Lines is a subsidiary of Carnival Corporation, the world's largest cruise company. The US Justice Department stated that the fine represents the largest-ever criminal penalty imposed for deliberate vessel pollution.
Triton's latest submersible, recently tested in the Bahamas, can currently reach a depth of 1km (0.6 miles). This prototype is designed to eventually reach the bottom of the Mariana Trench, a 11km (7-mile) descent. The centerpiece of the design is a 15cm (6in) thick glass sphere, manufactured by Rayotek Scientific in San Diego. This sphere will protect the three-person crew from the intense pressure of the deep sea while providing them with a panoramic view during their journey. Triton's goal is commercial: they plan to sell tickets for this unique experience. Bruce Jones, the company's CEO, believes that adventurous individuals would pay $250,000 for the opportunity to be among the few to visit the deepest point in the ocean, just as thousands climb Mount Everest and many are booking trips to near-Earth orbit. The company anticipates having the submersible operational within the next two years.

A groundbreaking review conducted by Queen's University and the NSPCC concluded that Northern Ireland's child protection system is effective. Dr. John Devaney, who led the research, stated that the system is robust. The report, titled "Translating Learning into Action," will be released on Thursday. Researchers analyzed 24 cases involving children who died or were seriously injured. Dr. Devaney emphasized that in the majority of cases, the deaths were unpredicted. However, he suggested improvements such as providing families with earlier support and ensuring professionals have the resources to remain involved for extended periods to sustain positive changes. The report also noted an increase in the number of children on protection registers compared to five years ago, attributing this to the enhanced protection system. Key findings of the report include: * The rate of non-accidental child deaths in Northern Ireland is declining due to a strong child protection system. * Most of the 24 case reviews praised the dedication and professionalism of staff working with the families involved. * The children in these reports were among many in similar circumstances known to professionals, and the reviews concluded that it was unlikely to have identified them as being at higher risk. * Services should intervene earlier with families before issues become entrenched and harder to address. * Services need to remain involved with some families for longer to ensure that parenting improvements are sustained. The report's authors recommended providing therapeutic services to children to address the psychological impact of poor parenting. They also suggested increasing opportunities for professionals to collaborate on assessments and interventions. Finally, they urged senior managers to ensure manageable workloads for professionals based on their experience.
Irish Water has announced that it will take until Thursday to restore water supply to 50,000 homes and businesses affected by a burst water main. The burst occurred on Friday morning. Irish Water stated that storage reservoir levels are critically low due to the repair work taking longer than expected. The age and type of the water main, along with its complexity, have contributed to the delay. The water main, which supplies the Staleen Water Treatment Plant, is located over four meters underground. Irish Water explained that the 50-year-old pipe has warped and is one of the highest pressure pipes, making the repair more challenging. A specialized solution is currently being designed, with normal water supply expected to resume on Thursday. The Staleen Water Treatment Plant provides drinking water to Drogheda and surrounding areas, as well as parts of south Louth and east Meath. To manage the remaining water resources, Irish Water re-implemented water rationing for certain parts of Drogheda at 8:00 AM local time on Monday. Louth County Council and Irish Water have been supplementing the water supply by using tankers to transport treated water from neighboring areas. Water tankers and containers have been deployed from across the Republic of Ireland and Northern Ireland Water. This water is being directly delivered into the water network to increase the available water for a rotating water roster system, which rations water to different areas in a planned manner. This system aims to ensure that all locations have access to water for specific periods. The roster of affected locations will be posted on the Louth County Council and Irish Water websites and will be regularly updated. Temporary water stations have been set up in various locations across east Meath and Louth. Consumers are advised to bring clean containers and boil water collected from these stations as a precautionary measure. NI Water has stated that it will continue to collaborate with Irish Water and provide support until the incident is resolved.

Archaeologists uncovered human remains at St Mary's Primary School in Leith after a skeleton was found during construction work for a new classroom. Experts believe the outlines of at least nine coffins found on the school grounds indicate a rich archaeological history in Leith. Richard Lewis, City of Edinburgh Council's culture convener, stated that the site's significance is greater than initially thought. He noted the school community's interest in the excavation and the children's eagerness to learn about the discoveries. John Lawson, City of Edinburgh Council archaeologist, explained that the excavations have revealed what appears to be a complex cemetery dating from the 15th to 17th centuries, containing at least nine graves, including adults and young children buried in coffins. While the presence of burials in this location was a possibility, the discovery was still a surprise. Lawson added that further excavations will provide more evidence to accurately date the burials and their connection to Leith's history. The area containing the remains has been secured, but the school remains open. Construction of the new classrooms will be delayed, with a completion date now expected at the end of June due to ongoing archaeological work.

Chris Martin, a 19-year-old rugby player for University College Cork, died suddenly two days after Christmas in 2015. He woke up struggling to breathe and passed away while his family sought medical help. His father, Michael Martin, urged those with mild asthma to manage their condition properly, expressing his shock that asthma could be fatal. Despite this, the Asthma Society of Ireland states that asthma causes an average of one death per week in the Republic of Ireland, dispelling the misconception that only severe cases are dangerous. Michael Martin is participating in the society's awareness campaign, which runs on Irish TV and social media. Chris was diagnosed with asthma at age four but had never been hospitalized. He was a very active teenager involved in sports like surfing and mountaineering. While he carried his reliever inhaler and used it during rugby, he often forgot his preventative medication. He was unaware that his asthma was not under control and that he was at risk for a serious attack. On December 26th, 2015, Chris went to bed as usual but woke up unable to breathe the next morning. They attempted to get him to a doctor for nebulizer treatment, but he collapsed while getting dressed and died in front of his family. Averil Power, the chief executive of the Asthma Society of Ireland, shared that Michael's story is not unique, citing other young people who died from asthma in the past 18 months, aged between seven and 21. Most were healthy and active, unaware of their risk. Power emphasized that anyone with asthma, regardless of severity, can be at risk, citing a 2014 UK study that found the majority of those who died had mild or moderate asthma and that underuse of preventer inhalers was a major factor. She advises patients to take their preventative medication as prescribed, even when feeling well, and to inform their GP of any problems. Power concludes that with proper care and treatment, most asthma deaths can be prevented.

In an election with 213,506 valid votes (41.6%), second preference votes are only counted if no candidate receives over 50% of the initial vote. In this case, the top two candidates receive the second preference votes from eliminated opponents. North Wales Police serves the entirety of the region generally considered North Wales, encompassing Anglesey, Gwynedd, Conwy, Denbighshire, Flintshire, and Wrexham counties. This area has a population of 675,000 and is largely rural, including Snowdonia National Park, home to Wales' highest peak, Snowdon. The major A55 road connects the area to cities like Manchester, Liverpool, Birmingham, and the port of Holyhead on Anglesey, which offers ferry services to Ireland. Mark Polin has served as the current chief constable since 2009. The force comprises 1,454 officers, 888 staff, and 232 community support officers. The 2016/17 budget for the force was £141 million. The force aims to save £4.54 million between 2017/18 and 2019/20. The police precept for Band D properties is £240.12, the highest in Wales compared to £220.06 in Gwent, £207.85 in South Wales, and £200.07 in Dyfed-Powys Police areas. During the year up to September 2015, there were 36,800 recorded crimes in North Wales, a slight 1% increase from the previous year, the lowest increase in Wales for that period. While incidents of violence against the person rose by 17%, all but one police force experienced similar increases. Sexual offenses increased by 11%, criminal damage and arson by 3%, while robbery fell by 12% and burglary dropped by 10%. A recent report by Her Majesty's Inspectorate of Constabulary (HMIC) found the force performed well in preventing crime and anti-social behavior and tackling serious and organized crime. However, the report also noted that other aspects of the force's effectiveness required improvement, particularly in its approach to investigating crime. HMIC identified shortcomings in the force's policy for allocating crimes to investigators, which was not clearly understood by staff. Examples

Tricia Marwick, speaking at the David Hume Institute, advocated for a fundamental review of committee operations. She proposed electing committee conveners through a vote by their peers. Currently, the Scottish Parliament has 17 committees with 132 positions distributed among 80 MSPs. Marwick suggests reducing this to 10 or 11 committees. She believes a change in culture is insufficient and structural reform is necessary. She questioned the effectiveness of the current committee structure, pointing to the lack of committee-initiated legislation since 2002 (excluding procedural matters) and the limited post-legislative scrutiny. Marwick argued that larger committees could better align with policy priorities by consolidating related subjects. These larger committees, focused on key parliamentary concerns, could be divided into smaller subgroups for more flexible discussions. She emphasized the need for the Scottish Parliament to clearly define its policy priorities and structure committees accordingly. Larger committees, she suggested, could operate more efficiently by allowing members to focus on specific tasks rather than all committee business simultaneously. Marwick also supports electing committee conveners as part of a cultural shift within Holyrood. While parties would retain proportional representation of conveners, these individuals would be directly accountable to the parliament through a secret ballot of all MSPs. This, she believes, would empower conveners to have a stronger voice, independent of any government's legislative agenda.

Rachel Treweek, aged 52 and currently Archdeacon of Hackney in the Diocese of London, will become the Bishop of Gloucester. While two women bishops have been appointed previously, Archdeacon Treweek is the first woman to lead a diocese, holding a position one rank below archbishop. She succeeds The Right Reverend Michael Perham, who retired last year. Representatives and clergy from churches throughout Gloucestershire are anticipated to attend the new bishop's welcome ceremony. She is expected to participate in a procession to the cathedral before her consecration by the Archbishop of Canterbury and her inaugural sermon. It is believed she will also be the first female bishop to enter the House of Lords. Archdeacon Treweek expressed her "immense joy and privilege" upon her appointment in March. The Church formally approved legislation permitting women bishops last November.
Security and foreign policy experts, in an open letter, state that the agreement falls short of the administration's standard for a "good" agreement. They advocate for fewer concessions regarding international nuclear inspections and research and development activities. This letter's publication coincides with the approaching deadline for a final accord. US Secretary of State John Kerry and Iranian Foreign Minister Mohammad Javad Zarif are scheduled to travel to Vienna on Friday to participate in the negotiations, which may extend beyond June 30th. The P5+1 (the US, UK, France, China, Russia, and Germany) aims to restrict Iran's sensitive nuclear activities to prevent rapid nuclear weapon development. Iran, seeking sanctions relief, maintains that its nuclear program is peaceful. The open letter, released by the Washington Institute for Near East Policy on Thursday, is signed by a bipartisan group of US diplomats, legislators, policymakers, and experts who have regularly discussed Iran for the past three years. The letter asserts that "most of us would have preferred a stronger agreement" and acknowledges that the agreement "will not prevent Iran from having a nuclear weapons capability" or require the dismantling of Iran's nuclear infrastructure. However, it contends that the agreement will reduce this infrastructure for the next 10 to 15 years and establish a transparency, inspection, and consequence regime to deter Iran from building a nuclear weapon. The letter urges President Obama to insist on Iran providing information about nuclear weapons research suspected by Western powers and granting the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) access to military sites. It also recommends establishing strict limits on the research and development of centrifuges used to enrich uranium, preventing a "rapid technical upgrade and expansion" of Iran's enrichment capacity after the deal's expiration. Enriched uranium can be used for both nuclear energy and weapons. A senior Obama administration official confirmed receipt of the letter and stated that it "largely aligns with the US negotiating position within the negotiating room".
William Matchett, a former RUC Special Branch officer with experience in policing Iraq and Afghanistan, authored a book about Special Branch during the Northern Ireland Troubles. He believes the type of terrorism the UK faces now differs significantly from that of the Troubles. Matchett notes that the current threat is characterized by swift, dynamic attacks involving vehicles, knives, and a deliberate attempt to kill as many people as possible. In contrast, attacks during the Troubles were more isolated and focused on a single target. Matchett argues that the need to quickly stop these attacks necessitates the arming of all police officers, including those on neighborhood patrols and plainclothes officers. This view comes after a terror attack in London that left seven dead and 48 injured. Three attackers were shot by armed police after driving into pedestrians and stabbing people. An off-duty policeman critically injured while tackling an attacker, and a British Transport Police officer, who faced the attackers unarmed, was seriously injured but is now stable. While all police officers in Northern Ireland are armed on duty and carry personal protection weapons off-duty, Metropolitan Police Commissioner Cressida Dick opposes routine arming of all UK police officers. She believes it would be uncomfortable for both the public and officers, and that it doesn't necessarily provide increased protection. Dick cites examples from France and other countries where armed officers have still been vulnerable to attacks. Instead, she advocates for specialized units trained to handle complex terrorist threats.
Yukiya Amano, the IAEA director-general, stated that Iran provided environmental samples from the Parchin military site, which he visited on Sunday. However, Amano emphasized that substantial work remains before the investigation can be finalized by December's deadline. Western nations suspect that nuclear weapons research occurred at Parchin, but Iran denies these allegations and maintains its nuclear program is purely peaceful. The completion of the IAEA's investigation is crucial for implementing the comprehensive agreement reached in July between six world powers and Iran. This agreement involves Iran limiting its sensitive nuclear activities in exchange for sanctions relief. The Parchin complex, located south of Tehran, focuses on ammunition, rocket, and explosives research, development, and production. Concerns about its potential role in Iran's nuclear program arose in 2004 when reports indicated the construction of a large explosives containment vessel for hydrodynamic experiments. The IAEA considers these activities strong indicators of potential nuclear weapon development. Despite inspectors not observing any unusual activities during a 2005 visit and environmental samples not revealing nuclear material, suspicions persisted. Iran repeatedly denied the IAEA's requests to revisit Parchin until Sunday, during which extensive landscaping, demolition, and new construction were observed. Amano, accompanied by Tero Varjoranta, head of the IAEA's Department of Safeguards, toured Parchin on Sunday. At a Monday press conference in Vienna, Amano disclosed they inspected a building previously only observed through satellite imagery. While no equipment was found inside, evidence of recent renovations was noted. Amano also announced that environmental samples were collected from "places of interest" at Parchin last week, with Iran participating in the sampling process. While he didn't specify if IAEA inspectors were present during the sampling, Amano stressed that the process did not compromise verification procedures. He assured that the agency could confirm both the integrity of the sampling process and the authenticity of the samples, stating they were collected under IAEA responsibility and monitoring. The collection of samples at Parchin by Iranian experts is believed to be part of a confidential "road map" agreement signed by the IAEA and Iran in July.
A trial concerning the 111 killings at a jail has been divided into four phases, one for each floor where the deaths occurred. The current phase focuses on eight inmate killings, but defense attorneys claim it's impossible to determine who fired the fatal shots. Previously, 48 policemen were convicted in the two preceding phases. This latest phase, which began on Monday with a new jury, is anticipated to last about a week. The final phase is scheduled to commence on March 17th. In August of the previous year, 25 police officers received lengthy sentences for their involvement in the massacre. Each received 624 years for the deaths of 52 inmates, although legally, they cannot serve more than 30 years in prison. An additional 23 officers were convicted in April. The defense can only appeal the verdicts after the entire trial concludes. Fifteen more police officers await trial. The killings occurred on October 2nd, 1992, when riot police responded to a prison riot. The riot originated from an argument between two inmates within one of South America's largest prisons at the time, housing nearly 10,000 inmates. It escalated into a battle between rival gangs. Carandiru prison was closed in 2002, following coordinated uprisings in 27 Sao Paulo state prisons where thousands of visitors were held hostage. In 2001, Colonel Ubiratan Guimaraes, who led the police operation to regain control of Carandiru, was convicted of using excessive force. However, he was acquitted on appeal in 2006.
News Group Newspapers (NGN), which has resolved many lawsuits related to its former publication, the News of the World, has consistently denied any phone hacking at its sister tabloid, The Sun. However, on Thursday, Mr. Justice Mann in London permitted claims against The Sun to proceed. NGN, now known as News UK, has settled over 1,000 phone hacking claims. The judge ruled that four claimants can modify their cases to include The Sun for the first time in the legal action against NGN. Two of these cases are among those scheduled for a trial in July. Chris Hutchings, a lead solicitor for the claimants alleging their private voicemail messages were hacked, stated: "Today's ruling brings The Sun directly into the civil litigation against News Group." He added, "The court has now allowed claimants, for the first time, to include claims of phone hacking by the newspaper." Hutchings also noted that the decision will impact up to 50 additional cases about to be filed against NGN and will broaden the documentation the company must disclose.
A YouGov poll for ITV Cymru Wales and Cardiff University, the first Welsh poll since Theresa May became Prime Minister, suggests 29% support for the Conservatives (up 6% from July). Labour is at 35% (+1%), Plaid Cymru 13% (-3%), UKIP 14% (-2%), and the Lib Dems 7% (-1%). Analyst Professor Roger Scully from Cardiff University's Wales Governance Centre said the results reflect May's "honeymoon period" as a new party leader. Projecting the poll uniformly across current Westminster constituency boundaries, Scully said the only change would be Labour losing Ynys Mon to Plaid Cymru. This would give Labour 24 out of 40 Welsh seats, the Conservatives 11, Plaid Cymru four, and the Liberal Democrats one. However, a proposed new Parliamentary map by the Boundary Commission would reduce the number of Welsh MPs to 29. Under those arrangements, Scully's projections show Labour narrowly winning the majority of Welsh seats with 15 MPs, the Conservatives 10, Plaid Cymru three, and the Liberal Democrats one. The poll's findings for assembly election voting follow a similar pattern. Scully's projections indicate Labour losing three seats, giving it 26 AMs, while the Conservatives gain three seats to become the largest opposition party in Cardiff Bay with 14 AMs. The projections give the other parties the same number of AMs as they achieved in May's assembly election - Plaid 12, UKIP seven, and the Liberal Democrats one. Scully said the "significant lift" in Tory fortunes suggested by the poll "confirms that the broad picture indicated in recent Britain-wide polls applies in full to Wales as well". He added, "It is not at all unusual for new prime ministers, or leaders of major parties to enjoy something of a honeymoon period". Scully cited the "Corbyn Bounce" after Jeremy Corbyn's leadership victory in September 2015, which was short-lived, disappearing by December. The poll, for ITV Cymru Wales and Cardiff University's Wales Governance Centre, surveyed 1,001 adults online by YouGov from 18-21 September 2016.
While playing the Miss Kitty game at a casino, Pauline McKee won 185 credits, but a message displayed that she had also won a bonus of $41,797,550.16. The casino refused to pay, claiming it was a computer error. Despite suing the casino, McKee's case was ultimately dismissed by the Iowa Supreme Court. She expressed disappointment, stating that she had hoped to use the money to help her children financially. The casino argued that its on-screen rules stated that malfunctions void all payouts and plays. The Iowa Racing and Gaming Commission (IRGC) investigated the machine, sending its hardware and software to an independent lab. The investigation found that the software was programmed to allow a maximum bonus of $10,000 but couldn't determine how the multi-million dollar bonus message appeared. Aristocrat Technologies, the machine's manufacturer, acknowledged this type of display error and suggested that component degradation could increase the likelihood of this rare occurrence. They had informed casinos about the glitch in 2010 and recommended disabling the bonus feature as a precaution, which the casino in question had not done. The IRGC concluded that the bonus display was invalid and that the slot machine malfunctioned. McKee's lawsuit, filed in 2012, alleged breach of contract and consumer fraud. The court ruled in favor of the casino in 2013, but the case continued as McKee's lawyers challenged the IRGC's jurisdiction and questioned whether the machine truly malfunctioned and if a contract existed between McKee and the casino. Similar cases involving erroneous bonus displays have occurred in the US. In 2009, a Mississippi court dismissed a claim for a $1 million bonus that appeared on screen, citing game rules that limited payouts to $8,000. McKee's case was finally rejected by the Iowa Supreme Court on April 24th.
Gordon McLeod, a 32-year-old heroin addict, was sentenced to jail after admitting to assaulting two elderly men in Edinburgh. McLeod, posing as an East Lothian council worker, tried to gain entry to Ronald Cormack's home by claiming there was a water emergency. He wore a high visibility vest and displayed a fake ID card before pushing past Mr. Cormack. Inside, McLeod struggled with Mr. Cormack and brandished a knife. Mr. Cormack and his friend, Jack Cairns, then apprehended the intruder, pushing him towards the door while calling the police. Judge Lady Scott commended the pensioners for their bravery and stated that McLeod's plan was to rob Mr. Cormack. She noted that McLeod had a history of offenses, including assault and robbery in 2006, for which he received a 42-month sentence. McLeod's defense counsel expressed remorse for his actions, acknowledging that forcing his way into the house was unforgivable.
A recent analysis by Alan Milburn and the Social Mobility and Child Poverty Commission highlights the issue of unequal educational opportunities for children. This analysis aligns with concerns raised by Ofsted and government policy favoring the academy model for school improvement. However, evidence suggests a more complex picture, as schools have converted to academies under varying circumstances. Similar to Foundation Trust hospitals introduced by Milburn during his tenure as Health Secretary, academies operate with a degree of autonomy within the public sector. However, a key concern raised in the report is the declining starting salaries for teachers compared to other graduate professions in the UK and similar developed economies. The midpoint starting salary for top graduate jobs is currently £30,000, while teachers in London earn £27,800 and around £5,000 less elsewhere. The Commission urges an increase in teacher salaries, coinciding with the government's advisory body on teacher pay reviewing submissions. Teaching unions are demanding pay rises exceeding the 1% limit set by George Osborne's spending review, citing difficulties in recruiting and retaining quality teachers, particularly in high-need areas. While the government is unlikely to budge on pay, it may face growing dissent within the teaching profession. A program to recruit 1,500 elite teachers for underperforming schools is underway, but many believe their effectiveness will depend on deployment in clusters. Ministers are emphasizing the long-term benefits of the teaching profession, including job satisfaction and future pay prospects. International research suggests that teachers with increased responsibilities often achieve reasonable long-term salaries compared to other countries. Despite this, the pay review body may reiterate its previous warning about the increasing difficulty in recruiting enough teachers to meet rising pupil numbers. It is unlikely to endorse the unions' request for an exception to public pay restraint for teachers.
A Leicester University study revealed a declining trend in stillbirth and neonatal death rates across the UK. Scotland led the way with a rate of 4.72 per 1,000 live births, compared to 4.3 in Nordic countries (Norway, Sweden, Denmark, Finland, and Iceland), often considered the benchmark for neonatal survival. The UK-wide rate for stillbirths and deaths within 28 days of birth dropped to 5.61 per 1,000 live births in 2015, down from 6.04 in 2013. Scotland's rate also decreased from 5.43 to 4.72, the lowest among UK nations. Mary Ross-Davie, director of the Royal College of Midwives Scotland, attributed the progress to initiatives focusing on prenatal smoking cessation, fetal movement monitoring, consistent fetal growth measurement by midwives and obstetricians, and prompt intervention when issues arise. She highlighted Scotland's early focus on this issue, establishing a national stillbirth group in 2013 and the Maternity Care Quality Improvement Collaborative. Ross-Davie acknowledged the impact of deprivation on stillbirth and neonatal death rates and emphasized the need for continued efforts to reduce these numbers in Scotland. She also noted the importance of addressing societal inequalities, drawing inspiration from Scandinavia's more equitable society with lower deprivation levels. Prof Elizabeth Draper, the report's author, stated that stillbirth rates across the UK fell by 8%. While similar in Scotland, the starting point was lower, resulting in a lower current rate compared to the rest of the UK. The ultimate goal is to significantly reduce stillbirths across all four UK nations to reach the levels seen in Nordic countries.
Hackers compromised several high-profile Twitter accounts, including Amnesty International and BBC North America, posting messages in Turkish that referenced "Nazi Germany" and "Nazi Holland." The tweets, which included the Turkish flag, mirrored comments made by Turkish President Recep Tayyip Erdogan criticizing German and Dutch officials. Twitter identified the source of the attack as a third-party app and removed its permissions. The company declined to provide further details. Forbes, government agencies, and celebrities were also targeted. Some hacked accounts included the message "see you on 16 April," potentially alluding to the Turkish referendum scheduled for that date. The Twitter hack coincided with escalating tensions between Turkey and Germany and the Netherlands, which had banned rallies by Turkish ministers ahead of the referendum.
A man in his 20s is hospitalized in Multan after suffering burns over 50% of his body. His family states that the man and the woman involved were in a relationship, but he did not want to marry her. The woman claims she acted in self-defense after being attacked. While hundreds of acid attacks are reported in Pakistan annually, it is uncommon for men to be the victims. The incident in Multan occurred early Tuesday morning. Local journalists report that the man was seen leaving the woman's house, screaming and covered in acid. A police official, speaking to AFP, stated that the man had visited the woman as usual and she threw acid on him after he again refused to marry her. The man was able to turn, so the acid burned his back but not his face, the official added. Pakistani broadcaster Dunya TV says women are victims in about 80% of acid attacks in the country. Experts note that acid is readily available in South Asia, and many victims are attacked after rejecting suitors or due to family disputes.

She commended the emergency services' response to the attack, which killed 22 people on May 22nd. However, she noted that Greater Manchester has lost up to 2,000 officers since 2010. Located along the Great Silk Road, Armenia has been influenced by numerous cultures and empires. After gaining independence from the Soviet Union in 1991, Armenia became embroiled in a violent conflict with Azerbaijan over the predominantly Armenian-speaking region of Nagorno-Karabakh. One of the earliest Christian civilizations, Armenia's first churches were established in the fourth century. Over subsequent centuries, it experienced periods of rule by the Byzantine, Persian, Mongol, and Turkish empires, as well as times of independence. With a population of 3.1 million and an area of 29,743 square kilometers, the major languages spoken in Armenia are Armenian and Russian, and the primary religion is Christianity. Life expectancy in Armenia is 71 years for men and 77 years for women. The country's main exports include diamonds, machinery, and foodstuffs. Serge Sarkisian, who became president in 2008 after winning a second term in early 2013, has a background in the military and defense. He served as a Soviet soldier, worked in the defense committee of the self-proclaimed Nagorno-Karabakh Republic, and held positions as Armenia's minister of defense, minister of national security, and head of the presidential staff. In 2009, Sarkisian signed a historic agreement to re-establish diplomatic relations with Turkey, but the rapprochement was short-lived and the agreement was suspended due to pressure from nationalists on both sides. Television is the dominant medium in Armenia, with around 25 private stations operating alongside two public networks. Major Russian networks are widely accessible. Armenians rely less on newspapers for news, as print runs are typically small and most publications are owned by wealthy individuals or political parties. While a media law prohibits censorship, libel and defamation can result in imprisonment, and journalists have been sentenced under relevant laws. Some key events in Armenian history include: the Armenian genocide of 1915-1917, where an estimated 600,000 to 1.5 million Armenians were killed or deported from Anatolia; the emergence of independent Armenia in 1918 following the defeat of the Ottoman Empire in World War I; Armenia's incorporation into the Soviet Union in 1922; the outbreak of the Nagorno-Karabakh conflict in 1989; Armenia's secession from the Soviet Union in 1991; the 2
Nias, a male Malayan tapir, was born at Port Lympne Reserve near Hythe to parents Malacca and Hunter. Malayan tapirs are critically endangered with only an estimated 1,500 to 2,000 individuals remaining. Adrian Harland, the reserve director, expressed excitement about Nias' birth, highlighting its significance for the species and the dedicated keepers. Found in the forests and rainforests of south-east Asia, Malayan tapirs are threatened by hunting, illegal logging, and deforestation. The Malayan tapir, also known as the Asian tapir, is the largest of the four tapir species. It is related to both horses and rhinoceroses and is an "odd-toed" ungulate, meaning it has four toes on its front feet and three on its back feet, each ending in a hoof. Gestation for Malayan tapirs typically lasts around 13 months. Calves are born with white spots and stripes on black skin, which fade as they mature, providing camouflage in their moonlit forest habitat. Nias is the eleventh Malayan tapir born at Port Lympne Reserve since 1989, establishing its reputation as a leading European breeder of this species.
Following acid attacks in London last month, which targeted bike and moped delivery riders, over 70 Deliveroo riders have expressed safety concerns, declining deliveries. In response, Deliveroo is implementing new safety measures, including an app feature for riders to report security concerns at specific delivery addresses. These reports will be shared with company headquarters and local authorities. The company is also trialing helmet cameras in Hackney, east London, a hotspot for rider incidents, to gather evidence and assist police investigations. Additionally, Deliveroo is hiring 50 new staff dedicated to rider safety. Last month, two riders, one from Deliveroo and one from UberEats, were among five people injured within 90 minutes in north London when moped riders threw acid at them. Deliveroo drivers, as independent contractors, are not eligible for sick pay if attacked and use their own vehicles for deliveries. Jabed Hussein, an UberEats rider and the first victim in the series of attacks, described the experience as "like getting fire on my face." He expressed concerns about the safety of the job and reported that riders are now ceasing work earlier due to safety fears. Deliveroo managing director Dan Warne emphasized the company's commitment to rider safety and their responsibility to enhance safety measures.
The body of 20-year-old India Chipchase was discovered on January 31st at a house on Stanley Road in Northampton. Edward Tenniswood, 52, from the same address, denies murdering her. During the trial at Birmingham Crown Court, prosecutor Christopher Donnellan QC stated that Miss Chipchase's body was found on a mattress at the defendant's home. Donnellan said Tenniswood approached Miss Chipchase at NB's cocktail bar in Northampton early on January 30th while she was intoxicated. Witnesses believed Tenniswood knew her and overheard him assuring her he would get her home safely, which he did not. Donnellan argued that Miss Chipchase's death was not an accident, claiming Tenniswood raped and strangled her at his house. He stated that Miss Chipchase resisted, leading Tenniswood to grip her throat and squeeze. She sustained blunt force trauma to her head and face, indicating a struggle. After her death, Tenniswood attempted to clean up the scene, re-clothing Miss Chipchase, covering her with a sheet, and removing her belongings. Donnellan pointed out that Tenniswood wore clear plastic vinyl gloves, which were later found in a bag of rubbish with Miss Chipchase's blood on the outside and Tenniswood's DNA on the inside. He suggested Tenniswood's motive was likely sexual, and that when she resisted, he became determined to have sex with her, leading to her death. The court heard that Tenniswood checked into the Ibis Hotel in Mare Fare at approximately 9:30 PM GMT on January 30th. Miss Chipchase's body was discovered in his home around 3:30 PM the following day, and he was arrested three hours later. Police observed a scratch on Tenniswood's neck and other marks on his body upon his arrest. The trial is ongoing.
